Jerusalem Talmud Terumot



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Five [persons] cannot give heave and if they gave it is not heave: the deaf and dumb<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">He is excluded only if he has no way of communicating with others.</i>, the insane, the minor, and one who gives heave from what is not his. The Gentile who gave heave for a Jew’s property, even with the latter’s authorization his heave is not heave<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">But a Gentile may voluntarily donate heave from his own produce.</i>.<br>A deaf person who speaks but cannot hear should not give heave, but if he did, his heave is heave. A “deaf person” mentioned anywhere by the Sages is a deaf-mute.<br>Rebbi Jehudah says, the heave given by a minor who has not yet grown two pubic hairs is heave. Rebbi Yose says, before he came to the time of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Niddah</i> 5:6: “The vows of a girl 11 years and one day of age are investigated (one inquires whether she understands the notion and severity of vows; if she does, her vows are valid.) If she is 12 years and one day old, her vows are <i>prima facie</i> valid but one investigates them during all of her thirteenth year. The vows of a boy 12 years and one day old are investigated; if he is 13 years and one day old, his vows are <i>prima facie</i> valid but one investigates them during all of his fourteenth year. Before that time, even if they say we know before Whom we made the vow and to Whom we dedicated a sacrifice, their vows are not vows and their dedications not dedications.” According to Rashi, “the time of vows” is when the vows are investigated; according to Maimonides [in his Code (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.3.2-6"><i>Nedarim</i> 11:3</a>) and in the first and third versions of his Mishnah Commentary] it is the time when all vows are automatically valid. {Rashi’s opinion and the similar one of Maimonides in the second version of his Commentary are the only ones compatible with the Yerushalmi; Maimonides’s other opinion can be justified by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 46b-47a" href="/Niddah.46b-47a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 46b–47a</a>.}</i>, his heave is not heave. After he has reached the time of vows, his heave is heave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Five [persons] cannot give heave,” etc. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman understood all of these from here (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:2-3" href="/Exodus.25.2-3"><i>Ex</i>. 25:2–3</a>)<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">While the verses deal with the voluntary heave for the Tabernacle, it is assumed that the word “heave” always has the same meaning (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.20"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 1, Note 70</a>) and, therefore, is under the same restrictions.</i>: “Speak to the Children of Israel that <i>they</i> should give heave for Me”, excluding the Gentile, “from every man”, excluding the minor, “whose intention is to volunteer”, excluding the deaf-mute and the insane. “The following is the heave you shall accept from <i>them</i>”, except from one who gives heave for what is not his.
Should not their<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Speaking of deaf-mute, insane, and minor.</i> action be proof of their intentions<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">One should allow non-verbal communication of “intention to volunteer”.</i>? As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.6.1">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 6:1</a>: “If somebody brings his produce up to his roof because of worms and dew descended on it, it is not under the category of ‘when given’ but if he intended this {that the produce should be wetted by dew}, it is under the category of ‘when given’. If a deaf-mute, insane, or minor person brought them up, …”. As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 141</a>, produce is not susceptible to impurity unless it came into contact with water (or any other fluid causing impurity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 136</a>) and that contact was desired, since the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:38" href="/Leviticus.11.38"><i>Lev</i>. 11:38</a>: “If produce got wetted by water … it will be impure” can also be read as: “If water was given on produce …”. It is inferred that the wetting, even if happening by a passive process, must have an active ingredient, <i>viz</i>., that the moistening of the produce must be agreeable to the owner.</i>: “If a deaf-mute, insane, or minor person brought them up, they are not under the category of ‘when given’ because they have action but no intention.” What is their action? Rebbi Ḥuna said, when he grabs it while full of dew<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 13a" href="/Chullin.13a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 13a</a>, R. Joḥanan explains that he turns the produce over in order to distribute the moisture evenly. This is professional action. R. Simson conjectures that the action envisaged by R. Ḥuna is the same as explained by R. Joḥanan.</i>. We also have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 3:8" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.3.8">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 3:8</a>. It is explained in that Chapter that water drawn intentionally will make produce susceptible to impurity even if the contact of the produce with it was unintentional. The example described in Mishnah 8 is that of cattle whose feet have to be washed, either because the animal was used for threshing and now is all dusty, or because of some medical condition. Then the water drops clinging to the animal after it was washed in the river will make produce susceptible to impurity unless the animal was driven to the river by “a deaf-mute, insane, or minor person.” Here again, if the cattle are not only driven to the river but actively washed, intention is clearly shown.</i>: “If a deaf-mute, insane, or minor person brought them down, even if he thought that [his animals’] feet should be doused, it is not under the category of ‘when given’ because they have action but no intention.” What is their action? Rebbi Ḥuna said, when he rubs them with water. We should also say here, let their action be proof of their intentions! Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Abbahu, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan; Rebbi Zeïra in the name of the rabbis (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>): “Your heave will be credited to you.” When thought is mentioned in the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The root both of “thought”, מחשבה, and “being accounted for”, נחשב, is חשב.</i>, his action cannot prove his intentions; when thought is not mentioned in the verse, his action can prove his intentions. Since here thought is mentioned in the verse, his action cannot prove his intentions.
Rebbi Yose said, I questioned before Samuel: But with bills of divorce, thought is not written and his action cannot prove his intention! As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.5.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:5</a>.</i>: “Everybody is permitted to write a bill of divorce, even an insane person, a deaf-mute, and a minor.” Rebbi Huna said, if a sane person directs him. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, is that (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut</i>. 24:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:3" href="/Deuteronomy.24.3">3</a>): “he writes for her”, for her personally<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This is an echo of the very short discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.5.2"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:5</a>. Rebbi Joḥanan points out that the divorce document must be written specifically for the woman in question. If two men with identical names are married to two women who have the same name, and both want to divorce them on the same day, the two divorce documents will have identical wording. Nevertheless, if the husbands switch the documents before they are handed to the respective wives, both divorces will be invalid. In both Talmudim, Samuel narrows down the meaning of the Mishnah in that he allows the incompetent only to write the formulaic portion under the direction of a competent person; but the data which make the document valid, date and names of the two persons involved, must be written by a competent person as agent of the husband. The argument here does not consider this restriction, except that R. Yose (Rav Assi of the first generation Amoraïm in Babylonia) is of the opinion that, since a document containing only date and names cannot be signed by witnesses and is not a divorce document, the writing of the incompetent is necessary for the validity of the document and one has to wonder why this is accepted.</i>? Rebbi Yose came back and said, there one person writes<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Writing a valid divorce document and using it for the divorce are two distinct actions; one may be valid without the other. But taking heave is valid only if the taking is intended for heave; the action itself needs thinking; this parallels the action of divorce, not the writing of part of the document.</i> and another person divorces. But here, the same person thinks and takes heave. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, there, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A competent person. Even if he writes all by himself there are two actions. This argument is valid only for R. Eleazar (ben Shamua, the Tanna) who holds that a divorce is validated by handing over the document before two witnesses. But according to R. Meїr who holds that a document is invalid if it has not been signed by two trustworthy witnesses, the writing of the document is not important, only the signing is, and this must be done by two competent persons unrelated to the husband.</i> would write himself and divorce, would that not be a divorce? But here, he thinks and takes heave.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One disagrees with this Mishnah teacher<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that incompetents may separate heave under the direction of a competent person.</i>. How is that? It turns out that Rebbi Joḥanan concerning heaves parallels Rebbi Ismael, the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa concerning sanctification, and Rebbi Joḥanan concerning divorces parallels the rabbis concerning sanctification, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Parah 5:5" href="/Tosefta_Parah.5.5">Tosephta <i>Parah</i> 5:7</a> reads: “Everybody is able to sanctify except the deaf-mute, the insane, and the minor; but R. Jehudah approves of the minor. R. Ismael, the son of R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa says that the sanctifications of a deaf-mute person, an insane, and a minor, who sanctified in the presence of others, are valid. The sanctification by a sexless person is invalid, for he is possibly uncircumcised, and an uncircumcized person cannot sanctify. The sanctification by a hermaphrodite is valid but R. Jehudah declares it invalid since he possibly is a woman and a woman does not qualify for holy matters.”<br>All this refers to the purification from the impurity of the dead, which needs sprinkling with fresh water to which a minute amount of the ashes of the red heifer must be added. The act of taking the ashes and putting them into a vessel filled with fresh water is called “sanctification.” It is a sacral act (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:17" href="/Numbers.19.17"><i>Num</i>. 19:17</a>) and as such needs the intent of the person executing it. R. Ismael, the son of R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa, applies the principle that for obligations for which “thought” is implied by the verse but not spelled out explicitly, actions may prove intent.<br>{A sexless person is one without testicles or female sex characteristics. He might be a male with undescended testicles. The hermaphrodite has a penis and testicles as well as female breasts and a vagina; he must be circumcised. The exclusion of women by R. Jehudah is rejected by the rabbis; women nevertheless may not sprinkle since the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:18" href="/Leviticus.19.18"><i>Lev</i>. 19:18</a>) requires this to be done by “a pure male.”}<br>The seeming inconsistency in the positions of R. Joḥanan is not resolved. {If one assumes that R. Joḥanan agrees with the position of Samuel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.4">Note 14</a>) there is no inconsistency since he will agree that the incompetent may write only the inconsequential parts of the divorce document.}</i>: “The sanctifications of a deaf-mute person, an insane, and a minor who sanctified are no sanctifications. Rebbi Ismael, the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa says, if they are alone, their sanctifications are no sanctifications. Together with others, their sanctifications are sanctifications.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i> 1:1</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 71a" href="/Gittin.71a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 71a</a>. This and the following paragraphs are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.2-10"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:1</a> (fol. 48c). Cf. also Mishnah and Halakhah 2.</i>: “If a deaf-mute person gave heave, it is not heave. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, when was this said? If he was born deaf-mute. But if he was normal and became deaf and dumb, he writes and others confirm his signature. <sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta (1:2</a>) and the Babli, the formulation is: “If he hears but does not speak, he is mute; if he speaks but cannot hear, he is deaf; both have the status of normal persons.” The sentence is missing in the Rome ms.</i> If he hears but cannot speak, he is like a normal person.”
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One disagrees with this Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>. They say, does not a Mishnah disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 14:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.14.1.2-5">Yebamot 14:1</a>: “If a normal man married a normal woman and she became deaf-mute he may divorce her or keep her, but if she became insane he may not divorce her; if he became deaf-mute or insane he may never divorce.”</i>: “If he became deaf-mute or insane, he may never divorce.” Why can he not write and have others fulfill his written instructions<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rabban Simeon, even if he became deaf-mute it should not make any difference since he may give written instructions.</i>? They upheld it, if he is illiterate. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected, does not a <i>baraita</i> disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 2:8</a>. The text there ends: “it is no divorce unless they hear his voice say to the scribe ‘write!’, and to the witnesses ‘sign!’ ” That Tosephta clearly disagrees with Rabban Simeon and disqualifies handwriting by a mute.</i>: “If he wrote himself, or he told the scribe to write and the witnesses to sign, even though the scribe wrote, the witnesses signed, they gave him [the document] and he in turn gave it to her, it is no divorce.” Rebbi Assi said, complete the sentence and there is no disgreement: “it is no divorce unless they hear his voice.” But even if he gave a sign with his head you say it is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i>7:1</a>: “If somebody lost his speech and they said to him, shall we write a divorce document for your wife? if he gave a sign with his head one checks him out three times; if he indicated ‘yes’ when it was expected, and ‘no’ when it was expected, they should write and hand it over.” The Tosephta also seems to contradict this Mishnah.</i>, here also it is invalid. Rebbi Mana said, it is valid: hearing the voice has the same status as seeing him nod his head<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta contradicts Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel but not the Mishnah. A written authorization is invalid but a direct authorization by the husband to scribe and witnesses, by speech for a normal person and sign language for a mute, is valid. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 71a" href="/Gittin.71a">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 71a</a>), Rav Cahana in the name of Rav accepts the position of Rabban Simeon, against the Tosephta.</i>!
Rebbi Ezra<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rebbi Azariah, a student of R. Mana (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.25"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 1, Note 90</a>).</i> asked before Rebbi Mana: Just as you say that three times he must give a sign with his head, must one equally hear his voice three times? He said to him, hearing his voice once, giving a sign with his head three times.
Rebbi Yudan said, there, if he said I did such and such, here, if he says do such and such<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">“There” refers to the statement of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel that written testimony of a deaf-mute is valid, “here” refers to the Tosephta that a written command by a deaf-mute is invalid for a divorce.</i>. Rebbi Benjamin ben Levi inquired: If he has understanding for the past, he should have understanding for the future; ifhe has no understanding for the past, he would not have understanding for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Yudan makes no sense; R. Benjamin supports R. Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.7">Note 21</a> ff.)</i>! Rebbi Eudaimon said, here we deal with a mute; there is no agency for a mute<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects the statement of Rabban Simeon and implies that the Mishnah in <i>Terumot</i> also applies to any deaf-mute, whether his disability be congenital or acquired. Since the heave of a deaf-mute is no heave even if the deaf-mute acts for a sane person, it follows that a deaf-mute cannot be an agent and, therefore, cannot appoint an agent. A divorce document must be written by an agent of the husband’s since the verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:1" href="/Deuteronomy.24.1"><i>Deut.</i> 24: 1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:3" href="/Deuteronomy.24.3">3</a>) requires that “he writes her a scroll of divorce”. If nobody can be an agent of the deaf-mute, he cannot divorce since even if he himself writes the document he cannot ask the witnesses to sign.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, we deal with a sane person<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts the statement of Rabban Simeon and shows that R. Joḥanan is wrong; there is no disagreement between Rabban Simeon and the Mishnah in <i>Giṭṭin</i>. A congenital deaf-mute cannot marry or divorce, while a hearing person who is mute is considered normal and can divorce by giving written directions, following Rabban Simeon and R. Mana. The only reason we require a triple confirmation of the will to divorce is to ascertain that the husband really wants the divorce, not that he wants to have a document ready should he want to divorce.</i>. Why is there no divorce? Because I say, he was occupied with his documents. We also stated so: “When was this said? When he explained it in his regular state. But if he was paralyzed by sickness, once is enough<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Sick persons have to be treated with consideration and only the necessary legal forms have to be observed, not formalities that are not stricly necessary. This is true certainly for critically ill persons, in particular for a childless husband who wants to spare his wife the trouble of freeing herself from an unwelcome brother-in-law (or maybe forbid her to a desirable brother-in-law.)</i>.”
Who states “the deaf-mute?” Not Rebbi Jehudah, since it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>; the remark about abent-mindedness is missing there.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that the sons of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Gudgada<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the first generation. A Levite who in the Temple was a guardian of the gates. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 3a" href="/Chagigah.3a">Babli (<i>Ḥagigah</i> 3a</a>) it is reported that his grandsons, the sons of his normal daughter, were ostensibly deaf-mutes. They went to the lectures of Rebbi and when Rebbi prayed that they should be healed, it turned out that they had learned the entire Mishnah and halakhic Midrashim from his lectures. R. Joḥanan all his life ate only food prepared by the standard of purity of sacrifices.</i> were all deaf-mutes and all purities<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">“Purities” is food prepared in purity, usually by the standards of purity of heave.</i> in Jerusalem were handled by them. They said to him, because purities do not need thought and may be produced by a deaf-mute, an insane, and a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If they are continually supervised by a normal adult.</i>. But heaves and tithes need thought and do not become invalidated by absent-mindedness.” Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi La: Thought is not written in regard to them, only watching<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the section dealing with impurity of sacred food, it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:9" href="/Leviticus.22.9"><i>Lev</i>. 22:9</a>): “They have to keep My watch.” Pure food remains in its state only if it is permanently guarded against impurity from the moment it is prepared for impurity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.3">Note 7</a>). A properly instructed deaf-mute may act as a guard.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>, discussed also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 3b" href="/Chagigah.3b">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i> 3b</a>.</i> The signs of an insane: One who goes out in the night, stays overnight in a graveyard, tears his clothing, and destroys what one gives to him. Rebbi Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since he precedes Samuel, the author must be the Babylonian Rav Huna, as given in the Babli.</i> said, only if all of that is in him since otherwise I say that one who goes out in the night is a man-dog<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κυ(ν)άνθρωπος (νόσος) “dog-man (malady)”; cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Talmudic Evidence of Greek Spelling</i>, in: Studi Classici in onore di Quintino Cataudella, vol. 4, pp. 71–72, Catania 1972; <i>Notes on the Talmudic Dictionary</i>:<i>Gandropos</i>-<i>Qantropos</i>, Lešōnēnu 35 (1971) 207–210.</i>; he who stays overnight in a graveyard burns incense to spirits, he who tears up his clothing is a choleric person<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χολικός “bilious”.</i>, and he who destroys what one gives to him is a cynic<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κυνικός “dog-like”, a philosopher who has no use for industrial products.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even only one of these is proof. Rebbi Abun said, what Rebbi Joḥanan said, even only one of these is reasonable only for him who destroys what one gives to him; even the greatest idiot does not destroy all one gives to him<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, Rav Papa declares this criterion to be the only one to stand alone, all others needing multiple confirmation following Rav Huna.</i>. The mad dancer<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Greek κόρδαξ, Latin <i>cordax</i>, “mad dance”, perhaps chorea. This and the following sentences refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:1</a> which states that a mad dancer cannot divorce his wife during an attack of his malady.</i> does not exhibit any of these signs. What is a mad dancer? Rebbi Yose said, a decrepit one<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic הִמּ “decrepit, senile person”.</i>. There came a case before Rebbi Yose of a weaver who, when one gave him red on black he was exerting himself<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It is not at all clear what his symptoms were. Since the profession of the sick person is mentioned, it seems that he was unable to fit two different strands of thread together on his loom; perhaps the description is of Huntington’s chorea or Parkinson’s disease.</i>, black on red he was exerting himself. He said, this is the mad dancer described by the Sages.
<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>.</i>“It sometimes he is insane [when he is sometimes healthy he is normal in all regards], when he is insane he is insane in all regards, when healthy he is normal in all regards.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He is a responsible and competent person in all legal respects.</i>” A case came before Samuel who said, when he is of sound mind he should deliver the divorce document<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">It is not enough that he be sane when telling the scribe to write and the witnesses to sign; he must also be sane when he delivers the document. Whether a document can be held over a spell of insanity is a matter of dispute between R. Simeon ben Laqish and R. Joḥanan in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.1.2-10"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:1</a>.</i>; one must investigate<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Giṭṭin</i> is slightly different: “Samuel holds with R. Simeon ben Laqish.”</i> following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish; as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, when he is of even mind; that is as Samuel [acted]. Following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said, only if he is of sound mind he may deliver the divorce document; (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 38:16" href="/Isaiah.38.16"><i>Is</i>. 38:16</a>) “make me healthy and let me live!”
Who stated “a minor”? This does not follow Rebbi Jehudah, as we have stated: “A minor whom his father put in a melon field where he was giving heave and his father sold on that basis, his heave is heave. He did not give heave but his father confirmed his action.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This is a shortened version of a text close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>: “<i>Rebbi Jehudah said</i>, a minor whom his father put in a melon field where he was giving heave and his father sold on that basis, his heave is heave. <i>They said to him</i>, he did not give heave but his father confirmed his action.” “They” are the Sages of our Mishnah.</i>” When the father came and confirmed his action, is heave made retroactively or for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">While produce must be “prepared” for impurity (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.3">Note 9</a>), heave is automatically prepared by its holy status and can become impure even if dry. In addition, consumption of heave by a non-Cohen is a deadly sin once heave is sanctified. The question is when the status of holiness is acquired.</i>? Rebbi Shammai said, let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This also is a shortened <i>baraita</i> close to a <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta, 1:5</a>: “<i>How does one give heave from what is not his</i>?<i>If he went to another person’s field</i>,<i>collected</i>,<i>and gave heave without permission</i>,<i>if he has to worry that it might be robbery</i>,<i>his heave is not heave</i>;<i>if he does not have to worry then his heave is heave</i>.<i>How does he know whether what he did is robbery or not</i>? When the owner came and found him standing on his property, if he said to him, take from these nice [fruits], if they are nice he does not worry about robbery, otherwise he has to worry. But if <i>the owner</i> collected and <i>added to his</i>, in no case does he have to worry about robbery.” The full Tosephta is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 52b" href="/Kiddushin.52b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 52b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 21a" href="/Bava_Metzia.21a"><i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i>21a</a>.</i>: “When the owner came and found him standing on his property, if he said to him, take from these nice [fruits], if they are nice he does not worry about robbery, otherwise he has to worry. But if he collected and gave to him, in no case does he have to worry about robbery.” Can you say it became heave retroactively? No, from now into the future! Here also, it will be made heave from now on into the future<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The minor (or the stranger in the Tosephta) can do the physical work of separating heave from the rest of the produce but only an adult owner can sanctify it, and only for the future.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:4:2-5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.4.2-5.4"><i>Giṭṭin</i>5:4</a> (fol. 47a).</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num</i>. 18:28</a>) “you” excludes partners, “you” excludes guardians, “you” excludes one who gives heave from what is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i>, not found in <i>Sifry Num</i>., must be from the exegesis of R. Aqiba. It appears, in different form, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 52a" href="/Gittin.52a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 52a</a>.</i>. Did we not state (<i>Terumot</i> 3:3): “Partners who gave heave one after the other?<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages in that Mishnah declare heave given by one partner without the knowledge of the other as valid.</i>” But one must be for Great Heave<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Heave given by the farmer; it is ironically called “Great” because it has no biblical minimum.</i>, the other for heave of the tithe. Did we not infer the laws of the Great Heave from heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The verse quoted refers only to the heave of the tithe, the 10% given by the Levites from their tithes.</i>? But one is for practice, the other for action<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">As practice, heave given by guardians is valid; giving tithe by one partner without the knowledge of the other is discouraged.</i>. “You’ excludes guardians; but did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:9:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.9.7">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:4</a>. In the first case, the home owner raises the orphans without being officially appointed guardian, neither by the father nor by the court after the father’s death. Nevertheless, the person caring for the orphans becomes de facto guardian.</i>: “Orphans dependent on a home owner, or for whom the father had appointed a guardian, must tithe their produce.” The colleagues say, here for a permanent guardian, there for a temporary guardian. Rebbi Yose asked, does that apply to: “he may sell movables to feed them but not real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Giṭṭin</i>: “He may sell slaves to feed them but not real estate.” All other sources give every guardian the right to sell real estate, under the supervision of the court, for the benefit of his wards.</i>?” But here one deals with an adult orphan<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are able to give heave for themselves, they have to do it (if they are competent.)</i>, there with an underage orphan.
“ ‘You’ excludes one who gives heave from what is not his.” What is the interpretation, one who gives heave from what is not his own, or one who gives heave from what is somebody else’s? Let us hear the following: If he declared his heap as ownerless and then smoothed it and took possession again, if you refer to one who gives heave from what is not his own, his heave is not heave, if you refer to one who gives heave from what is somebody else’s, his heave is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 1:6 states that tithes and heave are due after threshing, when the grain kernels have been assembled into a heap and the heap was smoothed. It is shown in <i>Ma‘serot</i>, Halakhah 1:1, that property which was ownerless at the moment when heave was due is exempt from heave. This seems to contradict the statement here that if a person declared his grain ownerless before it was smoothed, then gave heave, and only after that again took possession, his heave might be heave. The entire argument is possible only for R. Meïr, since R. Yose holds that property can be abandoned only if another person takes it up (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.1.6"><i>Peah</i> 6:1, Note 17</a>). It follows that any heave given in this case is given only because by rabbinic decree this kind of pro forma abandoning was declared invalid; hence, there is a rabbinic obligation of heave. As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 44b" href="/Nedarim.44b">Babli notes (<i>Nedarim</i> 44b</a>), any heave given from the repossessed grain must refer to the heap itself; it cannot possibly be given for unabandoned grain which is obligated by biblical decree (if we assume that heave is a biblical obligation after the Babylonian exile, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.7.1.1"><i>Ševi‘it</i> Chapter 7, Notes 11</a> ff.).</i>. Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumah</i> 6:4. Stealing sacred property obligates the thief to pay back the amount taken plus a fine of one fifth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:16" href="/Leviticus.5.16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:16</a>). The fifth is computed from above (amount stolen = 4/5 amount paid), which is one fourth from below (amount paid = 5/4 amount stolen). Halakhah 6:4 explains that one fifth has to be given to the Temple, restitution of Temple property, and another fifth to a Cohen, as restitution of heave.</i>: “If he stole Temple heave and ate it, he pays two fifths and the principal because there is no double payment for Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Double restitution is required only for theft from natural persons: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>) “He shall pay double to his neighbor.”</i>.” Who gave the heave if not the administrator? Is he not giving heave from what is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">If this argument were valid, no corporation would ever be able to be engaged in agriculture in the Holy Land since it never could tithe.</i>? And you say his heave is heave! The reason must be that he does not give heave from another person’s property. Or should we say, who gave the heave if not a Levite who dedicated his heave? But did not Rebbi Hoshaia state that there is no difference between him who dedicated his <i>ṭevel</i> and him who dedicated his heave? Rebbi Idi said, the administrator is like the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Any duly authorized person can give heave for any corporation. The question asked at the start is not answered since we did not find a case in which it would make any difference.</i>. This does not follow Rebbi Yose, since Rebbi Yose said the administrator is like any other person<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This opinion is not supported by any other source in Talmudic literature.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is from <i>Demay</i> 6:1, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:3-22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.3-22">Notes 7–10</a>.</i> Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num</i>. 18:28</a>): “You, also you,” to include your agent. Just as you are in the Covenant, so your agent must be in the Covenant. You appoint an agent, the Gentile may not. Rebbi Assi wanted to say that the Gentile cannot appoint another Gentile as agent, but he can appoint a Jew. Rebbi Zeïra said, from the <i>baraita</i> itself: You appoint an agent: does that not mean Jews? Similarly, the Gentile cannot appoint an agent: not even a Jew. Rav Hoshaia objects: Does the <i>baraita</i> support Rebbi Joḥanan? “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said: If the Gentile does not wish to give heave from his produce, he cannot give heave.” Therefore, if he wants to, he may give heave. Rebbi Abba said, if he confirms it after him.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: An Israel who said to a Levite: “You have a <i>kor</i> of tithe in my hand”, and this Levite went<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Without first acquiring the tithe; cf. <i>Ševi‘it</i> Chap. 8, Notes 13,14.</i> and made it heave of the tithe for other produce but the Israel went and gave it to another Levite, [the first one] has only a complaint on him<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">If the Levite ate his other tithe based on the heave of the tithe he thought he had given, he committed an inadvertent sin but has no legal way to ask for damages from the Israel.</i>. That is, if he handed it over<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He actually handed it over to the second Levite. In that case, the tithe was in the possession of a third party and the first Levite was tithing what was not his. The problem is that without an acquisition, the first Levite still seems to be tithing produce over which he has no power and this seems to be forbidden by our Mishnah.</i> before [the first Levite] made it [heave]. But not if this one made it before that one handed it over. Parallel to this Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3:4. The Mishnah declares that one may appoint an agent for the purpose of giving heave and then proceeds to explain what happens if the agency is terminated by the owner but not in the presence of the appointed agent.</i>: “If he terminated: if he terminated before he gave heave, his heave is not heave. But if he terminated after he gave heave, his heave is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Once heave is designated it is irrevocably holy (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.13">Note 57</a>).</i>.” All this follows: From the moment he said, you have a <i>kor</i> of tithe in my hand, he made him his agent and it turns out that he was tithing with the permission of the Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Since the power of an agent cannot be greater than that of the principal and since according to the majority opinion, the only thing the Israel owner may do is separate the tithe of the heave but not declare it as heave of the tithe, the Levite cannot act as an agent but must act as independent principal with the right to give heave; the statements are parallel but the Amoraic statement is not a consequence of the Mishnah.</i>. One may also explain it following Rebbi Yose, as “Rebbi Yose said: If the owner gave heave from the tithes, what he did is done.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 1:9" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).1.9">Tosephta 1:9</a>. According to Rebbi Yose, the first Levite becomes the agent of the Israel.</i>”
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: An Israel who told a Levite: “You have a <i>kor</i> of tithe in my hand and here is its price<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Tithe of which the heave of the tithe has been separated is totally profane in the hand of the Levite and may be sold by him. If the farmer separated the heave of the tithe, he may contract with the Levite to buy the tithe back from him.</i>;” the latter must worry about the heave of the tithe contained in it<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Before he can use the money, he must make sure that the tithe would have been profane in his hand. Therefore, he either must ascertain that the farmer separated heave of the tithe or he must give it himself from other tithe that is not yet profane before he can agree to the deal. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30b" href="/Gittin.30b">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 30b</a>, discusses both versions and finds them in need of emendations.<br>The Rome ms. has a completely different text: דְּמֵיי כּוֹר מַעֲשֵׂר יֵשׁ לָךְ בְּיָדִי וְהֵא לָךְ אֶת דָּמָיו אֵינוֹ חוֹשֵׁשׁ לִתְרוּמַת מַעֲשֵׂר שֶׁבּוֹ. “The value of a <i>kor</i> of tithe you have in my hand and here is its price, he does not have to worry about the heave of the tithe contained in it.” According to this version, R. Joḥanan extends his ruling in the case of R. Simeon ben Abba to all Jews who are tithing.</i>. For example, Elopisa<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The exact name has not been determined. Levy proposes Εὔελπις, Kohout Ἐλπίας, Krauss Ἀλοπίς. The Rome ms. reads the name as ליפסה.</i> gave tithes to Rebbi Simeon bar Abba and said, it is in order. He went and asked Rebbi Joḥanan who said to him, our brother Elopisa is trustworthy. Rebbi Issa<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">He probably is Issi bar Hini, a Babylonian who studied with R. Joḥanan and then returned to Babylonia where he founded his own Yeshivah.</i> asked before Rebbi Joḥanan, is that following Rebbi Yose, for “Rebbi Yose said: The owner who gave heave from the tithes, what he did is done.” He said to him, Babylonian<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בבליה for בכליה. Rebbi Joḥanan was known to address his Babylonian students as “Babylonian”.</i>, you understood it<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Joḥanan, this is practice to be followed. The same conclusion is reached in the Babli; there the statement is attributed not to the late Tanna R. Yose ben Ḥalaphta but to Abba Eleazar ben Gamla (or, ben Gamliel) whose title, Abba, points to the Temple period or the first generation of Jabneh.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> Who needs this? Rebbi Yose! Which [statement of] Rebbi Yose? What we had stated there: “He who did read the <i>Shema‘</i> and did not make his ear hear did fulfill his obligation.” For whom do we need that? For Rebbi Yose! For which statement of Rebbi Yose? For that which we have stated: “He who recites the <i>Shema‘</i> and does not make his ear hear has fulfilled his obligation, Rebbi Yose says that he has not fulfilled his obligation.” Rav Mattanah said: That formulation is Rebbi Yose’s. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> said: We could be of the opinion that the rabbis and Rebbi Yose disagree only about <i>Shema‘</i> since there it is written: “hear!”, but not about any other obligations. Since Rav Mattanah said “that formulation is Rebbi Yose’s”, it is the same for all commandments of the Torah. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:26" href="/Exodus.15.26"><i>Ex</i>. 15:26</a>) “Bend your ear to His commandments and keep all His basic laws”; your ears should hear what your mouth says.
Rav Ḥisda said: The deaf-mute person is not mentioned; it is a formula. In which Mishnah? Rebbi Yose said: It is reasonable to think that Rav Ḥisda concedes that the statement from <i>Terumot</i> is from Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Yose bar Rebbi Abun said: It must be said that that one is Rebbi Yose’s since we have stated the first five cases and did not include it<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The case of the deaf person who is able to speak is not included in Mishnah 1, detailing those whose heave is invalid, nor in Mishnah 6 in the list of people who should not give heave but whose heave is heave. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 2, Notes 162–166</a>.</i>, because their <i>terumah</i> is not <i>terumah</i>. Then we have stated the five later ones and did not include it. So in the end you must say, that one is by Rebbi Yose.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:1:1-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.1.1-20">Mishnah <i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:1</a>. The “appearance” is the assembly of all of Israel at the feast of Tabernacles during the Sabbatical year (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:10-13" href="/Deuteronomy.31.10-13"><i>Deut</i>. 31:10–13</a>); one derives from this the rules of appearance at all festivals of pilgrimage.</i>, we have stated: “Everybody is obligated for appearance except the deaf-mute, the insane, and the minor.” The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Eleazar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:12" href="/Deuteronomy.31.12"><i>Deut</i>. 31:12</a>): “So they should hear and learn<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The spelling is not masoretical.</i>.” So far one who speaks but cannot hear; what about one who hears but cannot speak<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">He should be obligated.</i>? Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Eleazar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:12" href="/Deuteronomy.31.12"><i>Deut</i>. 31:12</a>): “So they should learn,” so they should teach<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The first conjugation verb could also be vocalized as a causative. The same argument is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 3a" href="/Chagigah.3a">Babli (<i>Ḥagigah</i> 3a</a>) where it is noted that the second clause, “and learn”, would be superfluous if one excluded only the deaf but not the mute.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, this means that the principles of Rebbi are no principles<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In the language of the Babli: One does not make inferences from general principles, even when the exceptions are enumerated.</i>, since we have stated: “A <i>ḥereš</i> who speaks but cannot hear should not give heave,” and we thought that one who hears but does not speak is <i>ḥereš</i>, one who speaks but does not hear is not<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the parallel in <i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:1 (fol. 75d).</i> <i>ḥereš</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is not mentioned in the Mishnah.</i>. But we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:4:1-5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.4.1-5.2">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 12:5</a>, speaking of the ceremony of <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, the taking off of one shoe, which frees the widow of a childless man from having to marry her brother-in-law (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4-10" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4-10"><i>Deut</i>. 25:4–10</a>). The formalized statements by widow and brother-in-law before the court as described in the verses are an integral part of the proceedings.</i>: “The <i>ḥereš</i> whose shoe was taken off, the female <i>ḥereš</i> who took off the shoe, and she who takes off the shoe of a minor, all performed invalid ceremonies.” And Rebbi Joḥanan said, because they cannot say (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:8" href="/Deuteronomy.25.8"><i>Deut</i>. 25:8</a>): “he shall say”, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:7" href="/Deuteronomy.25.7"><i>Deut</i>. 25:7</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:9" href="/Deuteronomy.25.9">9</a>) “she shall say.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the person who is deaf but not mute can perform a valid procedure. This is also the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 104b" href="/Yevamot.104b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 104b</a>.</i>” We also have stated: “A “deaf person” mentioned anywhere by the Sages is a deaf-mute<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts the formulation of the Mishnah in <i>Yebamot</i>.</i>.” This supports Rebbi Jonah, for Rebbi Jonah said, this means that the principles of Rebbi are no principles.
<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">This and the next paragraph are also in <i>Yebamot</i> 13:2 (fol. 13 c/d).</i> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meїr: His heave is never heave unless he grew two pubic hairs<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">As a sign of puberty. Growing pubic hair is the accepted standard of the onset of puberty, the end of childhood, and the start of responsibility to keep all religious obligations (<i>Bar Miẓwah</i>). In all matters of biblical commandments, this standard cannot be relaxed.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana in the name of the rabbis (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>): “Your heave will be credited to you.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The root both of “thought”, מחשבה, and “being accounted for”, נחשב, is חשב.</i>” Anybody for whom “thought” is written may give heave. But anybody for whom “thought” is not written cannot give heave. They objected: But “thought” is not written for a Gentile, and he may give heave<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Gentile is not bound by the laws of the Torah (except for the commandments given to Noah), no verse requiring intent can apply to a Gentile. On the other hand, Mishnah 3:9 states that voluntary heave given by a Gentile is heave and subject to all its laws and requirements.</i>! Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rebbi Yudan.</i> in the name of Rebbi La (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:32" href="/Numbers.18.32"><i>Num</i>. 18:32</a>): “You should not carry guilt because of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The verse refers to the heave of the tithe. The Levites will be free of guilt if they give heave of the tithe before eating their part of the tithe. The rule established here is then also transferred to the Great Heave.</i>” He who may carry guilt can give heave, he who cannot carry guilt cannot give heave. They objected: But a Gentile cannot incur guilt<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">His heave is purely voluntary; even if he gives heave but mishandles it, he will not incur guilt. This shows that the second argument cannot be true; in this respect, the laws of the Great Heave cannot be derived from those of the heave of the tithe since no Gentile possibly can give heave of the tithe. One is stuck with the first explanation.</i>, and he may give heave! And did not Rebbi Hoshaia state that Gentiles have no “thought”? That is for preparation, here we talk about heave<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Gentile cannot become impure in biblical law, he cannot make anything impure in biblical law, and his intentions are irrelevant and inactive in preparing food for impurity, cf. Notes 7,9. But since heave must be declared, we must hold that the Gentile may validly declare some of his produce to be heave. This proves that the first objection is invalid: Even though the intentions of the Gentile are never required by biblical law, his voluntary intentions are accepted for heave.</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rav Cahana: Following the opinion that someone who cannot give heave cannot dedicate to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph deals with the minor who is able to give heave but has not yet grown two pubic hairs, for R. Yose if he is able to make vows and for R. Jehudah even if he is younger.</i>. Why did he not say: following the opinion that he can give heave, he can dedicate to the Temple? Because of Rebbi Jehudah, for Rebbi Jehudah says he can give heave but cannot dedicate<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The statement regarding heave is in the Mishnah. The statement about sacrifices must have been in a <i>baraita</i> not otherwise transmitted. Since a voluntary sacrifice must be dedicated by a vow, even R. Jehudah must agree that a minor who may not make a vow cannot dedicate a sacrifice.</i>. But Rebbi Joḥanan says, even following the opinion that he cannot give heave, he can dedicate<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to heave, where the exclusion of minors is based on a verse mentioning “every man” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.2">Note 3</a>), sacrifices are attributed to “a human” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:2" href="/Leviticus.1.2"><i>Lev.</i> 1:2</a>), including a minor.</i>. What can he dedicate? Holocaust and well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">These are voluntary offerings that also may be brought by Gentiles. Therefore, persons not under the obligations of biblical commandments may dedicate these sacrifices. {The use of the translation “well-being offering” for שלמים does not imply that this is the correct meaning of the word.}</i>. He cannot bring a sin sacrifice for fat because he is not obligated to a sin sacrifice for fat. He cannot bring a sin sacrifice for blood because he is not obligated to a sin sacrifice for blood<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since a minor is not obligated under the law, he cannot sin and, therefore, can never bring a sin offering. It is forbidden to eat the blood of any animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:10-14" href="/Leviticus.17.10-14"><i>Lev</i>. 17:10–14</a>); transgression of the prohibition is a sin which heaven will punish with extermination if intentional but which, if committed unintentionally, may be atoned for by a sin sacrifice (or, even if intentional, by repentance and the Day of Atonement). “Sacrifice for blood” is a catchword for “sacrifice to atone for a sin punishable by extermination.” It is also forbidden to eat those lumps of fat of cattle, sheep, or goats which would be burned on the altar if the animal were a sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23"><i>Lev</i>. 7:23</a>). Punishment for this offense, if documented by two witnesses, is whipping. Therefore, “sacrifice for fat” is a catchword for “sacrifice to atone for an unintentional simple sin.”</i>. May he bring a sacrifice relating to gonorrhea and skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">These are obligatory sacrifices of purification when the condition is healed, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14-15" href="/Leviticus.15.14-15"><i>Lev</i>. 15:14–15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32">14:1–32</a>. Without these sacrifices, the afflicted person cannot touch any dedicated food.</i>? Since it is obligatory can’t he bring it<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">A minor cannot be obligated for anything. Since the courtyard of the Temple is reserved for sacrifices, it is forbidden to bring profane animals into it. Therefore, the priests should be required to refuse entry to such a sacrifice when brought by a minor.</i>, or since he becomes impure by these can he bring it<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">If he has parents, the parents will bring the sacrifice for him to make sure the entire family can partake of the family offerings. Here we speak about a minor who is an orphan.</i>? If it is obvious for you that he brings, can he become an agent for these<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">To present somebody else’s sacrifice for the purification of that person.</i>? Since he may become impure by these, may he become an agent, or since he cannot become an agent for anything else, can’t he become an agent for these? Rebbi Yudan objected: His produce is <i>ṭevel</i> from the Torah but he cannot free his <i>ṭevel</i> by Torah law<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">If the orphan minor owns agricultural land, the harvest before taking heave will become <i>ṭevel</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.5.7"><i>Peah</i>, Chapter 1, Note 303</a>) and be forbidden for consumption. The prohibition is removed by giving heave. R. Meїr, quoted above, will prevent the minor from putting his own <i>ṭevel</i> in order himself.</i>! So here, even though he may become impure by them, he cannot become an agent. May he bring First Fruits following Rebbi Jehudah who says thatFirst Fruits have the status of territorial consecrated things<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">First Fruits are an obligation of the farmer (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:1-11" href="/Deuteronomy.26.1-11"><i>Deut</i>. 26:1–11</a>). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.1.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:1</a> it is stated that First Fruits must be eaten by Cohanim under the rules of heave. In Mishnah 3:10, R. Jehudah states that after presentation in the Temple, First Fruits may be given to any Cohen but the rabbis require that they be eaten by the priests serving in the Temple at the moment of presentation. This means that for R. Jehudah, the status of First Fruits is that of heave which may be eaten in the entire territory of the Holy Land (called “territorial consecrated things”) but for the rabbis they are consecrated to the Temple.</i>? He may not bring following the rabbis who say they have the status of things consecrated to the Temple. May he bring a sacrifice of pilgrimage<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">A pilgrimage to the Temple at one of the festivals of pilgrimage carries with it the obligation of two sacrifices, the sacrifice of appearance, (ראיון, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:16" href="/Deuteronomy.16.16"><i>Deut</i>. 16:16</a>) which is a holocaust, and a family sacrifice, (חגיגה, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:11" href="/Deuteronomy.16.11">Deut. 16:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:14" href="/Deuteronomy.16.14">14</a>; 12:18) which is a well-being offering.</i>? Since it is obligatory can’t he bring it, or since he may change its name to well-being offering, may he bring it? May he bring a Passover sacrifice? Since it is obligatory can’t he bring it, or since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said in the name of Rebbi Yudan the Prince, a person may bring a Passover sacrifice any day of the year when he changes its name to well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody selected an animal as Passover sacrifice but then it disappeared before the holiday and another animal was dedicated, if the originally dedicated animal reappears it is brought as a well-being offering any time after the holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 70b" href="/Pesachim.70b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 70b</a>).</i>, may he bring it? May he bring tithes of animals<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32-33" href="/Leviticus.27.32-33"><i>Lev</i>. 27:32–33</a>. Every tenth newborn in a herd of cattle or flock of sheep and goats has to be offered as a sacrifice. Tithes of produce are mentioned there in verses 30,31.</i>? If Rebbi Meїr is of the opinion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num</i>. 18:28</a>): “From all your tithes,” that all tithes were bracketed together<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. 105, this is derived from another verse as anonymous statement.</i>, then since he cannot bring tithes of grain he cannot bring tithes of animals. May he make substitutions<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:10</a>, it is stated that one may not substitute a different animal for an unblemished animal already designated as sacrifice. The act of substitution is not invalid but sinful and causes both animals to become sacrifices. Since a minor cannot sin, it is questionable whether his act of substitution is valid.</i>? If Rebbi Meїr is of the opinion that all tithes were bracketed together, then since he cannot bring tithes of grain he cannot bring tithes of animals; since he cannot bring tithes of animals he cannot make substitutions<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of substitution is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:33" href="/Leviticus.27.33"><i>Lev</i>. 27:33</a>, speaking of the tithe of animals. Since the tithe of animals is dedicated as sacrifice, the mention of the prohibition seems to be superfluous; it is taken to indicate that the rules of validity of tithes determine the rules of validity of substitutions.</i>. One may hold with Rebbi Simeon, since Rebbi Simeon says that tithes of animals teach you for all sacrifices about substitutions. That means, since he cannot bring tithes of grain he cannot bring tithes of animals; since he cannot bring tithes of animals he cannot make substitutions; since he cannot make substitutions for these he cannot make substitutions for any sacrifices. Is one guilty sacrificing for him outside the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">It is sinful to sacrifice an animal outside of the Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:23" href="/Deuteronomy.12.23"><i>Deut</i>. 12:23</a>). If the minor’s dedication of an animal as sacrifice is valid in biblical law, it is sinful to slaughter the animal outside the Temple. But if the dedication is valid only by rabbinic practice, the animal becomes dedicated only by its acceptance by the priests in the Temple and slaughter outside the Temple is not sinful.</i>? Cahana said, one cannot be guilty sacrificing for him outside the Temple. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish say, one is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement is quoted, in slightly changed form, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 46b" href="/Niddah.46b">Babli <i>Niddah</i> 46b</a>. In contrast to the Yerushalmi, the Babli states that RR. Joḥanan and Simeon ben Laqish declare the slaughterer guilty only by rabbinic practice.</i>. The statement of Cahana disagrees since Rebbi Jehudah frees his <i>ṭevel</i> as biblical law<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>ṭevel</i> comes into being by agricultural activity, not by the minor’s choice, if R. Jehudah permits the minor to give heave, he lets him remove a biblical prohibition and, therefore, gives the minor’s actions validity in biblical law.</i>. We can say he [Cahana] is following him who says they accepted tithes voluntarily<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.1.4"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:1 (Note 11</a>). Since R. Eleazar is mentioned only if R. Joḥanan disagrees, R. Joḥanan here holds that heave today is a biblical commandment, following R. Yose ben Ḥanina [<i>loc</i>. <i>cit</i>. Note 8; <i>Seder</i> <i>Olam</i> Chapter 30, cf. in the author’s edition (Northvale, N.J., 1998), pp. 257–259, x–xi.]</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not give olives as heave for oil nor grapes for wine. If one did, the House of Shammai say they contain their own heave<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Heave was given only for olives but not for oil. For the House of Hillel, both olives and oil remain <i>ṭevel</i>.</i>, but the House of Hillel say his heave is not heave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There, we stated: “One does not give heave from finished for unfinished,” and here you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Heave was given only for olives but not for oil. For the House of Hillel, both olives and oil remain <i>ṭevel</i>.</i>? Rebbi Illa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because of robbing the tribe. What means, because of robbing the tribe? Rebbi Ḥanania said, because of the exertion<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen must himself turn the olives into oil, the grapes into wine, but the verse says that heave is given as (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>) “grain from the threshing floor and complete produce from the (oil or wine) press.”</i>. Think about it, if there was a grain of rice<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">This speaks about grain (mentioned in the verse but not in the Mishnah). One does not give heave from finished produce for unfinished; the rice kernel will not be peeled by threshing the wheat. Hence, even if grain is given as heave after threshing, the Cohen might have some additional work to do. The same argument applies to the next question. Since grain is mentioned, Maimonides (<i>Terumot</i> 5:18) decides that cut grain cannot be given as heave for threshed grain. The parallel in <i>Sifry</i> (<i>Num</i>. 121) mentions only grain and wine.</i>? People willingly will pound something. Think about it, if it were in ears? So far goes our question. Rebbi Ḥanina, Rebbi Mana did not say so but he wanted to give heave proportional to the oil and gave only proportional to olives<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The decree of the House of Hillel is not biblical, not based on the verse, but rabbinic. While heave has no fixed percentage in biblical law, there are rabbinic guidelines on how much to give (Mishnah 4:3). Since olive oil is so much more valuable than raw olives, if the grower gives the required volume of oil as volume of olives, he will have given too little.</i>. Think about it, if he gave proportional to the oil? Now he did so, another time he will not do so<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">We have to explain the reason of the Mishnah to the uneducated; therefore, the explanation is in Aramaic.</i>. In addition, his neighbor will see him and say, this one wants to give a lot but I do not want to give a lot.
Ḥizqiah said, they mentioned only olives for oil and grapes for wine, hence not for any other produce. Rebbi Joḥanan said, not only olives for oil and grapes for wine but also any other produce<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Ḥizqiah cannot mean that only oil and wine are covered by Mishnah 4, everything else by Mishnah 10, since grain was added to the list both by the Yerushalmi and the <i>Sifry</i>. Therefore, one must conclude that here starts the discussion of the condition under which the House of Shammai accepts that at least the fruits themselves have been tithed. According to R. Joḥanan, any produce undergoing a manufacturing process, such as date honey and date beer from dates, is covered by Mishnah 4 for the House of Shammay (explanation of R. J. I. Kanievski).</i>. The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the producer would immediately start converting the olives or grapes into oil and wine and hand the Cohen the finished product, it still is no heave for the House of Hillel (Explanation of R. H. Kanievski).</i>.
Rebbi Ḥananiah, Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The argument of the House of Shammai is that he is like one who says: This shall be heave for itself and what is below it<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Since the House of Shammai accept that heave given for olives and oil is heave for the olives, they must accept that if one gives heave for two physically separate batches of olives, it must be valid.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah wanted to say, of its own kind<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is valid for one kind, for example olives. But if somebody has olives and grapes, he cannot give heave simultaneously for two batches of each.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said to him, do not accept that. When he said “this shall be<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">He really has to say: “This shall be heave.” At that moment, the batch from which he took the olives becomes freed from the obligation of heave and the next Mishnah will state that heave may be given only from <i>ṭevel</i>, not from profane produce.</i>”, what is in his hand was freed and the rest becomes profane, may profane free <i>ṭevel</i>? Rebbi Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">A fifth generation Galilean Amora, quoted only in the Yerushalmi.</i> said, it is simultaneous. When he said “this shall be”, what is in his hand was freed together with the rest. Rebbi Ḥanina said, that is convincing for the Great Heave where one must give heave from what is earmarked<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 1:9</a>. Since heave is given by estimate, it must be given from a well-defined lot. But heave of the tithe is exactly ten percent; it may be given anywhere if the volume, weight, or number of the produce to be tithed has been determined beforehand.</i>. But heave of the tithe must be defined by measure, weight, or count.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2:2.</i>, we have stated: “One may not give heave from impure for pure produce.” It was stated in the name of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:19" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.19">Tosephta 3:19</a>.</i>: “If he gave heave from impure for pure produce, what he did is done.” What does Rebbi Yose say here? If there, where all is lost for the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Impure heave must be destroyed, except for impure olive oil which may be used as fuel.</i>, he says that what he did is done, here, where not all is lost for the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The decree of the House of Hillel is not biblical, not based on the verse, but rabbinic. While heave has no fixed percentage in biblical law, there are rabbinic guidelines on how much to give (Mishnah 4:3). Since olive oil is so much more valuable than raw olives, if the grower gives the required volume of oil as volume of olives, he will have given too little.</i>, so much more? It was found stated in the name of Rebbi Yose: “One may not give olives as heave for oil or grapes for wine. If one did, the House of Shammai say it contains their own heave, but the House of Hillel say his heave is not heave.” Rebbi Yose contradicts himself. There he says, what he did is done; here he says, his heave is not heave. There, impurity is infrequent<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">For dry produce, even the produce of the vulgar will not be prepared for impurity. For wine and oil, the product in the hand of the vulgar is impure and the question does not arise; in the hand of the fellow it will be shielded from impurity (cf. <i>Demay</i>, Introduction).</i> and people will not err to say that one may give heave from the impure on the pure produce. But olives for oil and grapes for wine is frequent; if you say so, one will say that one may give heave of olives for oil and grapes for wine.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not give heave from gleanings, abandoned sheaves, <i>peah</i>, and ownerless property<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Tractate <i>Peah</i>, all these are exempt from heave and tithes. All examples of this Mishnah are amplifications of the statement that one may not give heave “from what is obligated on what is free from obligation, nor from what is free on what is obligated”.</i>; not from First Tithe of which its heave was taken<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">While First Tithe is normally taken from produce for which heave was already given, since it is under the obligation of heave of the tithe it still may be used to give the Great Heave as long as heave of the tithe was not given.<br>According to R. Simson, the Mishnah cannot speak of regular tithe which is totally profane after heave of the tithe is taken but must speak of First Tithe taken before threshing, when there was no obligation of Great Heave, and which is only under the obligation of heave of the tithe and becomes profane without Great Heave (<i>Ḥallah</i>, Halakhah 1:4).<br>Maimonides, in the later versions of his Commentary, reads שלא ניטלה. This text is also confirmed by the Kaufmann and Cambridge mss. of the Mishnah. Since the Halakhah does not discuss the text, the original reading cannot be ascertained.</i>; not from Second Tithe and dedicated produce<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Second tithe and Temple property have only restricted use; once they have been redeemed they are fully profane and free from obligation.</i> which were redeemed; not from what is obligated on what is free from obligation or from what is free on what is obligated; not from harvested produce for what is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Standing produce is not under any obligation of heave.</i> or from what is standing for what was harvested; not from new produce for old or from old for new<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:22" href="/Deuteronomy.14.22"><i>Deut</i>. 14:22</a>); “You should tithe both tithes of all produce of your land which grows <i>every year</i>,” it follows that one may not give heave and tithes from the produce of one year on that of another (<i>Sifry Deut</i>. 105).</i>; not from produce of the Land for that of foreign countries<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Which are free from any obligation by biblical law.</i> or from foreign countries for produce of the Land. If heave was given, it is not heave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: This is one of three well-explained verses<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The other two verses are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a> (Halakhah 2:4) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:3" href="/Deuteronomy.26.3"><i>Deut</i>. 26:3</a> (Halakhah 8:1). The argument here is repeated in <i>Ma’serot</i> 1:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:3:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.3.2-12"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.2-4"><i>Nedarim</i> 4:10</a>. In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 109, a similar statement is attributed to R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:27" href="/Deuteronomy.14.27">Torah (<i>Deut</i>. 14:27</a>): “The Levite shall come, for he has neither part nor inheritance with you.” You must give him from what you have while he has not. This excludes ownerless property where your and his hands are equal.
Gleanings, forgotten sheaves, <i>peah</i>, and ownerless property all have the same rule. Did one not already state everything<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, only “from what is obligated on what is free from obligation but not from what is free on what is obligated” would be needed; everything else is just illustration. However, it has just been stated that the first part is necessary to show that “gleanings, forgotten sheaves, <i>peah</i>, and ownerless property all have the same rule”. Similarly, one may make a point that the last case, concerning produce of the Land and of foreign countries, is necessary since if we hold that heaves now are only a rabbinical obligation, it is difficult to see what should be the difference between the heave of the Land and that of the surrounding countries where heave is rabbinical all the time. The only questionable part therefore remains that about standing and harvested produce which seems totally superfluous since nobody would ever think that standing produce was subject to heave.
