The answer is that “standing” does not really mean standing, but produce harvested at a time in its development when it is not yet human food and should have remained standing.</i>? Thus you have to understand the Mishnah: [One may] not [give] from produce one third ripe for produce not yet one third ripe, and when he gave heave, it is not heave. For Ḥizqiah it is a point of contention, for Rebbi Joḥanan it is everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement of Ḥizqiah and R. Joḥanan above, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.2.3">Note 142</a>. For one third grown, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 5:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.5.1.6"><i>Ševi‘it</i> Chapter 5, Notes 20–21</a>, for the rules of gleanings etc., cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.2.2"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 6, Note 24</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Five categories of persons should not give heave but if they gave, their heave is heave. The mute, the drunk, the naked person, the blind, and a man who had an emission of semen should not give heave but if they gave, their heave is heave.<br>One gives heave neither by measure, nor by weight, nor by count<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Since no amount is specified for heave in biblical law (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.2"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 1, Note 9</a>), the verse requires only thought in specifying the heave. “To measure” means to determine the volume.</i>. But one may give heave from what was measured, weighed, or counted. One does not give heave in a measuring basket or box, but one may give in them a half or a third<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">If no marks for ½ or ⅓ are on the measuring device.</i>. One should not give half a <i>seah of</i> heave<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 3, Note 164</a>. Half a <i>seah</i>, three <i>qab</i>, is a commercial measure (as dry measure <i>tarqab</i>, as fluid measure <i>hin</i>). Its size can be estimated in a <i>seah</i> vessel.</i> in a <i>seah</i> vessel because half of it is a [standard] measure.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Some of these [rules] are because of the benediction, some because the [persons] are unable to give from the best. The mute, the naked person<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">He may not utter the Divine name, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.5.5"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 3, Note 210</a>.</i>, and the man who had an emission<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.2-16"><i>Berakhot</i> Halakhah 3:4</a> ff.</i> because of the benediction, the blind and the drunk because they cannot give from the best.”<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">A much shortened version of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:1-2" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.1-2">Tosephta 3:1–2</a>.</i>
Abba bar Rav Huna said, one who drank [wine] should not pray but if he did pray, his prayer is a supplication. A drunk should not pray but if he did pray, his prayer is blasphemy. What is one who drank [wine]? Someone who drank a <i>quartarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter of a <i>log</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:5:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.5.10"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 3, Note 227</a>. In the Rome ms: “Even if he drank less than a <i>quartarius</i>.”</i>; a drunk is one who drank more. There<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>, it is said if he cannot speak before a king. Rebbi Zeïra asked before Rebbi Assi: May a drunk recite Grace? He said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:15" href="/Deuteronomy.11.15"><i>Deut</i>. 11:15</a>): “When you will eat and be satiated, then you must praise”, even if you are sleepy. The question is only, may he recite the <i>Shema‘</i>? Abba bar Avin<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">He might be identical with Rebbi Abba bar Avina, a Babylonian of the second generation who immigrated into Galilee and was ordained there.</i> said, a pious person asked Elijah, may he be remembered for the good, may a naked person recite the <i>Shema‘</i>? He said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:15" href="/Deuteronomy.23.15"><i>Deut</i>. 23:15</a>): “He should not see in you a thing of turpitude,” a word of turpitude. Ḥizqiah stated: both<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of Elijah applies in both cases.</i> for reciting [Grace] and reading [the <i>Shema‘</i>].
There<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:6.</i>, we have stated: “One who counts is praiseworthy, he who measures is better, and he who weighs is the best of the three.” And here, you say so? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, here for the Great Heave, there for the heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Which is called “tithe of the tithe”, one tenth of one tenth.</i>. We have stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 13b" href="/Beitzah.13b">Babli <i>Beẓah</i> 13b</a> (“Abba Eleazar ben Gimel”), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30b" href="/Gittin.30b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 30b</a> (“Abba Eleazar ben Gamla”), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 58b" href="/Bekhorot.58b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 58b</a> (“Abba Eleazar ben Gomel”), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 54b" href="/Menachot.54b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 54b</a> (“Abba Eleazar ben Gomel”).</i>: “Eliezer ben Gimel says, from where that one does not give heave by measurement, weight, or count? The verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>): ‘Your heave will be thought of for you’. You give heave by thought, you do not give heave by measurement, weight, or count. Just as this is for the Great Heave, so it is for the heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">The later Sages, Tannaїm and Amoraїm, disagree with this last statement even though it agrees better with the verse.</i>.”
Did we not state: “But one may give heave from what was measured, weighed, or counted”? Rebbi Eleazar said, so is the Mishnah: <sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta 3:4</a>, in the name of R. Jehudah.</i>“A person may measure his <i>ṭevel</i> and bring it into his house on condition that he not give heave by measure. A person may weigh his <i>ṭevel</i> and bring it into his house on condition that he not weigh<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, the sentence reads: “A person may weigh his ṭevel and bring it into his house on condition that he not tithe by weight”. This seems to be the correct reading, adopted by all commentators.</i> by weight. A person may count his <i>ṭevel</i> and bring it into his house on condition that he not give heave by count.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not give oil as heave on crushed olives, nor wine on pressed grapes. If one did, it is heave and he has to give another heave<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Simson and R. Isaac Simponti, the second heave has to be given only from the oil produced from the crushed olives after the first heave was given, and so from the future wine from the already pressed grapes. However, by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta 3:15</a>, in that case the second heave is true heave subject to <i>dema‘</i> and fifth. Therefore, the opinion of Maimonides is preferable, that the second heave has to consist of crushed olives or pressed grapes.</i>. The first one creates <i>dema‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah 3:2.</i> by itself and one has to pay the fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah 6:1.</i> but not the second<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">The second heave is profane food that must be eaten by the Cohen in ritual purity; this is a fine for breaking the rules.</i>.<br>One may give oil as heave for olives being pickled<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">Since for most people, olive oil is more valuable than pickled olives, substitution is permitted. The problem of not completely cured pickles and future raisins will be addressed in the Halakhah.</i> and wine for grapes to be made into raisins. If one gave oil as heave for table olives, or olives for table olives, or wine for table grapes, or grapes for table grapes, when he changed his mind to press them he does not have to give heave<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heave was legitimate when given, it cannot become delegitimized later.</i>.<br>One does not give heave from finished<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah Ma'serot 1:6, detailing the exact moments when finished produce is subject to heave and tithes. Therefore, grapes to be used to make raisins are definitely unfinished produce.</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Grain and wine, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27">Num. 18:27</a>. Since pickled olives and raisins cannot be turned into wine, they are not covered by Mishnah 10. This author will require that raisin wine be made from tithed raisins.</i> produce for unfinished, or from unfinished for finished, or from unfinished for unfinished. If heave was given, it is heave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 10, contradicting our Mishnah here since in Mishnah 10 no obligation is stated to give heave a second time.</i>: “One does not give heave from incompletely processed produce for completely processed produce,” and here you say so? Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because of a “fence” for the purification water, lest purification water become useless<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">“Purification water” is fresh water with some ashes of the red heifer, used to purify people from the impurity of the dead (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>). It is true that heave must be eaten by the Cohen in purity but normally immersion in a ritual bath is enough and sprinkling with purification water is not always necessary. All commentators, from the Gaonim to R. S. Lieberman, explain that “fence for the purification water” simply means that one tries to force the olive or wine grower to produce his oil and wine in ritual purity, for if one would permit giving heave from oil (wine) on oil olives (wine grapes), he either would make only a small amount in purity until he has given heave, or he would buy a small quantity of pure olives (grapes) from a fellow and pay the fellow to give heave in his name from his pure fluids. While this explanation seems pertinent, it does not explain the expression “fence for purification water”; it also contradicts the general tendency, clearly apparent in Tractate <i>Demay</i>, not to force the vulgar to conform to standards of observance he could not honestly sustain. But there seems no better explanation.</i>. What is more serious, robbing the tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The Cohen must himself turn the olives into oil, the grapes into wine, but the verse says that heave is given as (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>) “grain from the threshing floor and complete produce from the (oil or wine) press.”</i> or [breaching] the “fence” for purification water? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>. In the first case, Mishnah 4, his heave was not heave but he has to give heave only once and has no monetary loss.</i>: “[If one gave heave of] olives for olives and in the future he will crush them, grapes for grapes and in the future he will press them, it is heave and he has to give heave a second time.” In this case there is both robbing the tribe and the “fence” for the purification water, but we have stated that it is heave and he has to give heave a second time. This proves that the “fence” for the purification water is more serious than robbing the tribe.
What would Rebbi Yose say here? Since Rebbi Meïr takes “robbing the tribe” lightly but takes the “fence” for purification water seriously, Rebbi Yose who takes “robbing the tribe” seriously, certainly must take the “fence” for purification water seriously! It was found stated in the name of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">A quite different version is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>: “One does not give oil as heave on crushed olives, nor wine on pressed grapes. If one gave, it is heave and he has to give another heave. The first one creates <i>dema‘</i> and is subject to the fifth, but not the second one. From the latter one has to remove the tithes. Rebbi Yose says …” This Tosephta cannot be the source of the discussion here (as asserted by R. Eliahu Wilna and R. Moses Margalit) since the implication for R. Yose would be the opposite of what is asserted here and it is seen in the next paragraph that in the opinion of the Yerushalmi, R. Yose considers the second heave conditional and real heave. A Tosephta text closer to the Yerushalmi is reported in מלאכת שלמה (R. Solomon Adani) on the Mishnah.</i>: “One may not give olives as heave for oil or grapes for wine; if one did, his heave is heave and he does not have to give heave [a second time], the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, the House of Shammai say one gives heave but the House of Hillel say one does not give heave. Everybody agrees that if he gave heave, he does not have to give heave a second time.” There is proof that Rebbi Meïr takes “robbing the tribe” lightly but the “fence” for purification water seriously<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">While Mishnah 4 cannot be R. Meïr’s, it is assumed that Mishnah 8 is.</i>, Rebbi Yose takes “robbing the tribe” seriously<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that originally, the Yerushalmi must have read in R. Yose’s <i>baraita</i>: “he must give heave a second time”, but there is no supporting manuscript evidence. Of all the explanations of this confusing paragraph, the best is <i>Tosefta Ki-fshutah</i> pp. 332–333.</i> but the “fence” for purification water lightly<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This is established in the next paragraph.</i>!
There<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 5:2</a>. The other four examples refer to Mishnaiot in <i>Kilaim</i>,<i>Ḥallah</i>,<i>Miqwaot</i>,<i>Pesaḥim</i>.</i> we have stated: “Rebbi Yose says, in six matters are the House of Shammai lenient and the House of Hillel restrictive: Fowl may be served on the table together with cheese but cannot be eaten together according to the House of Shammai, but the House of Hillel say it may not be served or eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that fowl meat is not meat in the biblical sense since it says: “You may not cook a kid goat in its mother’s milk” and birds have no milk. Nevertheless, as rabbinic “fence”, one may not eat birds’ meat with any milk product.</i>. One gives heave from olives on oil and from grapes on wine according to the House of Shammai, but the House of Hillel say one may not.” Rebbi Mana said, it cannot be “from olives on oil” but “from oil on olives” because this is Rebbi Yose’s! For Rebbi Yose says it is heave and he has to give another heave; that is, if the first [heave] does no longer exist but if the first exists he gives it its name and that is enough<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">If the heave given first can be extended to cover the oil produced from the olives after heave was separated, it follows that R. Yose assumes that olives which were started in purity will be kept in purity until the last drop of oil has been extracted and the heave is legitimate as given from “pure on pure.” It follows that R. Yose rejects any consideration of a “fence for purification water”; he requires a genuine second heave only if either the first one was eaten or if the additional oil was made impure; in the latter case one may not use the first heave following Mishnah 2:1.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers back to the Mishnah, not the <i>baraitot</i> discussed in the meantime. A similar text appears in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>. Since the Great Heave was already taken the first time, “heaves” here must refer to the heave of the tithe.</i>: “The second [heave] should not be eaten until one takes from it heaves and tithes.” What is the status of the first? From what was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In the same Tosephta, though not the origin of this <i>baraita</i>, the full text is: “If one gives heave of olives on olives which will be crushed or from grapes which will be pressed, it is heave and he has to give a second one. The first creates <i>dema‘</i> but not the second. The first is subject to the fifth but not the second; but one has to give it a name. If he came back and made the first olives into oil or the grapes into wine, he does not have to give a second heave.” “To give a name” means to make a declaration that heave or tithes should be valid for such and such produce. Since one really should give heave and tithes from the finished product, if the oil is produced from the olives for which heave (or tithes) were taken, the name may be extended later to the finished oil or wine.</i>: “If he came back and made the first olives into oil or the grapes into wine, he gives it a name and does not have to give a second heave.” That means that he has to give a name to his tithes.
We have stated after this<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 10.</i>: “One does not give heave from finished produce for unfinished;” there it is for the past, therefore not to start<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 10, it is stated: “If one gave heave, it is heave”, which means that it is legitimized after the fact but cannot be accepted if done in defiance of the law. But in Mishnah 9, the language is positive, that everybody is free to give heave.</i>, [and here] even to start? If you say, is this different from when it is finished, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 1:6, detailing the exact moments when finished produce is subject to heave and tithes. Therefore, grapes to be used to make raisins are definitely unfinished produce.</i>: “Dried pomegranate kernels, raisins, and carob from when he made an orderly heap”? Rebbi Yose ben Yose in the name of Rebbi Isaac ben Eleazar: From finished product on unfinished is only forbidden from threshing floor and wine (or oil) press<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Grain and wine, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>. Since pickled olives and raisins cannot be turned into wine, they are not covered by Mishnah 10. This author will require that raisin wine be made from tithed raisins.</i>.
What is the basis of his giving heave<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">While there is no measure of heave indicated in biblical law, Mishnah 5:3 indicates the rabbinical standard of an estimated 2% of the volume for which heave is to be given. Is the basis the volume of oil that could have been extracted if the olives had not been pickled or is it the volume of pitted finished olives?</i>, on basis of their oil or as solid food? Rebbi Yoḥanan said, this was brought from Ḥilfai<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta 3:15</a>, but there Rebbi is reported to require heave proportional to oil, Rabban Simeon proportional to the solid food (“the food exluding the pits”).</i>: “Rebbi said as food, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said on basis of their oil, excluding the pits. Everybody agrees that one gives heave for pickled olives<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, the Vienna ms. has קולפסין, the Erfurt ms. קלופסין , the printed editionsקלפין . The word is usually identified with Greek κολυμβάς, ἥ, Latin <i>colymbas</i>,<i>-adis</i>, f., “pickled olives, olives swimming in brine.”</i> as food but not including their pits. Ḥananiah asked, is this the same for the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">If one moves something from private to public domain or more than four cubits in the public domain (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.4"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 1, Note 188</a>), it is a punishable offense only if a minimal amount is carried. The minimal amount is defined as a <i>quartarius</i> for drinks and the volume of an average dried fig for solid food. The <i>baraita</i> quoted here agrees with the text of the Tosephta, not the Yerushalmi.</i>? It was found stated: “Rebbi said, by a <i>quartarius</i>; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, by the size of a dried fig.”
Rebbi Ḥananiah said in the name of Rebbi Hillel: We have stated in support of the statement of Rebbi Hoshaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>. In the first case, Mishnah 4, his heave was not heave but he has to give heave only once and has no monetary loss.</i>: “[If one gave heave of] olives for olives and in the future he will crush them, grapes for grapes and in the future he will press them, it is heave and he has to give heave a second time.” And we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Since the heave was legitimate when given, it cannot become delegitimized later.</i>: “If one gave oil as heave for table olives, olives for table olives, wine for table grapes, or grapes for table grapes when he changed his mind to press them he does not have to give heave.” Because he changed his mind! Therefore, if he did not change his mind he has to give a second heave.
Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>): “Your heave will be counted as if it were grain from the threshing-floor.” From what He commanded the Levites to give heave, from the finished product<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The verse speaks of heave of the tithe and implies that the merit of heave of the tithe which accrues for the Levite is identical with that of the Israel giving heave from the threshing floor. This implies that the Israel gives only from the threshing floor, when all agricultural work connected with the harvest is complete.</i>. That means it is forbidden to give him ears. Ḥiyya bar Ada in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: If First Tithe was given from grain ears, it is forbidden to eat a snack from it<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">While heave and tithes are due only after cleaning up the threshed grain, using unthreshed ears for a regular meal would show that the ears are the finished product and subject to heave and tithes. But untithed ears may be eaten as a snack. Here, it is stated that giving heave and first tithe in any form declares the product as finished. If the tithe is tithe, then it is <i>ṭevel</i> for heave of the tithe and may not be eaten unless that heave is taken.</i>. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:32" href="/Numbers.18.32"><i>Num</i>. 18:32</a>): “You should not desecrate the holy things of the Children of Israel lest you die.” Will one be whipped for its <i>ṭevel</i> as biblical law? Rebbi Ashian<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the fifth generation, student of R. Jonah, possibly Ἀσίων.</i> in the name of Rebbi Jonah: A Mishnah states that one is not whipped for its <i>ṭevel</i> as biblical law, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:5</a>.</i>: “Heave of the tithe is like First Fruits in two respects and like tithe in two respects. One may give it from pure for impure or not earmarked produce<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 1:9</a>. Since heave is given by estimate, it must be given from a well-defined lot. But heave of the tithe is exactly ten percent; it may be given anywhere if the volume, weight, or number of the produce to be tithed has been determined beforehand.</i>, like First Fruits. It makes forbidden from the threshing floor and has a tariff similar to heave.” When can you say “from the threshing floor”, does this not mean after smoothing<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">The end of threshing, when the threshed grain has been collected and assembled in neat heaps.</i>? That implies that one is not whipped for its <i>ṭevel</i> as biblical law<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">The ears never were threshed, much less smoothed.</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not give heave from pure produce for impure, but if heave was given it is heave. In truth, they said if a cake of dried figs<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Fig cakes are not strings of figs put together when they are completely dry but made of fresh figs formed into cakes and then put out to ferment and dry. The sap of figs is not one of the fluids that make food susceptible to impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.1.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 5, Note 8</a>); if no water touched the cake before it was dry and the individual figs are still recognizable, then if later it is prepared for impurity and one fig was touched by an impure person, this fig will be impure by one degree more than the person who touched and the figs touching this fig will be impure by still one more degree, and so on, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a> (higher degree meaning lesser impurity). Since profane food can acquire only impurity in the first and second degrees, all figs not directly touching the impure fig will remain pure (if the cake is dry).</i> became partially impure one may give heave from its pure part for its impure part, and similarly for a bunch of vegetable or a heap [of grain]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">These are much less tightly compressed than figs in a fig cake.</i>. If there were two cakes, two bundles, two heaps, one pure and one impure, one should not give heave from one for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave can be given only from produce clearly earmarked, one would have to bring the impure cake close to the pure one so they can be enclosed somewhere together and one is afraid that the impure might touch the pure cake and spread the impurity.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, one may give heave from pure produce for impure.<br>One may not give heave from impure produce for pure, but if he gave in error<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment of giving heave he did not realize that the lot to be put in order was impure. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 32a" href="/Pesachim.32a">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 32a</a>) holds the Mishnah to be a biblical law, applicable only if the impure produce had been pure when it came under the obligation of heave. The Yerushalmi agrees that the prohibition has some biblical basis but it assumes the Mishnah to be applicable without restrictions. {Maimonides translates שוגג by Arabic סאהי “inattentive, absent-minded, distracted”; מזיד by מתעמד “deliberate, premeditated, willful; intentional”.}</i>, his heave is heave, if intentionally, he did not do anything. Similarly, a Levite who has <i>ṭevel</i> tithe which he continually uses to give<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">He had tithe of which the heave of the tithe was not given. Since heave of the tithe can be given from one lot on another without restriction, the Levite may use one lot to give heave of the tithe for all other lots, but only after he gave heave for the lot itself.</i>, if he is in error, what he did is done, if intentionally, he did nothing. Rebbi Jehudah says, if he had known of it earlier<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">That the produce was impure or, if tithe, that it was <i>ṭevel</i>. He holds that then his action cannot be unintentional, only negligent.</i>, even if he did it in error, he did not do anything.<br>He who immerses vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Impure vessels that only may be purified by immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> containing at least 40 <i>seah</i> of water. Immersion is a rabbinical prohibition on the Sabbath because it is making an unusable vessel usable, which in the case of visibly unusable vessels would be a biblical prohibition and a deadly sin.</i> on the Sabbath, if unintentionally he may use them, if intentionally he may not use them. He who tithes or cooks on the Sabbath, if in error it may be eaten, if intentionally it may not be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Tithing is a rabbinical, cooking a biblical prohibition. In both cases, the cook may not eat of his food until after the end of the Sabbath.</i>. He who plants<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Also a biblical prohibition. The restriction concerning the Sabbatical year is purely rabbinical, enacted because people tended not to observe the Sabbatical while everybody observed the Sabbath.</i> on the Sabbath, if unintentionally he may keep it, if intentionally it must be uprooted. In the Sabbatical year it must be uprooted, whether in error or intentionally.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not give heave from pure produce”, etc. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>): “Your heave will be counted as if it were grain from the threshing-floor and what was drawn off from the wine-press..” Since for threshing-floor and wine-press it is impossible that part of it be impure and part pure<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For fluids it is obvious that if part of it became impure, all is impure. The case of grain is more complicated but in two cases the assertion is certainly true. If the grain is prepared for impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 141</a>) before threshing, then in sweeping the grains together to form an orderly heap, any impurity will be spread through the entire heap. If the grain is not prepared, then any moistening of the heap will make the entire heap prepared.</i>, even so one infers what is possible from what is impossible<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The verse quoted refers to heaves of the tithe for grain and wine. For these, it is impossible to give Great Heave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.14">Chapter 1, Note 64</a>) from pure for impure since Great Heave is given only from earmarked produce; it is inferred that nowhere may one give heave from pure produce on impure.<br>The statement that abstract principles can be transferred from impossible to possible cases is not found in the Babli but Tosaphot refer to it several times to explain the background of talmudic reasoning (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 13b" href="/Beitzah.13b"><i>Beẓah</i> 13b</a>, <i>s. v.</i> כשם; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 30b" href="/Gittin.30b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 30b, <i>s. v.</i> וכי</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 54b" href="/Menachot.54b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 54b, <i>s. v.</i> כך</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 59b" href="/Bekhorot.59b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 59b, <i>s. v.</i> אף</a>).</i>. In that case, the heave given should not be heave! It is written: “From itself<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">“From itself” is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18" href="/Numbers.18"><i>Num</i>. 18</a> several times, verses 26, 28, 29, 30, 32. While it is asserted in the next paragraph that heave of the tithe, the subject of that paragraph, need not be from the particular batch of tithe for which it is given, it is emphasized sufficiently to point out that Great Heave may be given from any part of the batch, even if part of it is pure and the remainder impure.</i>”.
Everything teaches for itself and for others<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Every verse of the Torah first is needed for its subject but then can be used to deduce rules for other cases by the standard rules of inference, either following R. Ismael or R. Aqiba.</i>, the heave of the tithes teaches for others what it does not teach for itself<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">While very few verses speak about the obligation of the Great Heave and no details are given, the verses dealing with the heave of the tithe are many and partially contradictory. As explained in the paragraph, the general expressions are taken to refer to the Great Heave and the detailed instructions for the heave of the tithe.</i>. The heave of the tithe teaches for Great Heave that it should not be taken from pure produce for impure but itself can be taken from pure produce for impure. From where? Rebbi Yose in the name of Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Yannai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num</i>. 18:28</a>): “You shall give from it the Eternal’s heave to Aharon the priest;” it should be given to Aharon in his status as priest<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Sacral meals, the main duty of a Cohen, must all be eaten in strict purity.</i>. Cahana said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:29" href="/Numbers.18.29"><i>Num</i>. 18:29</a>): “From its best, the sanctified part from it;” take from its sanctifiable part. The heave of the tithe teaches for Great Heave that it should be taken from earmarked produce<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">“From itself” is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18" href="/Numbers.18"><i>Num</i>. 18</a> several times, verses 26, 28, 29, 30, 32. While it is asserted in the next paragraph that heave of the tithe, the subject of that paragraph, need not be from the particular batch of tithe for which it is given, it is emphasized sufficiently to point out that Great Heave may be given from any part of the batch, even if part of it is pure and the remainder impure.</i> but itself may be taken from non-earmarked. From where that heave of the tithe may be taken from non-earmarked produce? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:28" href="/Numbers.18.28"><i>Num</i>. 18:28</a>) “From <i>all</i> of your tithes,” one in Judea and one in Galilee. The heave of the tithe teaches for Great Heave that it should be taken only from finished produce, itself also should be taken from finished produce<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:5:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.5.9">Chapter 1, Note 206</a>.</i>.
Heave of the tithe is taken from pure produce for pure, from impure for impure, from pure for impure, but not from impure for pure<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Sacral meals, the main duty of a Cohen, must all be eaten in strict purity.</i>. Rebbi Neḥemiah says, just as it may not be taken from impure for pure, so it may not be taken from impure for impure. Rebbi Neḥemiah agrees in the case of <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 115b" href="/Bava_Kamma.115b"><i>Baba Qama</i> 115b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:19" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.19">Tosephta 3:19</a>) this is formulated as: “R. Neḥemiah says, one gives from impure produce for impure only in the case of <i>demay</i>,” since then the obligation is not clear-cut.</i>. That is, if he has from the same kind<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The consensus of the commentators is that this refers to R. Neḥemiah’s prohibition of giving heave of the tithe from impure for impure food.</i>. If he does not have anything from the same kind, let us hear from the following: Rebbi Ḥanina Eyntanaya went with Rebbi Zeïra to Ḥammat Gader. He bought him a roll<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Greek, cf. <i>Berakhot</i> Chap. 6, Note 103.</i> and wanted to put it in order from one day to the next<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was baked, one can be sure that the Great Heave had been given. The question is only about the heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i> which has to be given for bread from an uncertified baker (<i>Demay</i> Halakhah 5:1). Since the roll was for next morning’s breakfast, R. Ḥanina was wondering whether R. Neḥemiah would permit giving heave now since a roll handled by the unobservant certainly was impure, having been prepared for impurity by the water needed to make the dough.</i>. He said to him: We do not have to worry about a single opinion! Can you say that he had from the same kind<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Ḥanina is told not to worry, this implies that R. Neḥemiah forbids giving heave of the tithe of impure <i>demay</i> if some pure food of the same kind is in his possession. However, the question remains open since the only pure roll R. Ḥanania could possibly have bought the next day would have to be one made with fruit juice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.1.2"><i>Demay</i> Halakhah 5:1, Note 8</a>) and it is most unlikely that such rolls would be available commercially.</i>?
“In truth;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah. This statement is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.9"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 2, cf. Note 36</a>.</i>” Rebbi Eleazar said that every place where they stated “in truth,” it is practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai.
“If a cake of dried figs became partially impure one may give heave from its pure part for its impure part;” but if part of it becomes impure is not all of it impure<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since figs usually are washed before drying, they are susceptible for impurity and the water will connect all parts of the cake.</i>? The Mishnah deals with the case that they are connected only by fruit juice<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Fig cakes are not strings of figs put together when they are completely dry but made of fresh figs formed into cakes and then put out to ferment and dry. The sap of figs is not one of the fluids that make food susceptible to impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.1.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 5, Note 8</a>); if no water touched the cake before it was dry and the individual figs are still recognizable, then if later it is prepared for impurity and one fig was touched by an impure person, this fig will be impure by one degree more than the person who touched and the figs touching this fig will be impure by still one more degree, and so on, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a> (higher degree meaning lesser impurity). Since profane food can acquire only impurity in the first and second degrees, all figs not directly touching the impure fig will remain pure (if the cake is dry).</i>. But will it not be cut into two parts in the end<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody swallows an entire fig cake whole, could not the cake be put in order once a pure part was cut off? The answer is that this is preferred but one cannot take it for granted.</i>? Explain it if he gave it a name when it still was connected.
“And similarly for a bunch of vegetable.” The Mishnah deals with the case that it became impure as a bunch but when the single stalks were impure and he bound them together, this does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since unbound it would be giving pure heave for the impure, a later bunching cannot change the situation.</i>. But will it not be split and made into two parts in the end<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody swallows an entire fig cake whole, could not the cake be put in order once a pure part was cut off? The answer is that this is preferred but one cannot take it for granted.</i>? Explain it if he gave it a name when it still was connected. What is the situation if a bundle became impure, he unbound it<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In that stage, it would be giving from impure for impure. The question is not answered.</i> and bound it again?
Should we have stated only fig cake and neither bunch nor heap? If we had stated about fig cake and not bunch we would have said he may give heave for the fig cake which is all one body but not for the bunch which is not one body. So it is necessary to state also “bunch”. If we had stated bunch not heap, we would have said he may give heave for the bunch because it is all held together but not for the heap which is not held together in one piece. So it is necessary to state fig-cake, necessary to state bunch, and necessary to state heap.
Since we stated: “If there were two fig-cakes, two bunches, and two heaps,” why do we need this since Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:27" href="/Numbers.18.27"><i>Num</i>. 18:27</a>): “Your heave will be counted as if it were grain<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For fluids it is obvious that if part of it became impure, all is impure. The case of grain is more complicated but in two cases the assertion is certainly true. If the grain is prepared for impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 141</a>) before threshing, then in sweeping the grains together to form an orderly heap, any impurity will be spread through the entire heap. If the grain is not prepared, then any moistening of the heap will make the entire heap prepared.</i>,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Either this part of the Mishnah or the statement of R. Joḥanan is superfluous.</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, the Mishnah speaks about heaps of green melons<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.1"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 1, Note 38</a>.</i> or squash<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are large individual fruits, the argument of R. Joḥanan is not applicable; neither does R. Joḥanan’s statement follow from the Mishnah.</i>.
If there were two fig cakes, one partially impure and one partially pure<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Fig cakes are not strings of figs put together when they are completely dry but made of fresh figs formed into cakes and then put out to ferment and dry. The sap of figs is not one of the fluids that make food susceptible to impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.5.1.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 5, Note 8</a>); if no water touched the cake before it was dry and the individual figs are still recognizable, then if later it is prepared for impurity and one fig was touched by an impure person, this fig will be impure by one degree more than the person who touched and the figs touching this fig will be impure by still one more degree, and so on, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 137</a> (higher degree meaning lesser impurity). Since profane food can acquire only impurity in the first and second degrees, all figs not directly touching the impure fig will remain pure (if the cake is dry).</i>, may one give heave from one for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah permits taking pure heave also for the impure parts of the same fig cake, may one put both cakes together on a table where they are earmarked and give one heave for both of them?</i>? If he had two heaps<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">כרי is a heap of freshly threshed grain, ערימה one of totally dry grain.</i>, and from both of them he had given part of heaves and tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 4:1 states that if only partial heave and tithes were taken from a heap they would have no status as heave and tithes but, even though they would now be separated from the heap, they would still count as one for giving heave from an earmarked heap. May one give one heave for both in this case? The two cases are treated as similar.</i>, may he give heave from one for the other? The students of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya asked the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya and he said to them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:5" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.5"><i>Eccl</i>. 4:5</a>): “The fool folds his hands and eats his own flesh.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The answer, “no”, is too simple and a thinking person should not have to ask.</i>” Rebbi Eleazar in the name of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: He may not give heave from one for the other.
<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:8" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.8">Tosephta 3:8</a>, cf. <i>Tosephta ki-fshutah</i> for the textual variants.</i>“If somebody brought many kinds in a box, cabbage on top and cabbage on the bottom but something else in between, he may not give heave or tithe from one for the other.” That means, if there is no empty space. But if there is empty space he may give heave or tithe from one for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If the entire box is filled up, the two layers of cabbage are not earmarked together. But if there is free space where the walls of the box hold together just the two layers of cabbage, they are earmarked and can be tithed together.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:7" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.7">Tosephta 3:7</a>, in the name of R. Yose.</i>“The entire house of the wine press is one<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">All wine barrels in the house of the wine press are earmarked together.</i>.” The entire threshing house is one. It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah said, only if the main threshing place is in the middle. So far this is for wheat; [it is] the same even for stubbles, even for straw.
Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose: Was impure [produce] between them? He said to him, is that biting<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">If three layers are in the box but the middle layer is impure, bottom and top are not prepared for impurity, and there is no empty space, then top and bottom do not go together because they do not physically touch. For the notion of “biting”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.6.6"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 9, Note 172</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallah</i> 2:4</a>.</i>? The colleagues asked before Rebbi Yose: If there was a blanket between them? He said to them, that is the case of the five sacks, as it was stated: “Five sacks on the threshing floor, one does not give heave and tithes from one for the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This text contradicts the previous statement that the entire enclosed area of the threshing floor is earmarking for heave. In the parallel text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:10" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.10">Tosephta 3:10</a> and Yerushalmi <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 3:11, the text reads: “One gives heave and tithes from one for the other.” While the reading of the Tosephta does not prove anything for the Yerushalmi, the text in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> indicates that the text here is corrupt. Maimonides (<i>Terumot</i> 3:18) and Roš (<i>Hilkhot Ḥallah</i> 4) follow the text in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i>.</i>”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:18" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.18">Tosephta 3:18</a>; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:3:7-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.3.7-9"><i>Ḥallah</i> 2:8</a> (fol. 58d) the entire paragraph.</i>: “Rebbi Illaï<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The father of Rebbi Jehudah, one of the few known students of R. Eliezer.</i> says in the name of Rebbi Eliezer: For food in fluids, one gives heave from pure for impure<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A fluid becomes impure in its entirety if any drop of it becomes impure.</i>. How is this? If somebody pickled olives in impurity and wants to give heave in purity, he brings a funnel whose opening is less than [the width of] an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An opening smaller than the diameter of an egg lets impurity enter but not leave. Hence, anything in the funnel may become impure but it cannot transmit impurity to the food in the barrel.</i>, fills it with eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta and in <i>Ḥallah</i>, “olives”. This reading is required since eggs are not subject to heave. Also, the width of an egg is only an upper bound on the diameter of the funnel; since all olives touching the impure olives or their brine will become impure, one certainly is interested in minimizing the number of impure olives generated. In addition, unless the diameter of the funnel is less than the width of an olive, the pure olives filled into the funnel must be completely dry, otherwise they all become impure.</i> and puts it on top of the amphora; it turns out that he gives heave from what is earmarked<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The amphora and the funnel touching it form a well-defined domain in space.</i>.” Why does it have to be less than [the width of] an egg? Are these not single pieces<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.2.1.10">Note 1</a>. Since the lowest layer of olives becomes impure, only it and the layer directly above will be impure; all others will be pure and usable for heave.</i>? It is only that not many olives should become impure. “<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:18" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.18">Tosephta 3:18</a>; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 2:3:7-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.2.3.7-9"><i>Ḥallah</i> 2:8</a> (fol. 58d) the entire paragraph.</i> They said to him, the only food fluid called <i>fluid</i> is wine and olive oil<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The position of R. Eliezer may be accepted but the interpretation given it by R. Illaï is certainly wrong. One has to consider fluids, not wet solid food.</i>.” How is that? One beam for two pits or two beams for one pit<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For mechanical pressing, grapes or olives are put into a vat and are covered by a circular cover. On top of it is a beam which is depressed either manually or by a viselike device. If two adjacent vats are pressed simultaneously, this certainly is considered earmarked. The beam itself, wood without cavities, cannot become impure.</i>. One understands one beam for two pits, but two beams for one pit? Is it not that if it is partially impure it is totally impure<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A fluid becomes impure in its entirety if any drop of it becomes impure.</i>? Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Explain it, if he had intended to process it in one batch, and he changed his mind to make it in two batches<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Two beams for one vat are impractical; that case has to be thrown out. R. Eliezer permits giving heave from pure for impure if the vintner (or olive grower) originally had the intention of processing his entire harvest in one press. Then they become one batch. If later he decides to process in two different presses and it turns out that one is pure, the other impure, he may give heave for his entire harvest from the pure lot.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, this applies if it became impure after he syphoned off [the froth] and [the seeds] formed lumps. But if it became impure before he syphoned off and lumps were formed, it does not apply. Rebbi Tabi, Rebbi Joshia the son of Rebbi Yannai: Practice follows Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Practice follows Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Ḥuna, Rebbi Ḥanania: Practice does not follow Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Samuel: Practice does not follow Rebbi Eliezer. There came a case before Rebbi Immi and he did not decide; he said there are two against two. They said to him, but Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman decided! Nevertheless, he gave no opinion.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> 4:6.</i>, we have stated: “If somebody gives heave from a wine cistern and says: ‘This should be heave on condition that it be removed whole’, it means whole from breakage and whole from spillage<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the amphora containing the heave wine breaks or some of its contents are spilled before the amphora is safely removed from the cistern, the wine that flows back into the cistern is <i>ṭevel</i> in breakage and profane in spillage, never heave, and does not make the remaining wine <i>dema‘</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.21"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 1, Note 175</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.3.2">Chapter 4, Note 25</a>; <i>Terumot</i> Chapter 5). <i>Dema‘</i> wine is almost worthless.</i> but not from impurity. Rebbi Simeon says, also from impurity.” Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, this Mishnah speaks of the impurity of <i>ṭevul yom</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A person freed from impurity by immersion in water who will become pure at sundown. His touch does not make impure but it invalidates heave and sacrifices, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.5.3"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 6, Note 136</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ila said, since <i>ṭevulê yom</i> may be present around wine presses. Rebbi Yose said, a <i>ṭevul yom</i> has no touch for <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The touch of a <i>ṭevul yom</i> invalidates heave but not <i>ṭevel</i> and certainly not profane wine. Therefore, a <i>ṭevul yom</i> may work in a winery producing pure wine and R. Simeon is right when he assumes that a reservation concerning the touch of a <i>ṭevul yom</i> is in the mind of the person taking heave, even if this is not consciously expressed. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.3.2.2"><i>Ḥallah</i> 3:2, Note 43</a>.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> 2:16–17, formulated as anonymous statement. {In the Rome ms., the name of the tradent is R. Yose. This reading is proven false in the next paragraph.} The Tosephta cannot represent the Yerushalmi tradition since for תרומה גדולה it has תרומת ראשית “beginning heave”, a reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, about what was this said? About Great Heave which must be given from what is earmarked. But for the heave of the tithe, everything is supposed to be whole. If he gave Great Heave and heave of the tithe together, it is not whole.” Jehudah ben Rebbi said, he certainly did not want to overstep the words of the Torah in giving heave of the tithe before the Great Heave<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He explains why heave of the tithe is under the rules of Great Heave in case both are taken together. If Great Heave were invalidated but not heave of the tithe, it would turn out retroactively that heave of the tithe was given before the Great Heave, which not only is forbidden but also is very difficult to rectify. Therefore, we assume that he did not want any complications and, if made aware of this problem, would himself want to nullify the entire transaction.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected: Did we not state that “one may not give heave from impure produce for pure”? Think about it, when he did it and said, I did not intend to transgress the words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since one may not give impure heave for pure produce, why do we not stipulate that if the person giving in error would be made aware of what he did, he automatically would want to nullify the transaction. The answer is that in this case, the remedy would be worse than the problem.</i>! Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, if you say so, you let him flee from a minor sin to a major one! <i>Ṭevel</i> is a deadly sin, but an impure person who ate pure food oversteps a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text in both mss. but it is impossible since the impure Cohen who eats heave transgresses a <i>karet</i> prohibition. It must read: “a pure person who ate impure food”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:11" href="/Numbers.18.11"><i>Num</i>. 18:11</a>: “Every pure person in your house shall eat it,” <i>viz</i>., pure heave. The details are given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:1:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.1.2-15"><i>Bikkurim</i> 2:1</a>; there the text is as corrected here.</i>.
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Yose: “If somebody gave heave from impure for pure produce, whether it was in error or intentionally, what he did is done<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 3:19" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).3.19">Tosephta 3:19</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Phineas asked before Rebbi Assi: There<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">He asks about the statement of R. Jehudah in the Mishnah that previous knowledge invalidates later acts of absentmindedness. It is agreed that this holds if he forgets facts; what if he is in error about the law? One must assume that sometime in the past, he had been informed of the correct practice.</i>, it is when he thought that it was pure when he had known that it was impure, but he was of the opinion that one may give heave from impure for pure produce? He said to him, that is so, Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The editor of the anonymous Mishnah.</i> holds with Rebbi Jehudah. But for Rebbi Yose it would not make any difference.
<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are also in <i>Šabbat</i> 2:7, this paragraph also in <i>Beẓah</i> 2:1.</i> Our Mishnah is about large vessels, but with small vessels one may use a ruse and immerse them, as Rebbi Hoshaia had stated<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 16:12" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).16.12">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 16:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 2:8:4-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.2.8.4-5"><i>Beẓah</i>2:9</a>; a similar <i>baraita</i> quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 18a" href="/Beitzah.18a">Babli <i>Beẓah</i> 18a</a>. The Tosephta makes the procedure explicit: “Vessels impure in original impurity may not be immersed on a holiday, and certainly not on the Sabbath. But one may fill a cup, or a pail, or a bucket in order to drink <i>at the same time one thinks about it</i>” [that it should be a valid immersion.] Impurity can be removed only by conscious immersion.</i>: “A person may fill an impure vessel from the cistern as a ruse and immerse it.” Also, we have stated: “If his pail or garment fell into the cistern, one may use this as a ruse and immerse them<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">At the moment he goes to pull the things out of the cistern, he may intend to have the immersion count for removal of impurity even though the immersion itself was accidental.</i>.” Two Amoraïm, one says [one may use a ruse] for vessels impure in original impurity; but the other one says [only] for vessels impure in derivative impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For original and derivative impurity, cf <i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 137. The language of the Tosephta shows that it was unknown to the editors of the Yerushalmi.</i>. The one who said in derivative impurity objected to the one who said in original impurity: Even on weekdays, would it not need sundown<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The vessels are <i>Ṭevulê Yom</i>. As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 2:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.2.1.14">Note 51</a>, immersion purifies immediately for all profane food and purposes.</i>? He said to him, if he wants to use them in purity for profane food.
Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi: An intelligent woman washes a cup here, a bowl there, a dish there; it turns out that her entire house was wetted on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel is in <i>Šabbat</i> 15:3 (fol. 15b). The house has a dirt floor, and periodic wetting is needed to keep the air dust free. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 95a" href="/Shabbat.95a">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i>95a</a>) notes that this trick is superfluous in Babylonian practice. The Babli follows R. Simeon for whom any work is permitted on the Sabbath if its intention is legitimate and permitted, even if some side effect would constitute a forbidden action if done by itself, unless the forbidden action is unavoidable. In our case, wetting the floor to strengthen the ground and the adobe house would be an action derivative of building and forbidden. But wetting the floor lightly to avoid dust is a legitimate action and, since in this case the water will evaporate quickly and any building effect would be temporary, it will be permitted outright. It follows that the Yerushalmi rejects the point of view of Rebbi Simeon.</i>.
Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: “Unintentionally”, when it is forbidden, “intentionally” when it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The two parts must be parallel, the difference between “intentional” and “unintentional” can be only in the mind of the agent. There are two possibilities, either that he forgot that it was Sabbath or that he was unaware that cooking is forbidden on the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah states this: “He who tithes or cooks on the Sabbath, if unintentionally it may be eaten, if intentionally it may not be eaten”; “unintentionally”, when it is forbidden, “intentionally”, when it is [equally] forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since intentionally he must have known that it was Sabbath, the unintentional case must be the same.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 2:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 34a" href="/Ketubot.34a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 34a</a>, and partially in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 38a" href="/Shabbat.38a"><i>Šabbat</i>38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 17a" href="/Beitzah.17a"><i>Beẓah</i> 17a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 71a" href="/Bava_Kamma.71a"><i>Baba Qama</i> 71a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 15a" href="/Chullin.15a"><i>Ḥulin</i>15a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 52b" href="/Gittin.52b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 52b</a>. From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is also in <i>Šabbat</i> 3:1, fol. 5d.</i>: “What was cooked on the Sabbath, if it was done unintentionally it may be eaten, intentionally it may not be eaten, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah said, unintentionally it may be eaten at the end of the Sabbath, intentionally never. Rebbi Joḥanan the Alexandrian<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A student of R. Aqiba, Tanna of the fourth generation. It seems that the name <i>Sandlar</i> already then meant “Alexander’s”.</i> said, unintentionally it may be eaten at the end of the Sabbath by others but not by himself, intentionally neither by him nor by others.” Samuel followed Rebbi Joḥanan the Alexandrian. Rav in his own group instructed following Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Rav stated the exact rules to his students as theory. But in public he followed more restrictive rules. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 15a" href="/Chullin.15a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 15a</a>, Rav is reported to publicly proclaiming R. Jehudah’s rule.</i>, in public following Rebbi Joḥanan the Alexandrian. Rebbi Simeon bar Karsana<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the fourth generation, student of R. Aḥa. His appearance as reporting Rav’s direct instructions to his students seems anachronistic.</i> said, he instructed us following Rebbi Ismael, the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 2:16" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).2.16">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 2:16</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa said, in any case where an intentional sin is punished by extirpation and an unintentional sin by an expiatory offering<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Cooking on the Sabbath.</i>, if the sin was committed on the Sabbath unintentionally or intentionally, the result is forbidden both for him and for others; it may not be eaten. In any case an intentional sin is not punished by extirpation and for the unintentional sin no expiatory offering is due<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Tithing on the Sabbath.</i>; if it was done on the Sabbath unintentionally the result may be eaten by others after the end of the Sabbath but not by him, intentionally neither by him nor by others.” They asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: What do you say? He said, I have only the Mishnah: “He who tithes or cooks on the Sabbath, if in error it may be eaten, if intentionally it may not be eaten”. Rav Ḥisda heard this and said, the Sabbaths have been permitted! Did not Rav Huna say in the name of Rav and did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The first sentence here, without the introductory “in earlier times they said”, is the first sentence of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 2:14</a>. The remainder of that Tosephta, in the name of R. Jehudah, parallels the tannaitic text in the next paragraph.</i>: “In earlier times they said, one who forgets some dish on his stove on the Sabbath, unintentionally it may be eaten, intentionally it may not be eaten. People were suspected that they left it intentionally and said, it was forgotten; they forbade it to them when it was forgotten.” And here, you say so? Rebbi Ila said, they were suspected to leave it on, they were not suspected to cook. They imposed a fine for forgetting but not for cooking.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The first sentence here, without the introductory “in earlier times they said”, is the first sentence of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 2:14</a>. The remainder of that Tosephta, in the name of R. Jehudah, parallels the tannaitic text in the next paragraph.</i> They came back to say, a dish which will improve the more it shrinks is forbidden, deteriorate the more it shrinks is permitted. What is a dish better the more it shrinks? Cabbage, peas, and chopped meat.” Rebbi Tanḥum bar Illa said, they also considered heads of turnips and leeks as dishes better the more they shrink. What about eggs? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ismael: Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose: My father came home and found eggs and forbade them, hot water and permitted it<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, R. Jehudah permits hot water to stay on an open fire only if it came to a boil before the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Natan in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: I went with my father to Ḥammat Gader where they brought before us eggs small like <i>ḥizrar</i> apples<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 4:1</a>. The eggs must have been boiled a very long time to shrink to this small size. The statement might disagree with the Babli which reports that Samuel used eggs dipped alternatingly into boiling and cold water 1000 times as a diagnostic device for ulcers and intestinal bleeding since they were so hard that they were not digested and could be examined after evacuation but were small enough to be swallowed whole (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 50b" href="/Nedarim.50b"><i>Nedarim</i> 50b</a>).</i> and they tasted delicious like sweet bread<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Probably Greek πάγκρεας, -ατος, τό “sweet bread, pancreas” (E. G.).</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 3:1</a>. ‘<i>Assasiot</i> are an undetermined kind of legumes (in the words of the Gaonim: they exist in the Land of Israel but not in Babylonia) which need very long cooking to become edible, similar to lupines. Compare Arabic عصّ “to harden”.</i>: “A woman should not fill a pot with <i>‘assasiot</i> and lupines and put them into the oven at the start of the Sabbath, at nightfall. If she did, they are forbidden after the end of the Sabbath until the time they could have been done.” Rebbi Aḥa said: intentionally, following Rebbi Meïr; Rebbi Yose said: unintentionally, following Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Mana said, that is correct, did not Rebbi Yose say, “<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shabbat (Lieberman) 2:21" href="/Tosefta_Shabbat_(Lieberman).2.21">Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 2:21</a>. Both reasons are given in the Tosephta as basis of the decision of the anonymous majority but R. Jehudah’s statement is left without explanation. The <i>baraita</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 53b" href="/Gittin.53b">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 53b</a>) gives no reasons at all.</i> He who is planting on the Sabbath, if unintentional, he may keep it, if intentional, he must tear it out. In the Sabbatical year, he must tear it out whether [planting was] unintentional or intentional. Rebbi Jehudah says, it is the other way around. In the Sabbatical, if unintentional, he may keep it, if intentional, he must tear it out, on the Sabbath he must tear it out whether [planting was] unintentional or intentional because the profit of the Sabbath [is forbidden] to him.” And here, since you say that after the end of the Sabbath he has to wait until the time it could have been done, he did not gain anything from the Sabbath. What is the reason of the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous opinion in the Tosephta.</i>? People are suspected about the Sabbatical but not about the Sabbath. Another explanation: One counts Sabbaticals, one does not count Sabbaths<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since the fruits of trees are forbidden the first three years as ‘<i>orlah</i> and must be redeemed in the fourth year, everybody will realize that the tree was illegally planted in the Sabbatical.</i>. What is this about? If somebody planted less than thirty days before the Sabbatical and now it is the Sabbatical, if it is because of suspicion, there is no suspicion; if it is for counting, it is counted<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The first year of ‘<i>orlah</i> is the Sabbatical, the 30 days are not counted; cf. <i>Ševi‘it</i> 2:6, Mishnah and Note 45.</i>. Less than thirty days before the eighth year and now it is the eighth year, if it is because of suspicion, there is suspicion; can it be explained for counting<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first year counted is the first year of the Sabbatical cycle, nobody will notice that the tree was planted in the last month of the Sabbatical.</i>? But you have to say because of suspicion; they fined for the unintentional because of the intentional<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A reason usually attributed to R. Meïr, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:6:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.6.2-5"><i>Orlah</i> 3:7</a>. The text of the last clause in <i>Šabbat</i> is longer but the formulation here seems to be the original one.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not give heave from one species for another species; if he gave, his heave is no heave. All kinds of wheat are one. All kinds of figs, dried figs, and fig cakes are one; he may give heave from one for the other. At a place where there is a Cohen one gives from the best, but where there is no Cohen he gives from what is durable. Rebbi Jehudah says, one always gives from the best.<br>One gives a small whole onion<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous Tanna considers large onions better than small ones and whole onions more durable than divided ones.</i> as heave but not half a large onion. Rebbi Jehudah says it is not so, one rather gives half a large onion. Similarly, Rebbi Jehudah said one gives onions of town people as heave for rural ones but not rural ones for those of town people because they are city-dwellers’<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πολιτικός (adj.), “fit for, belonging to the city”. Onions sold in the city are of uniform quality while rural onions are those that cannot be sold in the city.</i> food.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yoḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: This is one of three obvious inferences in the Torah. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:12" href="/Numbers.18.12"><i>Num</i>. 18:12</a>): “All the best of olive oil, all the best of cider and grain<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This is the description of heave; the description as “best” is the basis of the position of R. Jehudah. In the verse, olive oil is treated separately from cider and grain. Everybody agrees that heave of olive oil must be kept separate from that of cider and grain. The argument, known as בנין אב מכתוב אחד, is that if two different species of trees are treated as distinct then two species belonging to completely different families of plants certainly must be treated as distinct.</i>.” Where do we hold? Is it to conclude that one may give heave from cider for grain and from grain for cider? One must object that cider and olive oil come from two different species of tree and one may not give heave or tithe from one for the other! This means that cider and olive oil are paradigms for two species of trees, in which case one may not give heave or tithe from one for the other. Then I have to add two species of grain, two kinds of produce of which one may not give heave or tithe from one for the other.
“All kinds of wheat are one.” It is needed [since it applies] even from white wheat on brown wheat and vice-versa<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">For the determination of these kinds, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.4.2"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 2, Notes 85–86</a>.</i>.
