After the preliminary wedding ceremony (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> 6:2, Note 46</a>) the bride really is “acquired by the husband’s money” and, by biblical law (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:11" href="/Leviticus.22.11"><i>Lev</i>. 22:11</a>) she may eat heave if the husband is a Cohen. By an old rabbinic institution, she is prevented from eating heave until the date originally fixed for the actual wedding, when it was planned that she should start living with her husband. The later authorities restricted the access of the Israel bride of a Cohen to the time after the final marriage ceremony.<br>The final marriage ceremony emancipates the bride from her father’s authority. During the time between preliminary and final ceremonies, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:2</a> states that both the minor and her father may accept a divorce document; R. Jehudah denies the right of the minor to receive the document (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.6"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 4, Note 117</a>). The Mishnah here can refer to with a woman eating heave in error only in case the woman still is a minor and the groom did not finally marry her at the previously stipulated time. In this approach, with the decree of the later authorities the mention of a divorce in our Mishnah became meaningless.</i>, according to which the betrothed daughter of an Israel eats heave and her father accepts her divorce document. Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">He must be R. Eleazar ben Pedat, the student of R. Joḥanan. Probably, his name was changed here to distinguish him from R. Eleazar ben Shamua‘, the Tanna, who is quoted in the next sentence. [The changes from “R. Eliezer” to “R. Eleazar” and vice-versa in all printed editions has no basis in mss. The Yerushalmi tradition of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:4</a> (Leyden ms. and Venice print, Mishnah ed. Low) as well as the main text of the Munich ms. of the Babli and the mss. of the later version of Maimonides’s Commentary read “R. Eleazar”; the Napoli Mishnah and the early version of Maimonides’s Commentary read “R. Eliezer”.] Since R. Eleazar is the main authority on divorce documents, it is reasonable to read “R. Eleazar” in the Mishnah.</i> says, you may even say following the later Mishnah; explain it that she said to him, bring my divorce document from place X and usually that should have taken him ten days to deliver to her but he found a galloping horse and brought it to her in five days. Rebbi Eliezer cannot follow Rebbi Eleazar, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:4</a>: “Bring me my divorce document, she eats heave until the document comes into her possession. Accept the document for me, she immediately is forbidden heave. Accept the document for me at place X, she eats heave until the document reaches place X; Rebbi Eleazar forbids her immediately.” This Mishnah speaks of the adult wife appointing an agent. (A minor, even though emancipated from her father, cannot appoint an agent. She can be divorced only by delivery of the document into her hands by the husband or his duly appointed agent. If she is too young to take care of the document, she cannot be divorced.)</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar forbids her immediately.” Does Rebbi Eleazar not agree that if she said, bring my divorce document from place X, that she may eat heave until the document arrives at that place<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">An agent who violates the terms of his appointment is not a valid agent for divorce documents. If the agent would accept the document at another place, the document would be invalid and the woman not divorced. There is no reason for her not to eat heave until the document reaches the specified place.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaninah said, when she said to him, bring my divorce document from place X, it is as if she had said to him, it shall become a divorce document only after ten days. What she ate, she ate with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, even R. Eliezer will agree that heave was eaten lawfully, there is no case of restitution of anything, and the explanation of R. Eleazar ben Pedat is contradicted.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥaggai asked the colleagues, from where that he who eats with permission is free<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This is not an attack on the argument of R. Ḥanina; if a person has no intention of breaking the law and is not breaking the law, obviously he is free not only of guilt but of all monetary obligations.<br>The parallel to this text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.2-10"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1,</a> fol. 45c; a text which differs in places from the text here. The commentaries assume that the text there is the better one; they take the question of R. Ḥaggai as directed against the argument of R. Ḥaninah; therefore, they are forced in the next sentence to follow the <i>Horaiot</i> text and to switch the terms “Israel” and “Cohen”, an emendation not supported here by any manuscript and contradicted by the third question of R. Ḥaggai.</i>? What is the difference between him who thought that it was profane but it turned out that it was heave, who is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">An Israel who ate what he thought was profane food. If it turns out that it was heave, he has to pay as stated in Mishnah 6:1. If it turns out that he was in fact a Cohen but did not know it, he does not have to pay even though at the time of eating he thought he was an Israel. If payment were a punishment for negligence, there is no reason why he should not be held liable.<br>In the Horaiot text, speaking of a person who thought he was a Cohen when he was not, the reference would be to the position of R. Joshua in the Mishnah here and the “declaration of the court” is the court decision which strips him of his priesthood, on the testimony of two cross-examined credible witnesses. The conditions under which an action of the court obligates him to bring a sacrifice are noted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 1:1:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.1.1.2-10"><i>Horaiot</i> 1:1</a>; the reading of <i>Horaiot</i> is appropriate there, the reading of <i>Terumot</i> is appropriate here.</i>, and him who thought that he was an Israel but it turned out that he was a Cohen, who is free? They said to him, by the declaration of the court<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The interpretation of the biblical law quoted later.</i>. He said to them, still I am having a problem. What is the difference between him who thought that it was a weekday but it turned out that it was a Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The person violating the Sabbath in error has to bring an expiatory sacrifice.</i>, who is obligated, and him who thought that it was a Passover sacrifice but it turned out that it was a well-being offering, who is free<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.3.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i>6:4</a>. The Passover sacrifice, a lamb or kid goat, has to be sacrificed during the afternoon of Nissan 14, even if that day is a Sabbath. It also has to be designated beforehand as Passover sacrifice and is counted as a special kind of well-being offering. If the 14th falls on a weekday, a festival offering, which is a well-being offering, is also slaughtered at the same time (cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, Northvale NJ 1995, p. 248.) On the Sabbath, any slaughtering not directly mandated by the biblical verse is forbidden. On that the Mishnah states: “If the Passover sacrifice [which is valid only if slaughtered for this particular purpose] was slaughtered not for its purpose on the Sabbath, one is obligated for an expiatory sacrifice. For any well-being offering slaughtered as a Passover sacrifice which was not usable [a calf or an old sheep or goat], one is obligated. If it was usable, R. Eliezer obligates him for an expiatory sacrifice, R. Joshua declares him free.” Why should he be free?</i>? They said to him, because he slaughtered with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The language refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.4.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6:6</a>: “If he slaughtered [the Passover sacrifice on a Sabbath] and it turned out to have a blemish, he is obligated [for an expiatory sacrifice]. If it turned out to be unusable because of an internal defect [which could not have been detected by inspection when the animal was alive], he is free. If he slaughtered and then it turned out that the owners had withdrawn from it [they chose another lamb as their sacrifice], or that they had died [and the sacrifice became unusable because nobody may eat from the Passover unless he joined the group before the lamb was slaughtered], or had become impure, he is free since he slaughtered with permission.” Since R. Joshua declares him free and the animal could have been a valid Passover sacrifice, he slaughtered with permission.</i>. He said to them, still I am having a problem. What is the difference between him who thought that it was permitted fat but it turned out that it was forbidden fat, who is obligated, and him who thought that it was forbidden but it turned out that it was permitted, who is free<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">He thought the fat he ate was forbidden when in fact it was permitted. Then in his mind he should be more guilty of negligence than the one who thought the fat was permitted when it was in fact forbidden.</i>? They did not answer him. What did he say to them? Let us take counsel! (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:23" href="/Leviticus.4.23"><i>Lev</i>. 4:23</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28">28</a>): “Or his transgression in which he sinned was made known to him; he has to bring.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The involved statement is explained to mean that one became aware of the sinful character of what he had done inadvertently. If a person never knew that certain acts are forbidden, he cannot become obligated for an expiatory sacrifice since no negligence was involved. If he knew and then did something inadvertently, he is obligated for his negligence. But if he knew beforehand that, had he known then what he knows now, his action would have been legitimate, there is no negligence and no sacrifice.</i>” He who returns from his prior knowledge is obligated for his error; this excludes one who would not stop even if he knew! Rebbi Assi went to see them and said, why did you not answer him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:23" href="/Leviticus.4.23"><i>Lev</i>. 4:23</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:28" href="/Leviticus.4.28">28</a>): “Or his transgression in which he sinned was made known to him; he has to bring”? They said to him, he asked the question and gave the answer<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is more explicit in <i>Horaiot</i>, and written in better style.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the second part of Mishnah 1.</i>: Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: This is one of three derivations<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.3.2">Chapter 1, Note 159</a>. The parallel in the Babli to the derivations here is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 66b" href="/Kiddushin.66b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 66b</a>. The argument of Rav, Rav Abba, somewhat changed, is there given in the name of Samuel’s father, Abba ben Abba. Elsewhere (<i>Sifry Deut</i>. 298. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 28b" href="/Sanhedrin.28b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 28b</a>) the argument of R. Yannai is given in the name of R. Yose the Galilean (the Tanna); the argument of Rav is not found in tannaїtic sources.</i> which are clear from the Torah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:3" href="/Deuteronomy.26.3"><i>Deut</i>. 26:3</a>): “You shall come to the Cohen who will be in those days.” Is there a Cohen now who is not a Cohen after some time? Who is that? That is one who was standing sacrificing on the altar when it became known that he is the son of a divorcee or of one who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, that his work is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Rosh Hashanah (Lieberman) 2:18" href="/Tosefta_Rosh_Hashanah_(Lieberman).2.18">Tosephta <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 2:18</a>, the similar verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:9" href="/Deuteronomy.17.9"><i>Deut</i>. 17:9</a> is explained that Cohen and judge in one’s own time have the same authority as Aaron and Moses in their time.</i>. Rav said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:11" href="/Deuteronomy.33.11"><i>Deut</i>. 33:11</a>) “You will want the work of his hands,” that the work of everybody who is of the descendants of Levi is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse is addressed to the entire tribe of Levi and not only to the recognized Cohanim. Since the sons of divorcees are also descendants of Levi, they are included (if they happened to be admitted to the service.)</i>.
In Rav’s opinion, only a sacrificing Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In the preceding verse, burning of incense and sacrifices is mentioned. This restricts the verse to service in the Temple.</i>. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, any Cohen<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For any ceremony for which a Cohen is needed: Not only the reception of First Fruits but also the presentation of the firstling, judging skin disease, etc.</i>. In Rav’s opinion, only for sacrifices in the Temple. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, even holy food in the countryside. In the rabbis’ opinion, only during the existence of the Temple. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion, even today.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Does this apply even to the remaining actions<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If he performed an action which has to be followed by another one and he is notified of his desecrated status in the middle of the first action, can he finish the entire procedure?</i>? If he received [the blood], does he sprinkle [the blood on the walls of the altar]; if he takes a fist full [of the flour offering], can he burn it [on the altar]; if he burned [the red cow], can he sprinkle [water mixed with its ashes, to purify]? Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi in the name of Rebbi Isaac: Since we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:5</a>, in which it is stated, as testimony from Temple times, that the priests have to accept every animal offered as a sacrifice of expiation (which has to be eaten by the priests) and are not permitted to investigate whether it was stolen or robbed, unless such defect was public knowledge. Both Talmudim agree that this arrangement can be justified by biblical verses and that it was strictly enforced since nobody would be able to bring such a sacrifice if the priests were allowed to set their own standards of what is kosher.</i>: “they made it like a purification sacrifice which atones even if obtained by robbery, as long as this fact is unknown to the public;” this means<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If a questionable sacrifice is valid as a sacrifice, then a questionable Cohen’s actions are counted as valid actions.</i> that if he received, he sprinkles; if he takes a fist full, he burns; if he burned, he sprinkles.
Rebbi Jacob ben Zavdi asked in the name of Rebbi Isaac: If he was standing sacrificing on the altar when it became known to him that he was the son of a divorcee or a woman who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah Moëd</i>, how do you treat him<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This thoroughly theoretical case deals with a High Priest who is informed that the special court judging the status of priests has stripped him of his office because he was found to be the son of a divorcee, or of a woman who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, by the testimony of two reliable witnesses. The problem concerns a person found guilty of involuntary or negligent homicide sentenced to exile in a city of refuge (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:9-34" href="/Numbers.35.9-34"><i>Num</i>. 35:9–34</a>). He may return to his home town only after the death of the High Priest during whose term of office he was sentenced (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:28" href="/Numbers.35.28"><i>v</i>. 28</a>). This implies that if he was sentenced while there was no High Priest, he is sentenced to life in exile.</i>? As if he had died and the homicide might return to his home town, or as if [the latter’s] trial had been concluded when there was no High Priest, when he never could leave from there?<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The answer will be given in the paragraph after the next.</i>
Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In slightly enlarged form, Tosephta <i>Miqwa’ot</i> 1:17–20; in much shortened form <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 66b" href="/Kiddushin.66b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 66b</a>.<br>The “reservoir of Discus” according to Rashi is named either after a place or a person <i>Discus</i>, Latin proper n.</i>: “It happened that the reservoir of Discus at Jabneh was damaged, measured, and found deficient<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Because something happened to the structure, they measured the volume of the water after the accident and found it to be less than 40 <i>seah</i> and, therefore, to be unusable for purification (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.8.4">Chapter 4, Note 112</a>). Since earlier, the <i>miqweh</i> was a valid one, a person who had immersed himself before the accident came to ask whether he could be considered pure because of the prior validity of the <i>miqweh</i>, or whether he was impure since now the <i>miqweh</i> was invalid.</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon declared pure and Rebbi Aqiba impure. Rebbi Ṭarphon said, the prior status of this <i>miqweh</i> was one of purity; it remains forever in its purity until it becomes known that it is deficient. Rebbi Aqiba said, the prior status of the impure is impurity, he remains forever in his impurity until it becomes known that he is pure. Rebbi Ṭarphon said, to what can this be compared? To one who was standing sacrificing on the altar when it became known that he was the son of a divorcee or of a woman who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, whose work is valid. Rebbi Aqiba said, to what can this be compared? To one who was standing sacrificing on the altar when it became known that he has a bodily defect, whose work is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:16-24" href="/Leviticus.21.16-24"><i>Lev</i>. 21:16–24</a>. Since it is stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:17" href="/Leviticus.21.17"><i>v</i>. 17</a>) that a person with a bodily defect “shall not come close to present the bread of his God,” it is clear that this prohibition overrides the general inclusion inferred from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:3" href="/Deuteronomy.26.3"><i>Deut</i>. 26:3</a> or 33:11.</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon said to him, how is that, Aqiba? I am comparing this to the son of a divorcee or of a woman who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, and you compare it to one with a bodily defect. Let us see to which case it really is similar; if to the son of a divorcee or of a woman who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, let us learn from the son of the divorcee, but if to one with a bodily defect, let us learn from the person with a bodily defect. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, the <i>miqweh</i> is invalid because of an inherent defect; the one with a bodily defect is disqualified because of an inherent defect. The son of a divorcee cannot prove anything since he is disqualified because of others<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">His parents.</i>. The <i>miqweh</i> is invalid because of itself, the one with a bodily defect is disqualified because of himself; the son of a divorcee cannot prove anything since he is disqualified by the court<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As noted below, only the court can strip him of his role as Cohen, and only after regular judicial proceedings.</i>. They voted on the matter and declared him impure. Rebbi Ṭarphon said to Rebbi Aqiba, he who separates himself from you is as if he separated himself from his own life.”
Rebbi Yose said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Aqiba, accepted by all other rabbis, that the son of a divorcee is not automatically disqualified but only by court decree.</i> means that if he was standing sacrificing on the altar when it became known that he was the son of a divorcee or of a woman who had performed <i>ḥaliẓah</i>, his work remains valid until he is disqualified by a court<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">For the original question, it means that the High Priest is validly officiating; therefore his removal will allow all homicides convicted during his tenure to return home.</i>. That [also] means that if it was doubtful whether he was impure or had a bodily defect, his work is valid if you can compare him to the son of a divorcee [or] the son of a woman who had given <i>ḥaliẓah</i>. But if you have to compare him to a person with a bodily defect, his work is invalid. But is the case of the <i>miqweh</i> not that of a doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The entire argument started only because it was not known at which point in time the volume of the <i>miqweh</i> fell below 40 <i>seah</i>.</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina said, this means that any blemish on the family needs a court [judgment]<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If it was not known at his birth that he was the son of a divorcee, he remains a Cohen until disqualified by a court.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, where do they disagree? Concerning slave and wife. But in the other cases, even Rebbi Eliezer agrees. Rebbi Samuel ben Rebbi Isaac said, the Mishnah says so: “or if it became known that it was <i>ṭevel</i>, or First Tithe of which its heave had not been taken, or Second Tithe or dedicated [food] that had not been redeemed,” and Rebbi Eliezer does not disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, he will not disagree that a person disqualified as a Cohen has to spit out, including the son of a divorcee.</i>.
What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? Because he started with permission. It was stated: “Rebbi Nathan said, not that Rebbi Eliezer said because he started with permission but Rebbi Eliezer holds that chewed is like swallowed.” It is the same with the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody carries food in his mouth through the public domain on the Sabbath, he has desecrated the Sabbath. But if he chewed the food it is as if he swallowed it, it becomes part of his body and there is no desecration of the Sabbath. Since practice follows R. Joshua, these opinions are irrelevant for practice.</i>, Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">If one chews leavened matter without swallowing, it is a transgression of the commandment not to eat leavened matter on Passover. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 39b" href="/Pesachim.39b">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 39b</a>) only forbids chewing grain kernels as a rabbinic precaution.</i>, the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Chewing is breaking the fast.</i>, the <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If a <i>nazir</i> chews raisins, it is as if he had swallowed grapes.</i>, carcasses and torn animals<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A carcass is the body of a permitted animal which was not killed by cutting its throat. A torn animal is a permitted animal which has an exterior or interior defect which excludes the animal’s long term survival. Such an animal may not be eaten. For R. Eliezer, chewing forbidden meat is as bad as eating it.</i>, abominations<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Reptiles and amphibia.</i> and crawling things<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Insects and worms.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody slaughters an animal and finds in it an abomination, that is forbidden as food. What is the reason? An animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:3" href="/Leviticus.11.3"><i>Lev</i>. 11:3</a>) “inside an animal you may eat,” but an abomination inside an animal you may not eat.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">[Sifra <i>Šemini</i> 2(9), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 69a" href="/Chullin.69a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>69a</a>] The verse reads: “All that has hoofs, with clefts through the hoofs, and that chews the cud, <i>inside an animal</i>, such you may eat.” This is taken to mean that a fetus <i>inside an animal</i> may be eaten after the slaughter of the mother. {Sadducees did require separate slaughter of the fetus; MMT lines 37–38.} It is stated here that this applies only if the fetus itself is a permitted animal. The question remains open, what is meant by בהמה “animal”? A four-legged animal, a mammal, or a kosher mammal?</i>
Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Hoshaiah asked: What is the difference between this<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The abominations in a slaughtered animal are compared to worms in fruits and vegetables of which <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta 7:11</a> (quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 67b" href="/Chullin.67b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 67b</a>) declares that one is not guilty of a transgression if one eats them as long as they never left the fruit.</i> and worms in peas, squash bugs<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, יתוּשׁ is translated as “mosquito”, the meaning of the word in modern Hebrew. However, a mosquito cannot be in vetch, only its larva which is not called יתוּשׁ. The translation follows H. L. Fleischer, from the Arabic root ختش. The translation fits not only here, but also in the story of Titus (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 56b" href="/Gittin.56b"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 56b</a>) who is said to have died from an enormous יתוּשׁ growing in his head.</i> in sweet vetch<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Translation of Rashi (Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 67b), supported by <i>Halakhot Gedolot</i> (ed. Hildesheimer <i>Hilkhot Dagim</i>) who translates by Arabic באקלי “legumes; portulac, fava beans”. Maimonides: A kind of figs. I. Löw proposes <i>Prosopis Stephaniana</i>, also of the family of legumes.</i>, worms in dates and dried figs? There, they are in their bodies<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">They grew inside the fruit whereas the reptile found inside a slaughtered mammal must have come from the outside.</i>; here it is not in its body.
<i>Muries</i> sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Fish sauce, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.16"><i>Demai</i> Chapter 1, Note 156</a>. If the sauce is cloudy, it does not have to be checked for worms but if one can see through it, it is forbidden if it is seen to contain worms.</i>, if it is not clear, is permitted; if it is clear it is forbidden.
Blood on a loaf of bread one shaves off and eats [the bread]. If it came from between his teeth he eats and does not worry<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7">Tosephta 7</a>:11 (in the name of R. Joshua), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 21b" href="/Keritot.21b">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 21b</a>. In the Babli, blood from bleeding gums has to be sucked off (it may be swallowed), in the Tosephta it has to be wiped off. Blood on the bread (no longer liquid) is only rabbinically forbidden.</i>. The abominations among mites, flies, wild bees, abominations and crawling things<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The list is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:11" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.11">Tosephta 7:11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 67b" href="/Chullin.67b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 67b</a>. The derivation from the verse is only here; a different one in <i>Sifra Šemini</i> 12(1).</i>, I might think [that they are forbidden] while they are inside the fruit, the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:26" href="/Leviticus.11.26"><i>Lev</i>. 11:26</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:27" href="/Leviticus.11.27">27</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:28" href="/Leviticus.11.28">28</a>): “They are impure,” when they exist independently rather than inside the fruit. I might think, even if they left and returned; the verse says “they are impure,” even if they left and returned. Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: As soon as they reach the rim of the food they are forbidden even if they return<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">As soon as any part of a worm or insect developing inside a fruit is visible from the outside, it becomes forbidden. (In talmudic theory, worms and insects develop spontaneously, not from eggs, and, therefore, may be considered as part of the fruit.)</i>.
Abba bar Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who slaughters an animal and found in it a pig may eat it. Rebbi Jonah said, it is forbidden to eat, what is the reason? An animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:3" href="/Leviticus.11.3"><i>Lev</i>. 11:3</a>) “inside an animal you may eat.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">[Sifra <i>Šemini</i> 2(9), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 69a" href="/Chullin.69a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>69a</a>] The verse reads: “All that has hoofs, with clefts through the hoofs, and that chews the cud, <i>inside an animal</i>, such you may eat.” This is taken to mean that a fetus <i>inside an animal</i> may be eaten after the slaughter of the mother. {Sadducees did require separate slaughter of the fetus; MMT lines 37–38.} It is stated here that this applies only if the fetus itself is a permitted animal. The question remains open, what is meant by בהמה “animal”? A four-legged animal, a mammal, or a kosher mammal?</i>” You should not eat a bird inside an animal and not an abomination inside an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 69a" href="/Chullin.69a">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 69a</a>, R. Joḥanan is quoted forbidding a pigeon found inside a slaughtered animal. That statement must have fallen out here since the interpretation of the verse also refers to birds which were not mentioned beforehand. On the other hand, the mention of the pig must have fallen out in the Babli since that source points out that the verse permits only the consumption of ruminants with cloven hoofs found inside a slaughtered animal. It is therefore determined, according to R. Jonah, that בהמה means only “kosher animal”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.16">Note 58</a>.</i>.
Not only a bed bug, but everything a person is afraid of<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">One has to spit it out even if it is sanctified food if it tastes like a health hazard.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he was eating a bunch of grapes and entered from the garden into the courtyard. Rebbi Eliezer said, he should finish, but Rebbi Joshua said, he should not finish. If it got dark on Sabbath eve, Rebbi Eliezer said, he should finish, but Rebbi Joshua said, he should not finish<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Commentary of Maimonides: We have a principle that <i>ṭevel</i> becomes subject to tithes by biblical law only when the produce sees the face of the house as it was said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13</a>): “I removed the holy food from the house.” Any fruits eaten on the field or in the garden are not subject to tithes because that is a snack. Another principle is that the Sabbath brings the obligation of tithes; it is forbidden to eat even a snack from <i>ṭevel</i> on the Sabbath since the Eternal said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:13" href="/Isaiah.58.13"><i>Is</i>. 58:13</a>) “you should call the Sabbath a pleasure”. Therefore, all one has is prepared so that he may eat or drink from it on the Sabbath; this will be explained in Tractate <i>Ma‘serot</i> (Chapter 4). And when Rebbi Eliezer says “he should finish”, the meaning is that he should leave the courtyard again and finish. But in the courtyard he may not finish eating. And so they said on the Sabbath “he should finish”, <i>i. e.</i>, he should leave it alone until the end of the Sabbath day and only then finish. But on the Sabbath itself he is forbidden to eat it. And when R. Joshua said “he should not finish,” it means he should not continue to eat before he tithed.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:9. The entire Halakhah is reproduced, <i>mutatis mutandis</i>, in Halakhah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:9.</i>, we have stated: “If a vine is planted in the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If one harvests, the produce should be free of tithes and heave, and available for snacks, until it is brought to the house. But if it grows in the courtyard, it is immediately in front of the house. R. Ṭarphon permits taking an entire bunch, or an entire water melon, for a snack. Rebbi Aqiba allows only a few grapes or a small piece of watermelon for a snack and requires taking heave and tithing for everything else.</i>, one may take an entire bunch, and similarly for pomegranate and water melon, the words of Rebbi Ṭarphon.” Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Ṭarphon says following Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Ṭarphon says, one makes the cut<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ma‘serot</i>: עוקצין, “the stalk”, what remains if everything else is eaten.</i> of food equal to its start. Rebbi Illa, Rebbi Assi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Either Rebbi Ṭarphon follows Rebbi Eliezer or Rebbi Ṭarphon makes a meal consisting of two or three snacks equal to one snack<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since snacks can be eaten after harvest without tithing, he allows snacks as large as one wants.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Eliezer? Because he started with permission. It was stated: “Rebbi Nathan said, not that Rebbi Eliezer said because he started with permission but Rebbi Eliezer holds he should wait until the end of the Sabbath or he should leave the courtyard again and finish.”<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, R. Joshua and R. Aqiba are greater authorities than R. Eliezer and R. Ṭarphon and Maimonides is justified in deciding following R. Joshua. However, in the Kaufman ms. of the Mishnah and many mss. of the Maimonides tradition, the names of rabbis Eliezer and Joshua are switched in the last part of the Mishnah; but cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2, Note 33.</i>

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Heave wine that was left uncovered<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Left uncovered while nobody was in the room. It was assumed that snake poison in the drink was poisonous to humans. While it is sinful to destroy food, it is worse not to destroy food that is potentially poisonous.</i> should be poured out, certainly if it was profane. Three kinds of fluids are forbidden when they were uncovered, water, wine, and milk. All other fluids are permitted. How long should they be left unattended to become forbidden? So that the crawling creature<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is mostly to snakes, but included are any poisonous creatures which might put some poison into the fluid. For health reasons, none of the potentially dangerous fluids may be used for any purpose.</i> might come from a nearby place and drink.<br>The measure of uncovered water, until the bitterness be lost in them<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Water left uncovered is forbidden unless it is diluted so much that the poison will become inactive.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, in vessels any amount<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Water left uncovered in a vessel is forbidden in any amount.</i> and on the ground 40 <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 3, Note 164</a>.</i>.<br>Pecked-at figs, grapes, green melons, water melons, gourds, and sweet melons, even large as a pitcher, large or small, separated from or connected to the ground which contain moisture are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">If these fruits show signs that they have been pecked at by an animal, we are afraid that it might have been a poisonous animal and the poison diffused through the entire fruit through its moisture. If it was pecked at while completely dry, one could cut out the affected area and eat the remainder.<br>For the determination of the plants’ identity, see <i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 1.</i>. What was bitten by a snake is forbidden because of mortal danger<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">A kosher animal bitten by a snake may not be slaughtered or its meat eaten since it may contain snake poison.</i>.The wine filter is forbidden when left unattended but Rebbi Neḥemiah permits it<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">If a wine filter (used to filter out the dregs, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.19">Note 126</a> ff.) was left unattended and afterwards used to filter wine, that wine becomes forbidden (unless new filter material was used) because of poison from the filter. R. Neḥemiah holds that the poison is retained by the filter.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Wine left uncovered must be poured out, even if it is heave. A dish cooked with water that was left uncovered must be thrown out, even if it is heave and certainly if it is profane. Rebbi Yose said, our Mishnah says so: “Heave wine that was left uncovered should be poured out, and certainly profane wine.” The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is forbidden to keep wine that was left uncovered; if it was kept and it turned into vinegar by itself it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Poison in sour wine will not prevent it from turning into vinegar.</i>. It is forbidden to keep figs and grapes that were pecked at; if they were kept and dried up by themselves they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Poison in figs or grapes will make them rot. If they dry out (without being heated artificially), it is a sign that no poison is present (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:16" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.16">Tosephta 7:16</a>).</i>.
[It happened that] the daughter of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya punched holes into figs so that the fellows should not eat them but (his) [her] father Rebbi Ḥiyya ate from them. Is that not forbidden? He knew of the matter. Is it not forbidden anyhow, have we not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta 7:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 9" href="/Avodah_Zarah.9">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 9a/b</a>. The Babli states that the rules for health hazards must be much stricter than those for religious obligations; cf. Halakhah 7.</i>: “If somebody saw a bird pecking at a fig or a rat nibbling at a watermelon, it is forbidden for I say, they ate from the place of a pecking hole.” She had been watching.
<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs also are in <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:3 (fol. 41a).</i> Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The hot, the bitter, and the sweet are not under the rules for being uncovered. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The hot, the bitter, and the sweet are not under the rules for being uncovered or under those of libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If left standing uncovered and unsupervised, they still may be consumed. If left in the hands of a Gentile, they do not become prohibited; the Gentile will not use them for libations.</i>. Rebbi Simeon explains: The hot, spice wine. And the bitter, vermouth wine. And the sweet, cooked wine<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, there are two explanations. 1. Hot, hot <i>tilia</i> (linden tree) wine. Bitter, wine from blighted grapes. Sweet, sweetened wine. 2. Hot, pepper wine. Bitter, vermouth wine. Sweet, sweetened wine (according to <i>Arukh</i>,<i>carenum</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.7">Note 94</a>)</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Zeidal<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">A third generation Amora whose name appears only here and in the parallel in <i>Avodah Zarah</i>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30a">Babli (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 30a</a>), he appears as R. Ismael ben Zeirod. In the Rome ms., his name is R. Joshua ben Gidul.</i> had cooked wine which was left unsupervised in the possession of a Gentile. He asked Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael who said to him, so says Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The sweet is not under the rules for being uncovered or under those of libation wine. Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael fell ill. Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Hoshaiah, Rebbi Abun bar Cahana, and Rebbi Ḥananiah the colleague of the rabbis went to visit him. They saw that Rebbi Joshua ben Zeidal was sitting [there]. They said, there is the master of the decision and the person of the happening; they went and asked him. Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael said to them, so says Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, the sweet is not under the rules for being uncovered or under those of libation wine. Rebbi Zeïra said to him, maybe that statement of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is for argument’s sake? He told him no, it is for action and you can rely on me<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30a">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 30a</a>.</i>. When they came back, there was Rebbi Hila standing with Rebbi Abun bar Cahana. He told him, if you did not prefer decisions, is it not a <i>baraita</i>? Since Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 29b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.29b">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 29b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.5.6">Tosephta <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 4:12</a>, the text reads: “[Gentile] cooked wine and <i>olentia</i> are forbidden because they originally were wine.”</i>: “Why is cooked wine of Gentiles forbidden? Because it started out as wine. Why is vinegar of Gentiles forbidden? Because it started out as wine.” Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says so: “wine<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">“Wine which originally was wine” is cooked wine which is thickened and lost its alcohol in cooking.</i> and vinegar of Gentiles which originally was wine.”
Rebbi Immi had guests. He said to them, if my cooked wine had not been left uncovered, I would have served you some. Rebbi Vivian told him, bring it and I shall drink. He said, he who wants to die should go and die in his own house.
The spiced wine of Bar Yudani was left uncovered. He went and asked the rabbis who told him, it is forbidden. But did not Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The hot, the bitter, and the sweet are not under the rules for being uncovered! The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Titus<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">He is mentioned only here and in the parallel in <i>Avodah Zarah</i>.</i>: One which consists of one third ground spices<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Spice wine is immune to exposure only if the spices make up one third of the volume.</i>.
They asked before Rebbi Abbahu: What [is the status of] cooked wine that was left uncovered? He said to them, Rebbi Joḥanan had a problem with <i>carenum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Wine that lost one third of its volume by cooking, Greek κάροινον. {The word may originally be Accadic, <i>karānu</i> “wine”, <i>kurūnu</i> “date liquor.”} It follows that “cooked wine” did not yet come to the state of <i>carenum</i>. R. Joḥanan could not decide whether the rules of uncovered fluid apply to <i>carenum</i> or not. Therefore, wine which lost less than one third of its volume in cooking certainly is forbidden.</i>, and you are asking me? Is that not [applicable to] cooked wine? He went to ask Rebbi Isaac who told him, it is forbidden. Rebbi Abbahu [then] remembered that Rebbi Joḥanan had said, it is forbidden.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ammi, Rebbi Eleazar: If he was entering and leaving it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This is the general rule for the prohibition of wine touched by a Gentile who might have used the wine for a pagan libation. The Gentile would not use wine that was not his if he had to expect the Jewish owner or one of his Jewish employees at any moment. That rule is extended here to wine left uncovered; one does not expect a snake to venture out of its hole if there is continuous traffic in the room.</i>. The barrel of Bar Neṭoza became uncovered. He went and asked Rebbi Abba bar Mamai, who told him, it is permitted. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ammi, Rebbi Eleazar: If he was sleeping it is permitted. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, one said, if he was sleeping it is permitted; the other said, it is forbidden. We do not know who said what. It is reasonable [to infer that] Rebbi Ḥanina said, if he was sleeping it is permitted, since Rebbi Eleazar everywhere relies on Rebbi Ḥanina.
Rebbi Yose ben Shaul understands it from this occurrence: A woman loved very much doing good deeds<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">“Good deeds” without specific identification are deeds of charity.</i>. One time, a poor man came to her and she served him food. While he was eating, she noticed that her husband was coming. She put him [the poor] on the upper floor<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Not to be seen alone with another man. In a parallel story in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 91b" href="/Nedarim.91b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 91b</a> it was not a poor man but the woman’s lover.</i>. She put food before her husband who ate, took a nap, and slept. A snake came and ate from what was before him; he [the poor] saw it. When he awoke and got up he wanted to continue eating what was before him. The one on the upper floor started to talk to him. That means, if he was sleeping, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Since without the warning, the husband would have eaten the contaminated food.</i>. It was familiar with it<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The snake was used to the dwellers in the house, otherwise it would not have ventured near a sleeping person. Therefore, food near a sleeping person is permitted except for houses with a house snake. (The text in the Rome ms., the Leyden text <i>Avodah Zarah</i>, and the quote in <i>Arukh</i> read כריך “used to” instead of בריר.)</i>. Is she not forbidden for being alone [with another man?]<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since a married Jewish woman is not supposed to be alone in a house with another man, could the husband not divorce her for suspicion of adultery without paying her the <i>ketubah</i> sum? The rules for this are detailed in the last chapter of <i>Qiddušin</i>.</i> Since he is not suspected in one thing, he is not suspected in the other, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 23:37" href="/Ezekiel.23.37"><i>Ez</i>. 23:37</a>) “For they committed adultery, blood is on their hands.”<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">An adulterer would gladly have let the husband die. Since the person upstairs did not let the husband die, he was not an adulterer and the wife is cleared. The Babli concurs (Note 97).</i>
It happened that a butcher in Sepphoris sold to Jews carcasses and torn meats. Once he drank wine Friday night, went to the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In the dark. In <i>Avodah Zarah</i>: He got drunk on Yom Kippur Eve.</i>, fell down, and died. The dogs were licking his blood. People came and asked Rebbi Ḥanina, should we lift him from before them<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Presumably he was lying in the street where the removal of a corpse on the Sabbath needs detailed rabbinic instruction on how to proceed without a violation of the Sabbath laws. Inside the courtyard it would not have been necessary to ask.</i>? He said to them, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a>): “Torn meat on the fields you shall not eat, you must throw it to the dogs!” This one robbed the dogs and fed it to Jews. He said to them, let them, they are eating from their own!
A pious person happened to be insistent<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This word is not the usual “to make fun of”, but a direct derivative of Arabic لجّ “to be insistent”, also لجلج “to repeat words in speaking”; cf. H. L. Fleischer’s notes to Levy’s Dictionary, <i>s. v.</i> לגלג.</i> in [the matter of] uncovered wine. Once he was smitten with a fever. They saw him sitting and giving a sermon on the Day of Atonement with a flask of water in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">For a medical necessity, he could take sips of less than a mouthful of water to combat his fever on the Day of Atonement. He would not let the flask out of his hands for fear it could stand unsupervised.</i>.
A person’s amphora of wine became uncovered. He went on the eve of the Great Fast to pour it out. Another person saw him and said, give it to me to drink. He said to him, but was it not left uncovered? He said to him, give it to me, the Master of the Fast will support [me]. He did not finish drinking when he started shaking.
Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: All poisons cause scab but the poison of a snake kills. <sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">A sentence is missing here which is preserved in <i>Avodah Zarah</i>: ר׳ חייה אמר אין נשאלין על הגילוין “Rebbi Ḥiyya said, one is not asked about uncovered [fluids].” From the next sentence, one must assume that R. Jeremiah transmitted the statement in the name of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba.<br>The meaning of the statement is that in case of possible danger to life, as expressed in the preceding sentence, no logical or rabbinic arguments are admitted and everybody is supposed to exercise the utmost care which means that in matters of doubt, the fluids cannot be used. Therefore, no questions about this can be asked from any rabbi.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah asked Rebbi Zeïra. As the master of the ruling, could he have asked?
Rebbi Zeïra was dozing; he was sitting and eating (and was sleeping) [in the evening]. He put his hand on a toman<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A vessel containing one eighth of a <i>kab</i> of wine. Since it got dark, he held that being in the room with the vessel was not sufficient.</i> and said, kindle a light. They lit a light and found a snakelet like a hair curled around it. He said to it: Evil one, was I not careful about you?
Rebbi Ammi said, one has to be careful with what the creatures<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">People.</i> are careful. It is forbidden to put a coin into the mouth, or a dish under the bed, a bread in the arm pit, a knife in the middle of a radish, a knife into an <i>etrog</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, all sweat that comes from a human is poison except the sweat of the face.
When they asked Rebbi Jonathan, he said: Am I the guarantor of your life<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">These sayings also point out how stupid it is to consult rabbinic authorities in health matters when the alternatives are a very minor monetary loss against loss of one’s life.</i>? Rebbi Yannai said, if you bit something off, you bit off soot. If you lose, you lose a pearl. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if you would sell yourself for the games<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>ludi</i>. He would sell himself as a gladiator (as R. Simeon ben Laqish had done according to Babylonian tradition, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 47a" href="/Gittin.47a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 47a</a>).</i>, you would sell yourself at a high price; but here at a very cheap price.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">A shorter version is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:14" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.14">Tosephta 7:14</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30b">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 30b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 115b" href="/Bava_Kamma.115b"><i>Baba</i> <i>Qama</i> 115b</a>. From here on, the text is missing in Yerushalmi <i>Avodah Zarah</i>.</i>: “With water that was left uncovered one should not scrub his house; one should not pour it into the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The person who creates a danger in the public domain becomes liable for all consequences (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33" href="/Exodus.21.33"><i>Ex</i>. 21:33</a>).</i>, or give it to drink to a Gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Gentiles do not worry about water left uncovered and the Gentile would drink it on his own, the Jew may not give him anything he knows is dangerous.</i>, neither should one use it to water another person’s animals but one may use it to water his own animals<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since the animal will not die from the poison but it will be weakened. The Babli also forbids watering his own animal.</i>. With water that was left uncovered one should not knead clay, or wash clothes, or soak pots, glasses, and food baskets<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Peak</i> Chapter 8, Note 83.</i>, and certainly not wash his face, hands, and feet. Others say, they forbade only the place of a scratch. His face is always like a scratch. The tips of his fingers and toes are always like a scratch. With water that was left uncovered one should not knead dough. Rebbi Neḥemiah said, baking is permitted because snake poison is destroyed by fire.” Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: That is, if it does not collapse<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">If the dough collapses in baking or the bread when cooling.</i>, but if it collapsed it is forbidden.
Everybody agrees that water which was heated is forbidden. What is the difference between bread and water? Here the fire rules<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The nature of bread dough is changed by heating.</i>, there the fire does not rule. There, a vessel separates, here no vessel separates<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Bread hangs in the <i>tannur</i> directly over the fire.</i>. Samuel said, what can this evil one do to me? I only drink hot [water]. His word says, cold water that was heated is permitted. Rebbi Ḥanina said, this evil one has a delicate taste; he will not drink hot water that cooled. His word says, hot water that cooled is permitted. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Both are forbidden. A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: <sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:13" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.13">Tosephta 7:13</a>, in different formulation and order.</i>“Hot water is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Even if left uncovered and unsupervised.</i> as long as it is steaming. But water left uncovered is forbidden even after it was heated. Pickling, preserving<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Preserving by cooking in water a very long time.</i>, and lupine<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Lupines become edible only after cooking.</i> water is permitted. Water in which he soaked[food for] pickling or preserving, or lupines, is forbidden. If he soaked wild grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Saul Lieberman, reading ענבים אוּבשים, fruits of the wild vine used in medicine (cf. Plinius, <i>Hist. nat.</i> Bk. 23, Chap. 14). The water will absorb the taste of these grapes; nevertheless, it remains forbidden if left uncovered.</i> for a sick person, it is forbidden.”
“<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 115b" href="/Bava_Kamma.115b">Babli <i>Bava Qama</i> 115b</a>. There (and in the Rome ms.) the opinion ascribed to R. Jehudah here is reported to be R. Neḥemiah’s.</i> The wine filter, Rebbi Neḥemiah says, if it is blocked on top even though it is open below. Rebbi Jehudah says, if it was covered below even if the top is uncovered it is permitted since snake poison is like netting<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, “like a sponge”. We do not know what was the filtering material used to remove the dregs from the wine.</i> and remains on top.” Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Simon in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i> says, if he did not stir it. But if he did stir, it is forbidden.
A flask in its casing is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:16" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.16">Tosephta (7:16</a>): “Forbidden.” For the Yerushalmi, the casing (θήκη) keeps the snake out; the Tosephta worries that a miniature snake was in the casing but not noticed.</i>; covered but not stoppered it is forbidden. Stoppered but not covered, if the stopper appeared on the outside it is permitted. Two Amoraïm, one said if it can be taken up by its stopper<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The cork or other material is so tightly pushed in that the flask may be lifted by it.</i>, and the other said, so that the head of the weaver’s shuttle could not enter<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Between the cork and the wall of the vessel.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, he who wants to do it right, stoppers and covers it.
A forgotten flask deposited in a litter, a chest, or a chassis<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">All three are of wood and used for transportation.</i> that later was found is forbidden. If he checked and afterwards put it in it is permitted. A case came before Rebbi, where it<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The snake was seen lifting the cover, drinking, and replacing the cover. This is considered aberrant behavior by the snake.</i> was seen uncovering and covering. He said, because of that evil one should we invalidate all covers? Rebbi Mana said, it is reasonable that in that case itself<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">If the snake was seen drinking.</i> was forbidden.
<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraph are also in <i>Šabbat</i> 1:4, fol. 3a, and, in slightly less complete form, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:9, fol. 41d; the discussion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 35b-36a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.35b-36a">Babli is <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 35b–36a</a>.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah said, why is Gentile’s milk forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">“Milk milked by a Gentile without a Jew seeing him” is forbidden for consumption but not for use; Mishnah <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:9.</i>? Because of admixture from impure animals. As we have stated: “If the Gentile stands and milks the herd, if a Jew stands nearby there is no worry.” Rav Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Why is Gentiles’ milk forbidden? Because of the uncovering<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since Gentiles do not care about uncovered milk and snake poison.</i>. Let him make cheese? Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, because of the poison between the holes<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, uncovered milk is not discussed. There, it is held that impure milk (horse or donkey milk) cannot be made into cheese but a mixture may produce cheese with the impure milk remaining in the holes of the cured cheeses.</i>.
There are three kinds of poison. Poison which sinks down, poison which floats on top, and poison remaining on the surface like a net<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30b">Babli, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 30b</a>, it is stated that the poison of young snakes sinks down (is heavier than water), that of middle-aged snakes is suspended, and that of old snakes stays on top (is lighter than water). There is no indication that the Yerushalmi subscribes to this theory. The “poison remaining like a net” seems to be colloidal.</i>. In the days of Rebbi Jeremiah, the barrels of the great assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The yearly or half-yearly assembly of all rabbinic scholars.</i> were uncovered. The first ones drank and did not die but the last ones died. I say that was sinking poison. Workers were on the field when the water pitcher was uncovered. The first ones drank from it and did not die but the last ones died. I say that was sinking poison. We have stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta 7:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 30b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.30b">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 30b</a>, in different formulation.</i>: An uncovered amphora or a water melon with pecking marks, even if the first ones ate or drank [from it], the later ones should not eat or drink; I say that there is sinking poison. Uncovered wine from which ten persons drank is forbidden for others to (eat or) drink after them; I say that there is sinking poison.
“Five fluids are not endangered by being uncovered: Fish oil, <i>muries</i><sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.16"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 1, Note 156</a>.</i>, vinegar, olive oil, and honey. Rebbi Simeon forbids honey<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:12" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.12">Tosephta 7:12</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 49b" href="/Chullin.49b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 49b</a>. In both sources, R. Simeon forbids all five fluids.</i>. The Sages agree with Rebbi Simeon if it was seen pecking<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">They will agree that snakes sometimes act in a perverted way but do not agree that this is enough for a general prohibition (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. 144).</i>.” Rebbi Ḥinena bar Pappos had an uncovered flask of honey in his hand. Before he could ask, it broke apart<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">The next paragraph will show that this was a miracle to save his life.</i>.
Rav and Samuel both say, the earth is permitted to be broken apart before it<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">A snake may tunnel under the earth. The comparison with a vessel suggests the theory that snake poison can dissolve earth but not fired clay and metals.</i>, but no vessel is permitted to be broken apart before it. Rebbi Ḥiyya the Great and Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi were sitting in a certain house<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Their houses usually had dirt floors. Houses with paved (mosaic) floors usually are called “palaces”.</i> when murmurs went around. They said, is it possible that it will be seen here? It was seen. They said, praised be He Who chose the Sages who said, the earth is permitted to be broken apart before it, but no vessel is permitted to be broken apart before it<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav was a student of his uncle R. Ḥiyya, Rav and Samuel cannot have been the authors of the theory but they may have given the final formulation.</i>.
Rebbi Yannai feared it so much that he put his bed on four beams [standing] in water. Once he stretched out his hand and found it near himself. He said, take it away from me, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116:6" href="/Psalms.116.6"><i>Ps</i>. 116:6</a>) “the Eternal watches over the simple-minded.”
What is the measure of a hole<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">When is a hole in a wall a potential hiding place of a snake so that one has to worry about fluids left uncovered? <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:16" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.16">Tosephta 7:16</a>.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina showed it thus: he said, the tip of my small finger. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, so that the tip of the small finger of a newborn cannot enter it. Even if [the hole] were smaller, it [the snake] could enter it but since it does not know how to leave again, it does not enter. A woven basket even if very tight is forbidden for it can enter at one place and leave at another. A knapsack<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this word is everybody’s guess. The meaning “knapsack” is R. M. Margalit’s guess, identified by J. Levy as Arabic شلَاق <i>šallāq</i> “beggar’s knapsack”. R. Eliahu Fulda writes only “some vessel”. Kohout’s suggestion to refer the word to Arabic صليقة <i>ṣalīqa</i> “slice of roast beef” has to be rejected.<br>The word may be Greek σάλαξ, -ακος, ὁ “miner’s sieve, coarse sieve, screen”; cf. text at <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:3:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.3.19">Note 126</a> (E. G.).</i> is forbidden even if it hangs very high since it will let itself down<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The snake will climb to the ceiling and lower itself to the knapsack/sieve.</i>.”
What is “close by<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The distance mentioned in the Mishnah.</i>”? Rebbi Samuel said, from the handles of an amphora to its mouth. Would it not be seen<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">How can a snake hide under the handle of an amphora?</i>? Rebbi Ḥanina said, it is a small kind called <i>šefifōn</i> and looks like a hair<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. text after note 108.</i>.
It was stated: “<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:14-15" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.14-15">Tosephta 7:14–15</a>; in different order and slightly different language.</i> Water on the ground forty <i>seah</i>. Rebbi Neḥemiah says, that a <i>Šiḥin</i> amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">A standard measure which is not determined in any parallel source. In the Tosephta there is an additional opinion, “others say, two <i>seah</i>.”</i> can be filled from it. Wine both in vessels and on the ground in any quantity. Wine is not under the rules of uncovering as long as it is fermenting. How long does it ferment? Three days. It happened that a snake was found near a wine vat; the case came before Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, R. Jehudah ben Baba.</i> and he permitted it. A source is not under the rules of uncovering as long as it is running. Rebbi Ismael the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, it happened that Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa went down to my father Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri to Bet Serii; he showed him a pool that contained only three <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.4.4"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 3, Note 164</a>.</i> of water, into which rain water was dripping. He bowed down and drank, saying this is an example of what is not under the rules of uncovering<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the pool is not stagnant but overflowing from the rain water, it is counted as a source or river and one may assume that all poison was washed away (explanation of R. S. Lieberman).</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The first two sentences, dealing with the problem of R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa, how long is a puddle considered a brook, is also in <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i>1:1, fol. 80b.</i> How long? Rebbi Mana bar Tanḥum<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i>, Ḥiyya bar Abun in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i> said, until the soap-wort blossoms. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The same name appears in <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i>; there he speaks in the name of R. Tanḥum ben R. Ḥiyya, an Amora of the third generation. In any case, R. Eleazar ben R. Yose cannot be the Tanna, son of R. Yose ben Ḥalaphta, who is the author in all other cases where the name is mentioned.</i> in the name of Rav: Until they<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">The soap-wort plants.</i> become like goose feet. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, when it became permitted on the field, it became permitted in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">If it rains so much that puddles on the field get the status of brooks, snakes hibernate and are no danger in the house. R. Hoshaiah disagrees and declares the danger to exist everywhere, at all times.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaiah stated: [The rules of] uncovering apply in the Land and outside the Land, in summer and in winter.
Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, who is the Tanna of pecking? “Rabban Gamliel says, also the snake because it spits out<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 9:3" href="/Mishnah_Parah.9.3">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 9:3</a>. The Mishnah states that water drawn for the purification rite of the ashes of the red cow becomes unusable if any animal drank from it because some of the animal’s spittle will be mixed with the water. In general, reptiles are reputed not to invalidate the water. Rabban Gamliel declares the snake to invalidate the water because it always will deposit some of its poison. Since the opinion of Rabban Gamliel is a minority opinion not followed in practice, R. Jehudah bar Pazi implies that the Mishnah here does not represent practice.</i>.” Rebbi Jonah said, who would turn commandments into peckings for us<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The rules given here are rules of hygiene, rather than religious observance. Therefore, the reference to Rabban Gamliel is irrelevant and the Mishnah here does represent practice.</i>?
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta 7:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 9" href="/Avodah_Zarah.9">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 9a/b</a>. The Babli states that the rules for health hazards must be much stricter than those for religious obligations; cf. Halakhah 7.</i>: “If somebody saw a bird pecking at a fig or a rat nibbling at a watermelon, it is forbidden [to eat from them] for I say, they might have eaten from the place of a pecking hole.” Rebbi Abba said, that means that a snake spits out repeatedly. Rebbi Jonah said, who considers for us commandments for peckings<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The rules given here are rules of hygiene, rather than religious observance. Therefore, the reference to Rabban Gamliel is irrelevant and the Mishnah here does represent practice.</i>? But for torn [animals]<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">A kosher animal, correctly slaughtered, can be eaten only if inspection shows that it was healthy. The quote here is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.3.7"><i>Berakhot</i> 9:4, Notes 209</a> ff.</i>, Rebbi Abba said in the name of the rabbis from there, “if it was slaughtered and the wolves took its innards, it is in order because intestines are presumed to be in order.” Should one not question whether they were pierced? Intestines are presumed to be in order. But here, you say so? It is worse if there is danger to life<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle; any logical arguments and rules of decision based on probabilities and prior assumptions are inapplicable in health matters.</i>; for peckings they went all the way with Rabban Gamliel.
It was stated: Rebbi Eliezer said, one may eat figs and grapes in the night without worry, since it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116:6" href="/Psalms.116.6"><i>Ps</i>. 116:6</a>) “the Eternal watches over the simple-minded.” Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rav: A fish with pecking marks is forbidden. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Tabi in the name of Rav: A living fish with pecking marks is forbidden. If it is salted, through the salt it trembles<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">It is visible whether the salted fish is infected or not.</i>. Dead<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">If it is clear that some animal pecked at the fish after it died.</i>, one cuts out the place of the pecking.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: A watermelon with pecking marks whose inside is rotten is forbidden. A person carried a sweet melon with pecking marks. He let ten people eat from it and they died. He covered it with spittle and it broke apart.
A person hated sheep’s meat. Once he was eating red meat when a passer-by said, this is sheep’s meat. He choked<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">According to Z. Ben-Ḥayyim, <i>The Literary and Oral Traditions of Hebrew and Aramaic Amongst the Samaritans</i>, Jerusalem 1961, vol. II, p. 442, Galilean כלעס is the equivalent of Samaritan כלעט, “to choke”. This invalidates the opinion of the dictionaries which see in כלעס a scribal error for Syriac בלעס, assumed to be derived from בלע “to swallow”.</i> and died. The donkey drivers stayed at an inn. They said, bring us lentils; they brought. They said, give us a second helping; they brought. They said to them, the first ones were better, what were those? They said, we found the spine of a snake in them; they choked and died.
It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 16:6" href="/Proverbs.16.6"><i>Prov</i>. 16:6</a>): “When the Eternal likes the conduct of a person, even his enemy will make peace with him.” Rebbi Meïr says, that is the dog. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi says, that is the snake. The shepherds milked; there came a snake, ate from it, but the dog observed it. When they came to eat, he started barking at them but they did not pay attention. At the end, he ate and died. A person made ground garlic in his house. There came a mountain snake and ate from it but the house snake observed it. When the people from the house came to eat from it, it [the house snake] started to throw dust down on them<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Another version of the same story is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 54:1" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.54.1"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 54(1</a>). In that source, all authors are late Amoraïm; the snake is identified as the enemy by R. Samuel.</i>. When they did not pay attention, it threw itself on it. A person invited a rabbi and let the dog sit next to him [the rabbi]. He said, how did I merit this insult? He said, rabbi, I am repaying his [the dog’s] good deed. Kidknappers came to town. One of them came and wanted to take my wife but he [the dog] ate his testicles.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An amphora of heave about which a doubt of impurity arose<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">If it were certainly impure, heave wine could be used to settle dust on dirt floors, heave oil or grain as fuel. If there only is a doubt, the heave is called “suspended”. It cannot be used as impure since it might be pure, nor as pure since it might be impure. R. Eliezer holds that one has to treat this heave according to all rules of holiness. R. Joshua holds that one has to make sure that the heave be used somehow.
