Since it never will be usable as pure heave, one has to make sure it will be usable impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bekhorot 33b" href="/Rashi_on_Bekhorot.33b">Rashi in <i>Bekhorot</i> 33b</a>).</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, if it was at a vulnerable place, he should move it to a hidden place. If it was uncovered, he should cover it. Rebbi Joshua said, if it was at a hidden place, he should move it to a vulnerable place. If it was covered, he should uncover it. Rabban Gamliel said, he should not change anything.<br>If an amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">The amphora contains heave wine which will become useless if impure.</i> broke in the upper part of the wine press whose lower part was impure, Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua agree that if one can save a <i>revi‘it</i> from it, he shall save that in purity<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">If a reasonable amount can be saved in a pure vessel, R. Eliezer agrees that one should save as much as possible even if by this action the remainder will become impure much more rapidly than if it were left alone.</i>. Otherwise, Rebbi Eliezer said, let it descend and become impure; he should not make it impure with his hands.<br>And so if an amphora of oil broke<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Even though impure oil is used as fuel, one still should save as much as possible from impurity.</i>, Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua agree that if one can save a <i>revi‘it</i> from it, he shall save that in purity. Otherwise, Rebbi Eliezer said, let it descend and be absorbed [in the ground]; he should not cause it to be absorbed by his hands’ [action].<br>About both of these cases, Rebbi Joshua said, this is not heave for which I have been warned not to make it impure; only not to eat it and not to [intentionally] make it impure<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to make heave impure by an independent action. But one is not required to avoid impurity if the wine or oil are going to be spilled without human intervention; in that case one may cause impurity to most of the heave if thereby one saves a small part in purity.</i>.<br>If somebody was on the road<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Alone, with no help in sight.</i> with loaves of heave and a Gentile said to him, give me one of them that I may defile it, otherwise I shall defile all of them, Rebbi Eliezer says, he shall defile all of them but one should not hand over any to him. Rebbi Joshua said, he should put it on a rock before him<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">On a rock, not in the dirt, to preserve the loaf as food. R. Joshua agrees that heave cannot be handed over to be made impure but an effort should be made within the letter of the law to preserve as much as possible in purity.</i>. Similarly, women to whom Gentiles said, give us one of you that we may defile her<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">Rape her.</i>, otherwise we shall defile all of you, they should defile all of them but no Jewish person should be handed over<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is agreed to by everybody.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, from the opinions of all three of them: It is forbidden to burn suspended [heave].
<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Most of this Halakhah is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.7.2-4"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:7</a>; a parallel treatment is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 14a-21a" href="/Pesachim.14a-21a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 14a–21a</a>. In the Mishnah, R. Ḥanina the Second of the Cohanim (who had to substitute when the High Priest was disabled) and R. Aqiba, who both had seen the Temple, report that the Cohanim never refrained from burning meat impure in a secondary way together with meat of original impurity, or oil becoming unusable by tertiary impurity together with impure oil of primary impurity, even though a momentary increase in impurity was inevitable. The late Tanna R. Meïr wants to infer that on Passover eve, when all leavened matter must be burned, one may burn leavened pure and impure heave together. His contemporary R. Yose (ben Ḥalaphta) objects and notes that both R. Eliezer and R. Joshua require that these heaves be burned separately. Their disagreement is that R. Eliezer requires suspended and impure heaves to be burned separately but R. Joshua permits them to be burned together.</i> The colleagues in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The first amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">The amphora of Mishnah 7, where R. Joshua only permits to put it in danger’s way but not directly to make it impure, gives R. Yose’s interpretation of the position of R. Joshua.</i> following Rebbi Yose, the second<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The amphoras of Mishnaiot 8–10, where R. Joshua permits causing impurity if this helps a good cause.</i> following Rebbi Meïr. The colleagues say, the first amphora following Rebbi Yose but Rebbi Meïr will not agree; the second following Rebbi Meïr but Rebbi Yose will not agree. Rebbi Yose told them, be careful what you teach. The first amphora following Rebbi Yose; therefore, following Rebbi Meïr one burns<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">One burns pure and impure heave together on the 14th of Nisan. The position of R. Simeon has to be proven since he does not appear in the Mishnah.</i> and following Rebbi Simeon one burns. Then Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Simeon should form a majority against Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Even though singly their opinions would be disregarded when in opposition to R. Yose.</i> and one should burn. In addition, we see that the rabbis, in cases which come before them, say, go and consider it suspended. We find that Rebbi Simeon says one burns, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.2-8.4">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:7</a>, speaking of pure and impure heave. If this is the teaching of R. Simeon, he must hold that they disagree for suspended and impure heave and, therefore, these can be burned together since practice always follows R. Joshua against R. Eliezer. But then suspended heave should be treated as impure, against Mishnah 7.</i>: “Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua agree that one burns each one separately.” On this, Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon taught that<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:5</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 20b" href="/Pesachim.20b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 20b</a>. The Tosephta is Babylonian, unknown to the Yerushalmi.</i>. And if you want to say that Rebbi Meïr does not recognize suspension<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Since most anonymous Mishnaiot are R. Meir’s formulation, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 4:5" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.4.5">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 4:5</a>, which directs burning only for six narrowly defined kinds of suspended heave, excludes most kinds of suspended heave from being treated as impure. Accordingly, R. Simeon’s is an isolated opinion, rather than practice to be followed.</i>, have we not stated: “Suspended heave and impure heave are burned on Sabbath eve when it gets dark, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say, at its appointed time<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:9" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.9">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:9</a>. Since on the Sabbath, heave may not be burned, it must be fed to animals to be disposed of.</i>.” Rebbi Zeïra said before Rebbi Mana<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana I, student of Rabbis Yannai, Joḥanan, and Assi.</i>, explain it if it is suspended because he intends to ask about it. He said to him, so said my teacher Rebbi Assi: All suspended [heave] we are dealing with here is suspended [heave] about which he has no intention of asking<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the heave can never be used again and may be treated as impure for practical purposes.</i>. But if he intends to ask about it, it has to be treated as pure. As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">A different formulation is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:18" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.18">Tosephta 7:18</a>: “I am keeping it; <i>until I</i> ask about the situation, it is <i>im</i> pure.” That formulation does not necessarily conflict with the one here. If the heave is left unusable for an indefinite period, chances are it will become impure. But if the question to a rabbinic authority is asked within a foreseeable time, there is no reason to declare the suspended heave impure.</i>: “If he said, it is pure or impure, then it is impure. But if he said, I am keeping it in order to ask about the situation, it is pure.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it if the doubt arose at sundown. Then you cannot infer anything<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no time to burn anything, the opinion of the Sages must be followed even by their opponents. This does not prove anything about the position of R. Meïr if the heave was already suspended in the morning.</i>.
You say, the first amphora following Rebbi Yose, and Rebbi Meїr will not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">While this is the text of R. Yose’s second argument in both mss. and the early prints, it is clear from the following that the discussion is about the second part of the statement of the colleagues, “the second amphora following Rebbi Meїr, and Rebbi Yose will not agree.” The discussion centers on Mishnaiot 8–9, where everybody agrees that in order to save some pure heave one may actively bring impurity on the remainder.</i>. But did we not state: “When has this been said? Regarding a vat which contains enough to lift it. But for a vat which does not contain enough to lift it, it is forbidden to make any amount impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text both here and in <i>Pesaḥim</i> but it is difficult to explain. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 21a" href="/Pesachim.21a">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 21a</a>) reads: “An amphora which broke in the upper part of the wine press while the lower part contains a hundred times as much impure (wine), Rebbi Eliezer admits to Rebbi Joshua that if he can save a <i>revi‘it</i> from it, he shall save that in purity; otherwise, let it descend and he should not make it impure with his hands.” The Mishnah does not explain what is in the lower part of the winepress. If there is enough profane wine to let one lift heave according to the rules of Chapter 5, not much is lost since the wine remains usable. But if heave cannot be lifted because the profane is not more than 100 times the heave all becomes forbidden and, at most, can be used as ointment or to sprinkle on a dirt floor to eliminate dust. This represents a considerable monetary loss.</i>” And if it follows Rebbi Meїr, whether it contains enough to lift it or does not contain enough to lift it, is it forbidden to make any amount impure<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Meїr permits to bring impurity to anything that later automatically would become impure.</i>? In addition, from what we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Pesachim 1:7" href="/Mishnah_Pesachim.1.7">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:7 (Note 184</a>) the discussion between R. Meїr and R. Yose is formulated not as a clash of traditions but of logical arguments, with R. Yose disputing R. Meїr’s inference but not his premise. The problem, as pointed out in the Babli, is that it is not clear whether the basis of the argument is the common statement of R. Ḥanina, the Second of the Cohanim, and R. Aqiba in the Mishnah there, or the partial agreement of R. Eliezer and R. Joshua in the Mishnah here. For the moment, we assume that R. Meїr and R. Yose refer to R. Eliezer and R. Joshua.</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, this is not the implication.” A person says “this is not the implication” only if he agrees with the premise. How is that? There, the Torah cares about the money of the Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 12:5. The difference between large and small losses is also noted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 20b" href="/Pesachim.20b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i>20b</a>.</i>, here what can you say<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the origin of the text is in <i>Pesaḥim</i>. “There” means <i>Terumot</i>, where potentially an entire harvest may be lost in the winepress. “Here” means <i>Pesaḥim</i>, where the question is only whether different kinds of leavened matter may be burned together or have to be burned separately.</i>? Does he not here also cayse him to lose money since he needs fire wood to burn each lot separately? They worried about a big loss, they did not worry about a small loss. Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, explain it following him who said, from the words of Rebbi Aqiba and the words of Rebbi Ḥananiah, the Second of the Cohanim<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.4.1">Note 184</a>. In their cases, no monetary loss is incurred and nothing can be inferred for our case.</i>.
Rebbi Ila and Rebbi Zeїra, both in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The first amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">The amphora of Mishnah 7, where R. Joshua only permits to put it in danger’s way but not directly to make it impure, gives R. Yose’s interpretation of the position of R. Joshua.</i> follows Rebbi Yose, the second<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The amphoras of Mishnaiot 8–10, where R. Joshua permits causing impurity if this helps a good cause.</i> both Rebbi Meїr and Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Zeїra asked before Rebbi Mana: Does it not disagree with Rebbi Meїr? He said to him, we may say it follows neither Rebbi Meїr nor Rebbi Yose since we find that Rebbi Meїr burns suspended [heave] in all cases<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that he treats suspended heave as impure and does not object to an increase in its impurity.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea and heard Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Zeїri: Rebbi Meїr burns suspended [heave] in all cases. I said to him, because he considers suspended [heave] from the words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">If there is a doubt of impurity in biblical law, the doubt has to be resolved in a restrictive manner and there is no reason not to treat suspended heave as impure.</i>. He said to me, do I not explain that it became impure in Gentiles’ dwellings? For we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Oholot 18:3" href="/Tosefta_Oholot.18.3">Tosephta <i>Ahilut</i> 18:7</a>. Since Gentiles bury their stillborn babies in their houses, Gentile dwellings in the Holy Land are potential sources of impurity of a tent (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.2.2"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 6, Note 25</a>). Any heave in such a permanent dwelling is therefore automatically suspended. However, since it is questionable whether impurity of the dead extends to Gentiles, that impurity is purely rabbinic.</i>: “From Gentiles’ dwellings one suspends; Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, one burns<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meїr is supposed to follow R. Yose ben R. Jehudah.</i>.” Rebbi Huna in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Rebbi Meїr burns suspended [heave] any day of the year, as we have stated: “Suspended heave and impure heave are burned on Sabbath eve when it gets dark, the words of Rebbi Meїr; but the Sages say, at its appointed time<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 3:9" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).3.9">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i> 3:9</a>. Since on the Sabbath, heave may not be burned, it must be fed to animals to be disposed of.</i>.” Should he not burn it during the morning hours<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">If Passover eve falls on a weekday, leavened matter must be eliminated before noon since the Passover sacrifice must be slaughtered in a leaven-free environment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:18" href="/Exodus.23.18"><i>Ex</i>. 23:18</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:25" href="/Exodus.34.25">34:25</a>). Therefore, even if Passover eve falls on a Sabbath, the elimination of leavened matter preferably should take place on Friday before noontime.</i>? Explain it if he was lazy and did not burn it [then]. Know that this is so, for he did not state “during the morning hours.” Is that not because he was lazy and did not burn [then]? Rebbi Abba Mari, the brother of Rebbi Yose said, explain it if impurity was created at that time, and you cannot infer anything<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the heave can never be used again and may be treated as impure for practical purposes.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Joshua and Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 5:2</a>, quoted in the next paragraph. While a firstling calf or lamb has to be treated as a sacrifice, R. Simeon permits any surgical operation if the health of the animal requires it, even if it is clear that by the operation the animal will become unfit as a sacrifice.</i> both said the same thing<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua permits to bring impurity to heave in order to save some part in purity; R. Simeon permits to make a blemish on a firstling calf in order to save its life.</i>. Rebbi Ilaї said: Rebbi Simeon in <i>Bekhorot</i> and Rebbi Joshua in <i>Terumot</i>, neither of them will agree with the other<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua permits to bring impurity to heave in order to save food. In the case of <i>Bekhorot</i>, the Mishnah states that one may not slaughter the firstling because of the man-induced blemish; the animal cannot become food for anybody. Therefore, it is not necessary that R. Joshua agree with R. Simeon. On the other hand, R. Simeon holds that pure and impure heave cannot be burned together; it is forbidden to make heave impure even if it must be burned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.4.6">Note 190</a>). Therefore, R. Simeon cannot be shown to agree with R. Joshua in <i>Terumot</i>.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Zeїra: Is it not reasonable that Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Joshua but Rebbi Joshua will not agree with Rebbi Simeon? Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:6:2-8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.6.2-8.4">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:7</a>, speaking of pure and impure heave. If this is the teaching of R. Simeon, he must hold that they disagree for suspended and impure heave and, therefore, these can be burned together since practice always follows R. Joshua against R. Eliezer. But then suspended heave should be treated as impure, against Mishnah 7.</i>: “Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua agree that each batch should be burned separately.<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:5</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 20b" href="/Pesachim.20b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 20b</a>. The Tosephta is Babylonian, unknown to the Yerushalmi.</i>” Could one not burn the two together<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Since the pure heave also must be destroyed by biblical decree.</i>? He said to him, there [the heave] is pure; by Torah law the heave is still in existence; you are the person who decided to burn it<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">By biblical decree, leavened matter is permitted on the 14th of Nisan until the time of the slaughter of the Passover lamb, which is shortly after noon (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.4.5">Note 210</a>). However, by rabbinic decree no leavened matter may be eaten two hours before noontime and all leavened matter must be eliminated at least one hour before noon. Therefore, at the time of burning, the heave would still be good heave according to biblical law for another hour and could not be considered as being lost automatically.</i>. In any case, would it not become unusable by being left unattended? Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, leaving unattended is from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">Heave must be eaten in purity, which can be guaranteed only if the heave is guarded at all times or at least kept at a place locked away from possible impurities (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.2"><i>Šeqalim</i> 7:2,</a> fol. 50c; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 34a" href="/Pesachim.34a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 34a</a>). Since on the 14th of Nisan, leavened heave cannot be eaten after 10 a.m. local time, there is no need to watch it any longer and, by being released from supervision, it should become invalid immediately even by biblical standards.</i>? The second amphora for Rebbi Meїr follows the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">As just shown, the lack of attention makes everything impure for practical purposes.</i>, coughing blood for Rebbi Simeon follows the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">In the next paragraph, R. Simeon’s interpretation of the biblical text is given.</i>! He said to him, one still watches it so it should not come in contact with other pure [heave]<sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the argument of Note 221 is inapplicable in our situation.</i>. Rebbi Isaac, the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya the scribe: Think about it, if it was put on coals<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">If one starts the fire to burn the heave, guarding against impurity certainly is unnecessary. The answer is that this argument is irrelevant since we deal with the moment before the fire is started.</i>? He said to him, after if was put there. Rebbi Mana said to Rebbi Shammai: You who say that Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Joshua! Even Rebbi Joshua does not agree with Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">The positions of R. Joshua in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:7:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.7.2-4"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:7</a> and <i>Terumot</i> 8:8–10 do not necessarily coincide, as explained by R. Ilaï.</i>! He said to him, these are Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to fully reconstruct the original position of R. Joshua since the only knowledge we have of his statements is through the interpretations of the students of R. Aqiba.</i>. There, Rebbi Meїr in the name of Rebbi Joshua, but here Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">This shows that the origin of the text is in <i>Pesaḥim</i>. “There” means <i>Terumot</i>, where potentially an entire harvest may be lost in the winepress. “Here” means <i>Pesaḥim</i>, where the question is only whether different kinds of leavened matter may be burned together or have to be burned separately.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 5:2" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.5.2">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 5:2</a>. Since a firstling is a sacrifice by birth, it has to be treated according to the rules of sacrifices. The only difference is that a dedicated sacrifice which develops a blemish must be redeemed to become profane whereas a firstling in the hands of a Cohen which develops a blemish automatically becomes quasi profane and may be eaten by everybody and in impurity. Therefore, Cohanim are suspected to induce blemishes on the firstlings in their possession.</i>, we stated: “A firstling afflicted by blood, even if it is going to die, cannot be bled, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. But the Sages say, it should be bled even if this causes a blemish. If it did cause a blemish, it should not be slaughtered because of it. Rebbi Simeon says, he should bleed it even if he makes a blemish.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Rebbi Jehudah parallels Rabban Gamliel, the Rabbis parallel Rebbi Eliezer, Rebbi Simeon parallels Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah 7 (the “first amphora”). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 35b" href="/Bekhorot.35b">Babli (<i>Bekhorot</i> 35b</a>) identifies the position of the Sages with that of Rebbi Joshua (i. e., the operative opinion in <i>Bekhorot</i> with the operative opinion in <i>Terumot</i>.) The Yerushalmi implies that practice should follow Rebbi Simeon.</i>. But we have stated: “Rebbi Simeon says, he should bleed it even if he knows he will cause a blemish.” This parallels the later opinion of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">His opinion about the “second amphora”, where he permits to induce impurity on most of the heave in order to save a small portion in purity.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The reason of Rebbi Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:24" href="/Deuteronomy.12.24"><i>Deut</i>. 12:24</a>): “You shall not eat it; spill it onto the ground like water.” I permitted you its blood only to spill it<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">One would have expected R. Jehudah to use <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:23" href="/Deuteronomy.15.23"><i>Deut</i>. 15:23</a>, speaking of the firstling: “Only its blood you shall not eat; spill it onto the ground like water.” The verse used gives the rules for animals which develop blemishes after being dedicated. This also includes firstlings.</i>. Rebbi Abba Mari, the brother of Rebbi Yose, objected: That is written about [blood of] invalid sacrifices: “You shall not eat it; spill it onto the ground like water.” Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, you get from it for preparation<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">Blood is one of the fluids that prepare dry food to accept impurity (cf. <i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Notes 136, 141). Since the rules for preparation are spelled out for water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:38" href="/Leviticus.11.38"><i>Lev</i>. 11:38</a>), other fluids have this property of water only if there is a biblical verse which compares them to water.</i>. Just as water prepares, so blood prepares. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Both of them explained the same verse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:21" href="/Leviticus.22.21"><i>Lev</i>. 22:21</a>): “Perfect it<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">Any sacrifice.</i> shall be for goodwill; any blemish shall not be on it.” Rebbi Simeon explains: As long as it is for goodwill, you may not induce a blemish. If it is no longer for goodwill, you may induce a blemish. But the Sages say, even if it is all blemishes, you may not add a blemish<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote">As Rashi explains in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 35b" href="/Bekhorot.35b"><i>Bekhorot</i>35b</a>, the Sages hold that R. Simeon would be justified if the verse read “<i>no blemish</i> shall be on it.” But the involved language, <i>any blemish</i>, forbids the imposition of a new blemish on existing blemishes.<br>Here ends the parallel in <i>Pesaḥim</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina: With a vessel which inside is pure but impure outside one does not defile a small amount even to save a large amount<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">This discussion seems to center on the position of R. Eliezer, who does not permit defiling heave under any circumstances, even in order to save some heave in purity. In biblical law, pottery can become impure only from the inside; once the inside is impure by primary impurity, the outside also is impure. The outside of a pottery vessel can be impure only by rabbinic decree, not by biblical law. A metal vessel can become impure from the outside; such a vessel, if touched by a fluid from outside, becomes impure; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.2.2"><i>Berakhot</i>, Chapter 8, Note 46</a>.<br>Whether the discussion is about the position of R. Eliezer or R. Joshua and the kind of impurity of vessels involved is a matter of controversy in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 21a" href="/Pesachim.21a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 21a</a>.</i>. But did we not state: “Let it descend and become impure; he should not make it impure with his hands?<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">This suggests that making impure indirectly, by a vessel, is permitted.</i>” Rebbi Samuel bar Berekhiah said, explain for two vessels, one pure inside and impure outside, the other impure inside and pure outside<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina does not refer to the position of R. Eliezer (who is not to be followed in practice) but to that of R. Joshua (whom practice follows). It is stated that if there is a chance that the wrong vessel will be chosen, which would make everything impure, nothing should be done.</i>. But that Mishnah speaks of one impure vessel! Rebbi Mana said, explain it if the vat is pure; the Mishnah deals with an impure vat<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah speaks of the “lower part of the winepress”. If that is a cistern built in the ground, it is part of the ground and cannot become impure. In that case, if the cistern is empty, the heave should be left to flow into it. This is the case considered by R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina. The Mishnah must speak of a winepress whose receptacle is movable and therefore can become impure together with its contents.</i>. Did we not state: “Let it descend and be absorbed [into the ground]; he should not cause it to be absorbed by his hands’ [action].” Explain it if it rolled onto to a <i>bet happĕrās</i><sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">בית הפרס is a ploughed field where a grave had been. It is possible that some bones were dug up; the entire field is impure by rabbinic practice. Therefore, the rules are less strict than for biblical impurity and according to everybody the vessel may be used if only its interior is pure.</i>.
In the opinion of the colleagues, first and second amphoras. In the opinion of Rebbi Yose, an amphora of wine and one of oil<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">This refers back to the beginning of the Halakhah, notes 184 ff. The colleagues make an essential difference between “first” and “second” amphoras, as explained there. R. Yose (the Amora) may hold that R. Joshua permits exposing heave to impurity only for wine, which cannot be used if it is unclear whether it is pure or impure. Olive oil <i>can</i> be used as fuel for lamps even if pure; it <i>must</i> be used as fuel once it becomes impure. It follows that suspended heave of oil is usable and the Amora R. Yose holds that it should not be exposed to impurity even according to R. Joshua.</i>.
It is not reasonable if one already was impure<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote">In one of the heave cakes was impure, it is reasonable to hold that that cake might be handed over to the Gentile to give him the impression that he was defiling when in fact his actions had no effect.</i>. It is not reasonable if one was a slave girl<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote">Since slaves cannot marry, a slave girl (of Gentile origin, becoming quasi Jewish by immersion in a ritual bath) is barred from having sex with Jews for whom extramarital relations are sinful. [Sexual relations of a Jew with a person whom he could not marry are criminal acts, punishable by the courts.] Therefore, since sex is a fundamental human right, a slave girl is free to have guiltless sexual relations with slaves and Gentiles; she cannot be defiled by sexual relations with Gentiles. Since a freed slave girl can marry any Jew except a Cohen, her sleeping with these Gentiles would not impair her marriage chances if she should be manumitted later. Therefore, her being given to the Gentiles is similar to the impure heave being handed over.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 7:20" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).7.20">Tosephta 7:20</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 94:9" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.94.9"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 94(9</a>).</i>: “A group of people on the road were met by Gentiles who said to them, give us one of you that we may kill him, otherwise we shall kill all of you; even if all of them are killed they should not hand over a Jewish person. If they designated one, like Sheba ben Bikhri<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Sheba ben Bikhri revolted against David and the entire affair was intra-Jewish (2<i>S</i>. 20), the action of the people of Abel Bet Maakhah is taken as legal precedent in all cases.</i>, they should hand him over so as not to be killed.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, on condition that he be guilty of a capital crime like Sheba ben Bikhri; but Rebbi Joḥanan said, even if he is not guilty of a capital crime like Sheba ben Bikhri. Ulla bar Qoshav<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms. and <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>: Qoshar.</i> was proscribed by the government. He fled and went to Lydda to Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. They came and surrounded the city. They said to them, if you do not hand him over to us, we shall destroy the city. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi went to him and talked him into being handed over to them. Elijah, may be be remembered for good things, used to appear to him<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Levi.</i>; he<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Elijah.</i> stopped appearing. He<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Levi.</i> fasted many fasts; he<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Elijah.</i> appeared to him and said, do I appear to informers? He<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Levi.</i> said to him, did I not act according to a <i>baraita</i>? He<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">Elijah.</i> said to him, is that a statement for the pious?
Rebbi Issi was captured in Safsufa<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">In the next story, the place is under the dominion of Zenobia, queen of Palmyra. The extent of her dominion in Syria in not well documented. There are some places <i>al-Safsūf</i> in Galilee. Note Arabic سفسف “to sift”, سفساف “matter of little value, importance.”</i>. Rebbi Jonathan said, may the dead be wrapped in his shroud<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">He considers him as dead. In the Rome ms., after R. Jonathan, R. Joḥanan is quoted in the same sense. Since in the following R. Joḥanan is described as “old man”, and R. Issi (Assi) was a student of R. Joḥanan, the mention of R. Jonathan is an anachronism.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if I should kill or be killed, I shall go and save him by force. He went and negotiated; they handed him over to him. He said to them, come to our old man, he shall pray for you. They came to Rebbi Joḥanan. He said to them, what was in your mind to do to him shall be done to these people. They did not reach אפיפסרוס<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome ms., אפיפסרון. Cf. Greek ἄποψις, -εως, ἡ, “outlook, tower from which one looks out”. Perhaps “they did not reach the lookout” or “they had not vanished from sight.” (E. G.)</i> when all of them were gone<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote">They all had died.</i>.
Zeir bar Ḥinena was captured at Safsufa. Rebbi Ammi and Rebbi Samuel went to negotiate for him. Queen Zenobia said to them, your Creator usually does wonders for you; put Him under pressure! There came a Saracen carrying a sabre. He said to them, with this sabre did Odenathus<sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote">Zenobia’s husband. In the Babli, his name is בר נצר. It is not clear whether Odenathus killed his own brother or the brother of the Saracen.</i> kill his brother. Zeїr bar Ḥinena was saved.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, I was robbed by the people of Qanah. He went to the assembly hall where Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish was asking him repeatedly but he did not answer. He said, what happened? He said, what can you do? He said, all limbs depend on the heart and the heart depends on the wallet. He said, I was robbed by the people of Qanah. He said, what do you have? He said to him, show me their corner<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">Their quarter in Tiberias.</i>. He went out to show him. They saw him from afar and started to chirp. They said, if that is Rebbi Joḥanan, may he take half of it<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">The Rome ms. adds: ואין ריש לקיש הוא יסוב כולה “when it is R. Simeon ben Laqish, he will take everything”.</i>. He said to them, by your life I am taking all of it; he took all.
<sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote">A more complete version is in <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 63(24); <i>Yalquṭ Šim‘oni</i>#110.</i> The boys of Rebbi Jehudah Neśia hit Diodes<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Diocles</i>, reportedly the original name of <i>Diocletianus</i> before he became a general and then emperor. דיוקלוט <i>Dioclot</i> perhaps reflects contemporary spelling pronunciation.</i> the swine-herd. He became king and descended to Paneas. He sent letters to the rabbis that they should be at his place immediately after the end of the Sabbath. He said to his courier, do not deliver the letters before the evening, near sundown. The courier came to them in he evening, near sundown. Rebbi Jehudah Neśia and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman had gone to bathe in the public baths of Tiberias. An אנגיטרוס<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">Rome ms.: אנטיגרס; <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>(Theodor-Albeck) ארגינטין, ארגנטין, ארגנטי, ארגינט, ארגינטי also אגרנטין, <i>Yalquṭ</i> ארגינטין. The name of this friendly demon has not been determined.<br>Since <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> clearly is the primary source, רגנטי(א) may mean “guiding spirit” from Latin <i>rego</i> “to lead; conduct; guide”; cf. <i>regens</i>,<i>-ntis</i>“prince, ruler”, <i>rectus</i>,<i>-a</i>,<i>-urn</i> “led straight; direct; in a straight line” (E. G.).</i> came to them; Rebbi Jehudah Neśia wanted to scold him<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>, the demon danced before them. The corresponding sentence must have been lost here.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, let him; he appears for wonders. He said to them, how are the rabbis? They told him about their problem. He said to them, go and bathe; your Creator will perform a miracle for you at Sabbath’s end. He<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">The friendly demon, after Sabbath’s end. Diocletian descended to Paneas from the North or East; the demon had to bring them up from South and West.</i> carried them and brought them up. They<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">Diocletian’s staff.</i> said to him, the rabbis are outside. There was that bathhouse, he<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Diocletian.</i> let it be heated for seven days and seven nights. He<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">The demon.</i> went out and cooled it before them. They went in and then appeared before him<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Diocletian.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote">Diocletian.</i> said to them: Because your Creator performs miracles for you, you insult the majesty! They said, we insulted Diocles the swine-herd; we do not insult Diocletianus the king. Even so, one should never vex a little Roman or a little Gueber<sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote">The tribe of the Sassanid kings in Persia. In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> and <i>Yalquṭ</i>: גולייר cf. <i>galearii</i>,<i>-orum</i>, “a kind of soldier’s servants.”</i>.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sows heave in error it should be ploughed under; if intentionally it must be kept<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The entire harvest will be heave. In case of unintentional use of heave grain, if the error is discovered early enough, the farmer has a chance for a new crop. If it was intentional, the field cannot be used for commercial produce the entire growing season.</i>. If it is one third ripe it must be kept, whether in error or intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As explained earlier, “one third ripe” means that the grains are well formed and one third ripe, not that only one third of the entire growing season has passed. The grain is heave and may not be destroyed.</i>. But flax must be ploughed under when [sown] intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The reason is explained in the Halakhah: To avoid that the flax stalks (which are not food but the most valuable part of the plant) could be used.</i>.<br>But it is subject to gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since the new crop grown from heave seed is heave only by rabbinic decree, it is not freed from the biblical obligations.</i>. Israel poor and poor Cohanim collect; the Israel poor must sell to the Cohanim poor for the value of heave but the proceeds are theirs<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">What is collected must be treated as heave but since it is intrinsically profane, the proceeds are profane in the hand of the poor non-Cohen.</i>. Rebbi Ṭarphon says, only Cohanim poor may collect; maybe they would forget and put some in their mouths<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The poor non-Cohen will incur guilt eating what was declared to be heave.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, if that is so, then only pure persons should collect<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since eating heave when impure is a deadly sin.</i>.<br>Also, it is subject to tithe and the tithe of the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since the new crop grown from heave seed is heave only by rabbinic decree, it is not freed from the biblical obligations.</i>. Poor Israel and poor Cohanim may take; the poor Israel must sell to Cohanim for the value of heave but the proceeds are theirs<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">What is collected must be treated as heave but since it is intrinsically profane, the proceeds are profane in the hand of the poor non-Cohen.</i>. He who flails<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">He removes the grains from the ears by hitting with a flail. He is praiseworthy because he does not use an animal. Since grain is human food, its heave may not be used as animal feed. On the other hand, muzzling a threshing animal is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut</i>. 25:4</a>).</i> is praiseworthy; he who threshes, what shall he do? He hangs baskets over the neck of the animal and fills it from the same kind<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">So that the animal may eat from the basket while threshing.</i>. In that way, he will not muzzle the animal or feed it heave.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If someone sows heave in error it should be ploughed under;” etc. Would it not be reasonable to switch<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Should not the crop sown intentionally be ploughed under and the one sown unintentionally be left standing?</i>? They fined him that his field should be worthless under the heave<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the yield will be heave, the farmer sowing intentionally will be deprived of the use of his field for the entire growing season.</i>.
Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon asked: Can he be trusted if he says, I did it intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If he prefers to have a field of heave to having to plough it under.</i>? They said to him, if other people knew, it is not on his [word]; otherwise, the mouth which forbade is the mouth that permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A general legal principle that if somebody testifies to something, not known from other sources, which would put him at a disadvantage, he himself may offer additional information which explains away the pejorative aspects. This rule is accepted in both Talmudim.</i>.
Rebbi Benjamin ben Gidul asked: If he had sown vetch? If he had sown heave from outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Vetch is animal feed; it is subject to heave only by rabbinic usage. Produce from outside the Land is subject to heave only by rabbinic decree.</i>? They said to him, it is a “fence”, and there is no “fence” for a “fence”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Reading גדירה for Babylonian גזירה; the rules of the Mishnah are rabbinic decrees promulgated as a “fence around the Law” to safegard the Biblical institution of heave. Rabbinic heave needs no “fence”.</i>.
That means<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Mishnah stating that produce one third ripe should be left standing in any case.</i> that sanctity falls on it when it is still standing<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If the illegally grown produce would acquire the status of heave only after harvesting, there would be no difference before and after it is one third ripe.</i>. That means, if he separated before it was one third ripe it did not become holy<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If heave would be holy when taken from produce not one third ripe then it would always be forbidden to plough under any produce from heave since one may not destroy heave.</i>.
“[It] must be ploughed under when [sown] intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah, speaking of flax heave sown.</i>.” They fined him lest he profit from the wooden parts<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The reason is explained in the Halakhah: To avoid that the flax stalks (which are not food but the most valuable part of the plant) could be used.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked: If he dedicated sheaves of his [harvest]<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He dedicated some sheaves as heave even though the duty of heave starts only after threshing. It is implied by the preceding Halakhah that any heave dedicated from grain that was at least one third ripe is legal heave. Hence, the sheaves are legal heave.</i> and they were switched<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">They were mixed up with profane sheaves. Gleanings from profane sheaves are for the poor. The question implies that practice follows R. Meїr in Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 4:8 that in cases of doubt, the gleanings belong to the poor. Nevertheless, grain falling from heave sheaves is heave; since the heave sheaves are no longer identifiable, all gleanings are potential heave and have to be sold to Cohanim as heave. Since there is no answer given, R. Yose’s hypotheses are accepted.</i>, do even so poor Israel and poor Cohanim collect?
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Tahorot 8:3" href="/Tosefta_Tahorot.8.3">Tosephta <i>Ṭahorot</i> 8:4</a>.</i>: “The wife of a vulgar may grind with the wife of a Fellow when she is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The notions of “vulgar” and “fellow” are explained in the Introduction to <i>Demay</i>. The grain to be ground must be prepared to accept impurity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 141</a>. If the wife of the vulgar considers herself impure, she will refrain from touching the grain. If she holds herself to be pure she still is impure by Fellows’ standards but will not refrain from touching the grain and will make it impure by Fellows’ standards.<br>In the Tosephta, R. Simeon disagrees and forbids grinding together with the wife of a vulgar since the latter, while careful herself in the days of her impurity, will not care if her friends touch the grain.</i>. But when she is pure she should not grind, for she will hold herself more pure that the other.” Following the words of Rebbi Ṭarphon she should not grind even when she is impure lest she forget and put some into her mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is not mentioned in the Tosephta, the teaching of R. Ṭarphon cannot represent practice.</i>!
So is the Mishnah: “If that is so, then only pure persons should collect.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, both Cohanim and non-Cohanim may collect if they are pure. This text is in most Mishnah mss., except most of the Maimonides tradition. Cf. J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנוסח המשנה <sup>2</sup>, p. 448; N. Sacks, ed., משנה עם שנויי נוסחאות, זרעים ב p. קעב.</i>”
So is the Mishnah: “It is subject to heaves<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Great Heave and Heave of the Tithe.</i>, tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">First tithe (always), second tithe (in years 1, 2, 4, 5 of the Sabbatical cycle) and the tithe of the poor (in years 3, 6 of the Sabbatical cycle.) The special mention of the tithe of the poor is needed only as introduction to the next sentence, that non-Cohanim are entitled to the tithe of the poor in case of doubt.</i>, and the tithe of the poor.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.5-6">Tosephta 8:5–6</a>.</i> If he<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The Levite planted the onions before he gave Heave of the Tithe; when all is still <i>ṭevel</i>. After Heave of the Tithe has been given, the tithe is totally profane in the hand of the Levite.</i> planted one pound of onions of First Tithe and it became ten pounds, these are subject to tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “Subject to heave and First and Second Tithes.” Since onions do not disappear in the new growth, the entire harvest is <i>ṭevel</i> by biblical law.</i>. Its First Tithe is given for the heave of the tithe and for what he planted<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For the Heave of the Tithe that should have been given before the onions were planted.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Any Jew who illegally planted Second Tithe that was not redeemed. Unredeemed Second Tithe may only be used as food inside the walls of Jerusalem.</i> planted one pound of onions of Second Tithe and it became ten pounds, these are subject to tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “Subject to heave and First and Second Tithes.”</i> and he still has to redeem the second tithe from what he planted.
“ “He who flails is praiseworthy.” Praiseworthy in relation to whom? To him who threshes. “He who threshes, what shall he do?” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:3" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.3">Tosephta 8:3</a>; quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 90a" href="/Bava_Metzia.90a">Babli <i>Baba</i> <i>Meẓi‘a</i> 90a</a>.</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon: “He hangs vetch for her and she likes it better than anything.” Rebbi Yose said, this implies that if he did this with profane [grain]<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Even if it is permitted to feed the animal from the grain to be threshed, vetch may be substituted for the grain (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut</i>. 25:4</a>).</i> he does not transgress “do not muzzle.”
“<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A different <i>baraita</i> is quoted in Babli <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 89b: “Cows trampling on grain or threshing heave and tithes are not subject to ‘do not muzzle’ but because of the bad impression one brings some of the same kind and hangs it around their necks; R. Simeon says, he hangs vetch for her and she likes it better than anything.” The translation follows the interpretation of Maimonides.</i> One does not transgress ‘do not muzzle’ while softening legumes or fenugreek<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear how cattle were used in these cases.</i>, but it is forbidden because of the bad impression.” Some Tannaїm state: “Threshing which is permitted to you<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut</i>. 25:4</a> applies only to threshing profane grain. The opposing opinion applies the verse both to profane and sanctified food; this is the basis for the Mishnah.</i>.” But some Tannaїm state: “Threshing which is not permitted to you.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph belongs to the discussion of Mishnah 10:1 and is partially repeated in Halakhah 10:1.</i> Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, our Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">10:1, speaking of onions stored with lentils when one of the kinds is heave and the other profane. In that case the profane food will become legal heave if it has absorbed the taste of the heave.</i> after the lentils lost their sap; [then] the lentils shrink<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In Halakhah 10:1, the reading of both mss. is צופדות; this has been translated here (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Lamentations 4:8" href="/Lamentations.4.8"><i>Thr</i>. 4:8</a>). In Halakhah 9:4, both sources read צופרות “deflect”.</i> it that it will not give [any taste]. But if he gave it before the lentils gave their sap; it does not deal with that case<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If the lentils are moist, a dry outer skin of the onion will not be impermeable.</i>. And just as you say, the lentils shrink it that it will not give<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The taste of the onions into the lentils.</i>, so the lentils shrink that it will not absorb<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The onion will not absorb the taste of the lentils.</i>. How is that? If he put a profane onion into lentils of heave, the lentils shrink it that it will not absorb. That means, if it is dry, but if it<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The onion.</i> is moist it is forbidden. Onions and leeks are forbidden moist or dry, whole or cut up. If he removed the top it is as if cut up. If they are two or three they are as if cut up<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 10:1 mentions “onion” in the singular.</i>.
This all is if the outer skin<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Of the onion. The outer skin of a dry onion is not usually used as food. Therefore, it should not have the sanctity of heave.</i> does not impart taste. But if it does, there are Tannaïm who state: “Threshing which is permitted to you.” The Mishnah there<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Mishnah here, 9:3.</i> implies also threshing which is not permitted to you, since we have stated: “He hangs baskets over the neck of the animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah forbids muzzling the animal, it considers the grain to be heave. Normally, the obligation of heave starts only after threshing. This means that the Mishnah considers growth from heave as heave in biblical law (negated by the Babli, <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 90a). By analogy, since the outer skin of green onions is food, the dry outer skin of an onion of heave also retains its quality of heave. In addition, practice follows those who prohibit the muzzling of threshing animals under all circumstances.</i>”
“When it threshes,” not when it tramples on it. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, even if it tramples on it<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what “trampling” means. Maimonides (<i>Śekhirut</i> 12:6) seems to read it as “stepping on” and permits muzzling cattle when driven from one place to another. Here, “trampling” must refer to some agricultural work; a detailed definition of the conditions imposed by the biblical use of “threshing”, based on <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 287, is in <i>Maʻserot</i> 2:6 and its parallel Babli <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 89a.<br>Most commentators refer the disagreement here to the earlier statement that there is no prohibition of muzzling if vegetables are crushed (Notes 38–39).</i>.
Rebbi Abun said: Rebbi Aqiba asked Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 90b, R. Jonathan asked R. Simai.</i> in order to examine him: If somebody muzzled outside and then brought [the animal] inside<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Reading the verse as: “Do not muzzle an ox while he is threshing”, does this imply that one may use a muzzled ox for threshing when the act of muzzling was done away from the threshing floor?</i>? He said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 10:9" href="/Leviticus.10.9"><i>Lev</i>. 10:9</a>): “When you come to the tent of meeting;” from when you come to the tent of meeting<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli is more explicit: Since the paragraph forbids priests to serve in the Temple when they have alcohol in their system, it implies that it is forbidden for a priest, who had come to the Temple while sober, to drink alcohol during his service. Therefore, as a matter of biblical style, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut</i>. 25:4</a> has to be interpreted following R. Ezra.</i>. Rebbi Ezra<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Azariah.</i> said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut</i>. 25:4</a>) “Do not muzzle an ox at his threshing;” do not thresh using a muzzled ox.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The growth from heave is heave<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">By rabbinic decree. In the opinion of the Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 13c) this is one of the “eighteen decrees” which the House of Shammai forced on the House of Hillel; according to the Yerushalmi (<i>Šabbat</i> 3c), it might be one of these decrees. The reason given is that one wants to prevent a Cohen from keeping impure heave for sowing since in the meantime he might inadvertently eat from it.</i>; the growth from their growth is profane. But the growths from <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Seed grain is exempt from heave and tithes.</i>, First Tithe, aftergrowth of the Sabbatical<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The entire prohibition of aftergrowth of the Sabbatical year is rabbinical, as is heave from outside the Land.</i>, heave from outside the Land, <i>demaʻ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since in biblical law, a minority of forbidden food disappears in the majority, if the <i>ṭevel</i> content of <i>dema‘</i> is strictly less than 50%, the prohibition is rabbinical.</i>, and First Fruits are profane. Growths from dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Dedicated to the Temple to be sold and its proceeds used for the Temple.</i> [seeds] and Second Tithe are profane; one redeems them corresponding to the time of sowing<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The language implies that grains from Second Tithe or dedicated grain were sown without being redeemed, otherwise these would be totally profane. One has to redeem from next year’s crop only an amount equal to the original seed grain. The reading of the Rome ms. and the Munich ms. of the Babli is: בדמי זרעם “for the value of their seeds;” the meaning is the same.</i>.<br>One hundred <i>lagenae</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Name of a vessel and a measure of grain, Greek λάγυνος, λάγηνος, Latin <i>lagena</i>,<i>lagaena</i>,<i>lagoena</i>,<i>lagona</i>, Accadic <i>lignu</i>,<i>liginnu</i>; used here for the surface area from which one <i>liginnu</i> can be harvested. The size of a <i>lagena/liginnu</i> in Mishnaic times is unknown (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 7:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.7.4.5"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 7, Note 85</a>). The Halakhah assumes that each <i>lagena</i> is a separate plot but the farmer forgot what he sowed where.</i> of heave and one profane are all permitted for produce whose seeds desappear<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">If a field (probably the standard field of one <i>bet seah</i>) is sown to yield 101 <i>lagenae</i>, and 100 parts of the seeds were profane and one part heave, then all the yield is profane and usable by everybody since even the growth from heave seed is forbidden only by rabbinic degree. (This would make 1 <i>lagena</i> = 4.975 square cubits.)</i>; but for produce whose seeds do not disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Bulbous plants.</i>, even if there are one hundred profane and one of heave they all are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since the original heave is still in existence, it is forbidden by biblical decree and the entire crop is available only to Cohanim.</i>.<br>Growth from <i>ṭevel</i> is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, grain becomes forbidden food as <i>ṭevel</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:5:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.5.7"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 1, Note 303</a>) after threshing and cleaning until heave and tithes are taken. Before threshing, one may eat the grains from single ears. However, for produce whose seeds do not disappear, the quality of <i>ṭevel</i> is never removed and such produce is forbidden even as occasional snack before heave and all tithes are taken. (However, tithes for any produce other than grain, grapes, and olives are rabbinic in character.)</i> for anything whose seeds disappear, but if the seeds do not disappear even the growth from the growth is forbidden. What is one whose seeds do not disappear? For example arum, garlic, and onions. Rebbi Jehudah says, garlic is like barley.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> We have stated on this<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to growth of heave.</i>: When has this been said? Anything whose seeds disappear. But in produce whose seeds do not disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Bulbous plants.</i>, the growth from their growth is forbidden. But <i>ṭevel</i> is mostly profane<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">88% is profane if neither heave nor First Tithe were taken.</i>, First Tithe is mostly profane<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">90% is profane. If heave of the tithe is taken, the resulting grain is totally profane and is no longer called “First Tithe”.</i>, aftergrowth of the Sabbatical is infrequent<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">That one would be able to collect enough grain from aftergrowth to serve as seed grain.</i>, heave from outside the Land is infrequent<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Even from countries bordering on the Land where there is a rabbinic heave; that heave should not be brought into the Land.</i>, most of <i>dema‘</i> is profane<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since in biblical law, a minority of forbidden food disappears in the majority, if the <i>ṭevel</i> content of <i>dema‘</i> is strictly less than 50%, the prohibition is rabbinical.</i>, First Fruits are infrequent.
“Growths from dedicated [seeds] and Second Tithe are profane; one redeems them corresponding to the time he sowed.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He redeems the entire storage for the value of that <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">One has to redeem only the value of the seed grain, not the entire harvest.</i>. The difference between dedicated [produce] and Second Tithe: Second Tithe, whether produce whose seeds disappear or whose seeds do not disappear, is redeemed by the wholesale price<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Maʻaser Šeni</i> 4:2. The Babli (<i>Baba Meẓiʻa</i> 57a) holds that redemption may be done below the wholesale value; this is never mentioned in the Yerushalmi.</i>; only that produce whose seeds disappear is redeemed by current prices, produce whose seeds do not disappear is redeemed following the early price<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The wholesale price at the time and place of sowing.</i>. Dedicated [produce], whether produce whose seeds disappear or whose seeds do not disappear, is treated in its place and time<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The wholesale price at time and place of redemption is the operative price.</i>; only that one redeems the entire storage of produce whose seeds disappear for the value of that <i>seah</i>, but for produce whose seeds do not disappear he has to redeem the [contents of] the entire storage.
Rebbi Yose said: This implies that if there were 100 <i>seah</i>, each one lifted from 100 <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In a hundred cases, one <i>seah</i> of heave fell into 100 <i>seah</i> profane grain and one <i>seah</i> was lifted as replacement heave. That replacement is now treated as heave and the remaining 100 <i>seah</i> as profane. But if now one of the 100 <i>seah</i> of heave was accidentally exchanged for a <i>seah</i> of profane grain and it is impossible to identify which grain is profane, all 100 <i>seah</i> (99 replacement heave and one profane) are permitted to everybody since the holiness of replacement heave is rabbinical, just as the holiness of growth from heave seeds is rabbinical.</i>, and one profane was switched with one of them, all would be permitted.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 5:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.5.2.3">Halakhah 5:6, Note 77</a>.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said if a <i>seah</i> of heave was lifted from 100 of profane produce, anything will cause it to become insignificant. And here, if he collected and some profane produce fell into it, should it not be permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the seeds do not disappear.</i>? As Rebbi Joḥanan said there<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The statement is from Mishnah 5:9 that different kinds are considered as one if they are ground together and now are indistinguishable.</i>, he mills and it is permitted, so also here. There, both kinds came into his hands. But here, only <i>ṭevel</i> came into his hands if he collected from one [<i>lagena</i>]. But if he collected from two, both kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Growth from profane and heave seeds. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:4" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.4">Tosephta (8:4</a>) it is formulated that the particular stringencies for vegetables whose seeds do not disappear are eliminated once the vegetables are harvested and R. Simeon even permits harvesting for the express purpose of eliminating the stringencies. It is clear that this Tosephta was not known to the authors of the Yerushalmi.</i> came into his hands.
How far<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The growth from <i>ṭevel</i> whose seeds do not disappear is forbidden. The Mishnah does not spell out the status of the second year’s growth.</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is forbidden for three harvests<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson does not have this clause; it seems that the sentence is the confluence of two parallel traditions. The second clause does not add anything to the first.</i>; it is forbidden up to the fourth harvest, but the fourth is permitted. Rebbi Samuel ben Eudaimon asked before Rebbi Mana: How far for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Heave planted from bulb vegetables whose first growth retains the status of heave (Mishnah 4).</i>? He said to him, they made the fourth harvest like the first harvest. Just as the first harvest of growth whose seeds disappear is forbidden for heave and permitted for <i>ṭevel</i>, so the fourth harvest of growth whose seeds do not disappear is forbidden for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">But the fifth harvest would be permitted (R. Simson).</i> and permitted for <i>ṭevel</i>.
What do you have<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">What are plants whose seeds do not disappear?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, e. g., bulb plants<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The word כלובסין is unexplained. For the translation, I am reading בּוֹלְבָּסִּין “bulbs”; cf. <i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 42 {but compare Greek κολοβός, όν “stunted, undersized” (E. G.)}.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Garlic up to the size of a barley corn is a plant whose seeds disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The dispute is about the statement of R. Jehudah in the Mishnah that garlic is like barley for these rules. That the statement of R. Jehudah is discussed in practical terms signifies for <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Pesachim 34a" href="/Rashi_on_Pesachim.34a">Rashi (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 34a</a>) that practice follows him; this is denied by Maimonides.</i>. Larger than that, it is a plant whose seeds do not disappear. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Garlic is like barley in every respect.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who weeds with a Non-Jew among bulb plants<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Defined by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:3" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.3">Tosephta (9:3</a>) as arum, garlic, onions, and leeks; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.6.3"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 2, Note 126</a>.</i>, even though his vegetables are <i>ṭevel</i> one may eat a snack from them<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A snack can be eaten from anything that grows until it is harvested in bulk and ready for storage. This Tanna holds that a Gentile’s real estate in the Holy Land is still subject to the obligations of heave and tithes before Jews may eat from its yield (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.9"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 4, Notes 129–131</a>).</i>. Saplings of heave that became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">They are forbidden as food and should have been burned.</i> if planted become pure not to transmit impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing in the ground can be impure. But nevertheless impure heave is forbidden as food and, since the growth from heave is heave, this prohibition is transmitted to the new growth. This is the interpretation of Maimonides (<i>Terumot</i> 11:23). R. Abraham ben David holds that once the impurity has been removed, the heave becomes food for Cohanim. His opinion is well supported in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 70a" href="/Menachot.70a">Babli (<i>Menaḥot</i> 70a</a>) which quotes as a Mishnah the statement that nothing connected to the ground can be heave. That statement is not in the Mishnah texts and is incompatible with the Yerushalmi here (Notes 99 ff.).<br>For the anonymous Tanna, the second growth is permitted, for R. Jehudah the third growth.</i> but they remain forbidden to be eaten until he removes what is edible<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">And burns it.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, until he removes a second time.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This follows Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Who with R. Jehudah holds that Gentiles’ possessions in the Land are free from obligations of heave and tithes. Since R. Jehudah and R. Simeon are greater authorities than R. Meïr who holds that Gentiles’ possessions are subject to heave and tithes, it is difficult to understand the Mishnah following our rules.</i>. If it follows Rebbi Joḥanan, even a full meal! But since Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Ḥiyya said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.4.10"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 3, Note 132</a>.</i>: Rebbi Simeon agrees that nevertheless he must separate tithes as a matter of practice; therefore we have to say that this follows Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The rule given by the Mishnah is rabbinical; for R. Meir it would be biblical.</i>.
It was stated: <i>Demay</i> is permitted to him who weeds with a Samaritan<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">On the Samaritan’s property. If he only suspects the Samaritan not to have tithed the plants used to produce the seeds, he may eat snacks from the fruits but not if he is sure that the Samaritan used untithed fruit. One has to wonder about the entire paragraph since Samaritans are Sadducees following only the written law. For them, the only produce subject to heave and tithes are grains, grapes, and olives. Therefore, Samaritan onions, etc., are certainly not tithed. This is the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:7" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.7">Tosephta (8:7</a>) which treats all Samaritan bulb plants as growths from <i>ṭevel</i> except after a Sabbatical year.</i>. Therefore, not if it is certain! Why? For <i>demay</i> disappears when it is in the ground but the growth of forbidden produce<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If the seeds were certain <i>ṭevel</i>, the new growth goes under the rules of Mishnah 6.</i> cannot lift it.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Saplings of heave that had become impure he may plant and make them heave but if they were heave from before they already were pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If they were impure and he planted them to free them from impurity, he may then declare the growth to be heave and it is permitted for Cohanim. But if it was impure heave before planting, the new growth cannot be eaten by anybody (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.9.3.1">Note 92</a>).</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Water that became impure is made to touch and may be used as water for Tabernacles but if it was water for Tabernacles from before it already is pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Flowing water or standing water of at least 40 <i>seah</i> removes impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.8.4">Chapter 4, Note 112</a>). Impure water in a vessel can be purified by immersing the vessel in a <i>miqweh</i> so that the surface of the water in the vessel barely touches (“kisses”) the water of the <i>miqweh</i>. Water of Tabernacles is the water used as a libation on the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.1.5.2"><i>Ševi‘it</i> Chapter 1, Notes 51–52</a>). Before being used, it has to be dedicated in a Temple vessel.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 34b" href="/Pesachim.34b">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 34b</a>) concludes from the parallel treatment of heave and libations that dedication in or for the Temple is as strong and permanent a designation as heave.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Figs and grapes that had become impure one may squeeze and make them heave but if they were heave from before they already were pushed aside<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Grape juice is considered a new food; if there is less than the volume of a chicken egg, the fluid cannot accept impurity. But if the grapes were impure heave, they are not food and no food can come from them. Grape juice squeezed from these grapes is not a drink, pure or impure.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Assi, in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, one squeezes less than the volume of olives<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">An indefinite plural means 2, as smallest integer &gt;1 [cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Seder Olam</i> (Northvale NJ 1998), p. 6].
