Since a chicken egg is not smaller than the convex hull of two olives touching one another (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 80a" href="/Yoma.80a"><i>Tosaphot Yešenim Yoma</i> 80a</a>), squeezing not more than the volume of two olives will keep the volume safely below the minimal volume that might lead to impurity. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 34b" href="/Pesachim.34b">Babli (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 34b</a>) does not define what is “less than the volume of a chicken egg.”</i> and his wine is good even for libations. They said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagrees. Both about leniency and restriction? If you say about leniency, this is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish does not agree that juice squeezed from a fruit becomes a new kind of food; it is the fluid form of the previous fruit and remains impure even if squeezed in amounts less than a chicken egg.</i>. If you say about restriction<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish cannot deny that impure heave remains forbidden as food for everybody; even if he would accept the trick of squeezing small quantities he could not accept that this would make impure heave drinkable.</i>, did we not state: “Until he removes what is edible?”
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: so is the Mishnah, “until he removes the leaves a second time.<sup>105”</sup>

Chapter 10



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An onion which one added to lentils, if whole it is permitted, if cut up it depends on transferring taste<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Profane onion in cooked but cold heave lentils; if the heave changes the taste of the profane food everything has to be eaten by a Cohen under the rules of heave. Otherwise, the profane remains profane and permitted to everybody.</i>. Any other cooked food, whether whole or cut up, depends on transferring taste. Rebbi Jehudah permits in anchovies<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In translation and interpretation, one has the choice between biblical “stinking dish” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Joel 2:20" href="/Joel.2.20"><i>Joel</i> 2:20</a>) or mishnaic “small salted fish in brine” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.5.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 5:5</a>), Arabic צחן. Maimonides chooses the first alternative, R. Simson the second. One has to wonder that the medical doctor Maimonides would think of saving spoiled food.<br>If an entire onion of heave is put into the brine to absorb impurities, the anchovies remain profane according to R. Jehudah.</i> where it only serves to remove pollutants.<br>If a mashed apple<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The heave apple’s influence makes the entire profane dough change nature; even if the amount of apple is negligible, its action is not and it cannot be disregarded.</i> is added to dough and it soured, [the dough] is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the lentils have to be eaten by a Cohen in purity. In all these discussions, “forbidden” means forbidden to the layman and the impure.</i>. But if barley grains fell into a cistern of water, even though they made it stink, [the water] is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since anything that destroys the taste does not make forbidden.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An onion which one added to lentils,” etc. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rav Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a shortened version of the discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:3:7-4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.3.7-4.2">Chapter 9, Halakhah 4, Notes 41–46</a>. The sentence in parentheses is superfluous. Its text follows the Rome ms. Venice and Leyden read: אמר אמר.</i> said, our Mishnah [in case] after the lentils lost their sap; [then] the lentils shrink it so it will not absorb [any taste]. (As you say, lentils shrink it so it will not absorb.) Similarly, lentils shrink it so it will not give.
The Mishnah speaks about a profane onion which he put into heave lentils; but not the case of a heave onion put into profane lentils<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the lentils have to be eaten by a Cohen in purity. In all these discussions, “forbidden” means forbidden to the layman and the impure.</i>. If it is dry, but moist is forbidden. An onion, but leeks are forbidden moist or dry, entire or cut up.
If he removed the tip<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Of the onion.</i> it is as if cut up. If there were two or three small ones, they are as if cut up. Only if the outer shell does not transmit taste<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The onion mentioned in the Mishnah is dry, with an inert brown outer skin. Green onions are like leeks.</i>. But if the outer shell transmits taste, it is forbidden.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 2:1.</i>, we have stated: “If fish improved in value when cooked with leeks of second tithe, the increase is proportional<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If the leeks were worth <i>a</i> and the fish <i>b</i> before cooking but now the whole is worth <i>c</i>&gt;<i>a</i>+<i>b</i>, then some of the proceeds of the dish, <i>ac/</i>(<i>a+b</i>), has to be used to buy food to be consumed according to the rules of second tithe.</i>.” Rebbi Hoshaia said, that Mishnah does not follow Rebbi Jehudah, since we have stated: “Rebbi Jehudah permits in anchovies where it only serves to remove pollutants<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Even though cleaned anchovies in brine fetch more on the market than uncleaned ones.</i>.”
The rabbis of Caesarea asked: The saying of Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This Yerushalmi standard is quoted in <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 2:1 (fol. 53b), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 1:3:6-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.1.3.6-17"><i>Orlah</i> 1:4</a> (fol. 61a), 2:6 (fol. 62b), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:1:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.1.2-14"><i>Nazir</i> 6:1</a> (fol. 54d), 6:10 (fol. 55b). It is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 97b" href="/Chullin.97b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 97b</a> where the more lenient standard of 1 part forbidden in 61 permitted is established.</i>, that all forbidden [food] is estimated as if it were onion, as if it were leeks<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden food that fell into permitted is permitted if the taste of the forbidden food could not be tasted in the permitted, assuming the forbidden items were onion or leeks.</i>, does this contradict Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Jehudah allows the use of heave onion to improve non-heave fish.</i>? Rebbi Jehudah will agree regarding an onion of the Temple or an onion of idol worship.
<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The main place of the paragraph is in <i>Šabbat</i> 3, fol. 5d. The text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:1:2-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.1.2-27"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:1</a> (fol. 57a), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:4:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.4.2-12"><i>Pesaḥim</i>2:4</a> (fol. 29b).</i>“If a mashed apple is added to dough which soured, [the dough] is forbidden” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:9" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.9">Tosephta 8:9</a>, the language is: “Rebbi Yose says, what is induced by [the apple] is not souring.” This is the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 36" href="/Pesachim.36">Babli in <i>Pesaḥim</i> 36a/b</a>. According to the Yerushalmi it is not clear whether what is induced by fruit juice falls under the legal category of “to turn sour;” this is the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 54a" href="/Menachot.54a">Babli in <i>Menaḥot</i> 54a</a>. In any case, the quote here cannot be from the Tosephta.</i>: Rebbi Yose permits it. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: They disagree when it becomes sour from the juice [of the apple]. But if it becomes sour from its solid substance it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Even the anonymous Sages admit that dry mixing of a mashed apple with flour will not induce souring.</i>. Just as Rebbi Yose says there<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Here in <i>Terumot</i>. R. Yose will not agree that what happens if a dough is kneaded with pure apple juice is souring; therefore he holds that mixing dough with apple juice can have no legal consequences.</i>, its souring is not clearly souring, so he says here<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 3:3: “One may not put an egg next to the samowar [that it should become a soft-boiled egg on the Sabbath] and one may not break it on [hot, exposed to the sun] towels [to make a fried egg on the Sabbath], but Rebbi Yose permits it.”</i>, its cooking is not clearly cooking.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:9" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.9">Tosephta 8:9</a>, in slightly different wording. The main source of this paragraph is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 2:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.2.4.2-5">Orlah 2:5</a> (fol. 62a); a slightly shortened version is in Avodah Zarah 5:3 (fol. 44d); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 67b-68a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.67b-68a">Babli Avodah Zarah 67b/68a</a>.</i>Everything that can be smelled is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">If forbidden food is mixed with permitted food, the mixture is forbidden as long as the forbidden substance changes the taste of the food. According to R. Simeon, whose opinion is the majority opinion in the Babli, this holds only if the admixture does not spoil the taste of the permitted food.</i>, whether it improves or spoils, the words of Rebbi Meїr. Rebbi Simeon says, if it improves it is forbidden, if it spoils it is permitted.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, that author is Ulla (Rebbi La).</i> said, in what do they disagree? If it first improved but then spoiled. But if it spoiled and later improved, even Rebbi Meїr will agree. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no difference whether it spoiled and improved or improved and spoiled, it is a disagreement. There<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Here, in <i>Terumot</i>.</i>, we have stated: “If barley grains fell into a cistern of water, even though they made it stink, the water is permitted.” What is the status of that Mishnah? Rebbi Joḥanan said it is a disagreement; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said it is the opinion of everybody. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said this tradition here<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to the Mishnah, not the Tosephta.</i>: Rebbi Joḥanan said it is a disagreement; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said it is the opinion of everybody.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody takes hot pitta bread out of the oven and deposits it on the mouth of an amphora of heave wine<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The hot flat bread absorbs the taste of the wine.</i>, Rebbi Meїr forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the lay person and the impure.</i> and Rebbi Jehudah permits. Rebbi Yose permits in the case of wheat and forbids in the case of barley because barley absorbs<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">It absorbs smells and particles.</i>.<br>If an oven was heated with heave cumin and one baked bread in it, the bread is permitted since it absorbs not the taste of cumin but the smell<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to taste, smell is considered immaterial.</i> of cumin.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Zeїra said, they asked before Rebbi Yannai: What [are the rules] if he put it on the plug<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A clay plug that closes the open top of the amphora and forms a barrier for the smell.</i> of the amphora? He said to them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 36:33" href="/Job.36.33"><i>Job</i> 36:33</a>): “Its neighbor<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse, ריע is derived from רוע “to sound the trumpet.”</i> will inform about it.” What means, “its neighbor will inform about it”? Rebbi Yose said, that which we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Makhšiřin</i> 3:2–3: “2. An amphora full of fruits added to fluids, or one full of fluids added to fruits; if they absorbed, all those which absorbed are prepared. They spoke about water, wine, and vinegar, but the rest of the fluids are pure. R. Neḥemiah declares legumes pure because legumes do not absorb. 3. If somebody takes hot pitta bread out of the oven and deposits it on the mouth of an amphora of wine, Rebbi Meїr declares it impure but Rebbi Jehudah declares it pure. Rebbi Yose declares it pure in the case of wheat and impure in the case of barley because barley absorbs.”<br>For preparation of solid food for impurity, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 141</a>. For the impurity of fluids, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Notes 136–137</a>.<br>The amphora contains fruit not prepared for impurity. Since the walls of a clay amphora are porous, the fruits inside (or outside) may get moist. Since human action puts the amphora into the fluid, the moisture prepares for impurity. This applies only to water and wine; the rest of the impure fluids (olive oil, date honey, and human body fluids) are viscous and do not penetrate a clay wall. In Mishnah 3, the wine is impure. Bread, being made with water, is always prepared for impurity. R. Meїr holds that the bread will receive the taste of the wine by absorbing particles evaporated from the wine. Since he holds that all smells transfer prohibitions and impurity, the bread must be impure.</i>: “An amphora full of fruits added to fluids, or one full of fluids added to fruits.” As you say there, only if the fluids touch the amphora; so here only if the loaf touches the plug. Rebbi Mana said, from this it follows as you say there, only if the fluids touch the amphora itself; so here only if the loaf touches the plug itself<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since the plug sits on the narrowest part of the neck of the amphora, in most cases a pitta deposited on top of the rim will not touch the plug. As noted in the next sentence, this argument is valid only if the pitta is almost cold since a hot pitta will sink down and always touch the plug.</i>. His opinion implies, even [if the pitta] is cold. Rebbi Abba said, the problem was about a cold one<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The original question directed to R. Yannai.</i>. But did we not state “hot”? Rav Ḥisda said, that one should not say since the vapor absorbs it should be permitted.
Following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, water acts on fruits, [other] fluids do not act on fruits, against Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 3:2" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.3.2">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 3:2</a> not quoted in our text, that only water and wine (table wine or vinegar) can influence the status of fruits through a clay wall. Everybody agrees that viscous fluids cannot act through the clay wall. The only problem is wine, which in the anonymous Mishnah 2 is equated with water but in Mishnah 3, R. Jehudah denies that it can act on bread. “Other fluids” here means the fluids excluded in Mishnah 2 plus wine. The statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish seems to point to an inconsistency in the position of R. Jehudah.</i>! But Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish says, water acts on fruits and loses fluid volume, [other] fluids act on fruits and do not lose volume<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since for R. Meїr it is enough that the fruits absorb some of the smell of the wine even if they do not become moistened.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, [other] fluids do not act on fruits at all<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 2 is R. Meїr’s alone; R. Jehudah would classify wine as “other fluid.” Mishnah 2 is anonymous; therefore practice follows R. Meїr in this instance.</i>.
And what Rebbi Joḥanan said, when we went to Caesarea to study Torah with the Great Rebbi Hoshaia, we put our flat-cake on boxes<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κιβώτιον “box, chest”. For <i>muries</i>, cf. <i>Demay</i> p. 384.</i> of fish sauce and we tasted there the taste of fish sauce. Is this against Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Then R. Joḥanan would agree with R. Simeon ben Laqish that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 3:2" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.3.2">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 3:2</a> determines practice following R. Meїr, against our rules that R. Jehudah is the greater authority than R. Meїr.</i>? They put it into the brine<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἁλμεύω “to put into brine”.</i>. Is that not forbidden because they rob the owner? They had obtained the owner’s permission.
Wheat below and barley above<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This discusses the statement of R. Yose in the Mishnah whom practice has to follow not only because he is the highest authority but also because he gives a reasoned decision partially following each of the opponents in the matter. One deals with a two-layer dough made from two different grains.</i>; just as wheat does not absorb so the barley will not absorb. Barley below and wheat above, just as barley absorbs so wheat will absorb.
May one roast two spits together in one oven<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This oven has the form of a conical frustrum and is open at the top; the hot air and the particles of meat it might carry escape with the draft through the open top. It is clear that in an enclosed oven one may not cook kosher and nonkosher food together.</i>, one of kosher meat and one of carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Meat from an animal not killed in a ritually acceptable way.</i>? Rav Jeremiah said in the name of Rav, it is forbidden. Samuel said in the name of Levi, it is permitted. A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 5:11" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).5.11">Tosephta <i>Pisḥa</i> 5:11</a>.</i> disagrees with Rav: “One may not roast two Passover sacrifices together in one oven because they could be mixed up<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Passover sacrifice must be eaten by its subscribers. A person who was not part of the party at the time of slaughter is prohibited from eating of the sacrifice. The Tosephta notes that roasting the two sacrifices together is forbidden only because they should not accidentally be switched but not because particles of one could be carried by the hot air onto the other sacrifice.<br>The Passover sacrifice has to be “roasted over the fire,” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:8" href="/Exodus.12.8"><i>Ex</i>. 12:8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9">9</a>. It is permitted to roast the sacrifice in an oven because the oven serves only to intensify the fire. It is forbidden to roast the sacrifice in a closed oven since then it also would be roasted by the heat stored in the walls.</i>.” They said only, because they could be mixed up; therefore, not if they could not be mixed up<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 96" href="/Pesachim.96">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i>96a/b</a>. In both Talmudim, practice follows Levi.</i>.
Calamint water, Rav said it is forbidden, Samuel said it is permitted. Samuel served Rav calamint water<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṣitra</i> has been defined as calamint at the end of <i>Ševi‘it</i> 7:2. Calamint is a weed that may be used as spice. Since it is food only by the intent of the person collecting it, it is not subject to heave, certainly not outside the Land. So if somebody in Babylonia gave heave from calamint, any drink made from it is permitted to everybody. We have a principle that if a person thinks of something permitted as forbidden, another person may not serve him what he thinks is forbidden. If Samuel disregarded this injunction, he must have thought that Rav’s position did not merit attention, or he managed to convince Rav of his error.</i>.
That inner part<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">טויה II in Levy’s dictionary is explained from Arabic طُوِي “to be hidden”. This refers back to Rav’s statement that roasting kosher and non-kosher meats together in an open oven makes the kosher meat forbidden. Rav requires even that afterwards the oven be kashered by burning dry branches in the empty oven, to remove any particles of nonkosher meat from the walls. (Explanation of R. M. Margalit.)</i>, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: One fires it with dry branches.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Fenugreek that fell into a water cistern, for heave and second tithe if the seeds are enough to impart taste, disregarding the wood<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The wooden parts of fenugreek give less taste than the seeds but may be used as cheap substitute. Nevertheless, the wood is not subject to heave and tithes and cannot be counted in matters of heave and tithes.</i>. But for the Sabbatical, <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard, and dedicated seeds, if seeds and wood together impart taste<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For these, all that can be used as spice must be counted and makes the water forbidden.</i>.<br>If somebody had bundles of fenugreek of <i>kilaim</i> from a vineyard, they should be burned. If he had bundles of fenugreek from <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi holds (Notes 54–57) that, while the wood in itself is not subject to heave and tithes, bundles of fenugreek on the stalk are objects of trade. Therefore, the making of bundles is the end of the harvest and fenugreek is subject to heave and tithes in the hand of the farmer as soon as it was bundled for sale. The Mishnah speaks both of the Geat Heave and Heave of the Tithe.<br>For the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 13a" href="/Beitzah.13a">Babli (<i>Beẓah</i> 13a</a>), stalks of fenugreek are not subject to heave and tithes; the obligation of heave starts only after the separation of seeds from the stalks by threshing (“pounding” in the Mishnah.) Therefore, the situation of the Mishnah can happen only if no heave was given but the farmer irregularly gave First Tithe to the Levite before separating the seeds from the stalks. The tithe bundles in the hand of the Levite are now <i>ṭevel</i> for Heave of the Tithe; the Levite has to prepare them by pounding and then give the Cohen his due. But if he simply hands one bundle out of ten to the Cohen, it becomes heave and he may not go back on his word and try to take the stalks for himself.</i>, he pounds them and computes how much seed this contains and separates the seeds but not the wood. If he separated, he should not say I shall pound, take the wood, and give the seeds but he gives seeds with the wood.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Does not the wood spoil repeatedly? Our Mishnah follows him who says that anything that spoils is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If the action is to spoil, why should the water be forbidden at all?</i>. But even according to him who says anything that spoils is permitted, he agrees here that it is forbidden. Why? Fenugreek improves anything into which it falls.
How much is a bundle? 25 plants<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In the technical sense, a “bundle of fenugreek” is only a bundle of at least 25 plants (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.3.4.1">Mishnah <i>Orlah</i> 3:6</a>). Also, a contract for delivery of bundles of fenugreek requires delivery of bundles of 25 each.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, four to a bed<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If the expression חבילה is used for impurity of beds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 18:9" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.18.9">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>18:9</a>), it means groups of four.</i>.
It<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah.</i> implies that they become <i>ṭevel</i> in bundles<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Without “threshing.”</i>. Why not single ears? One never gathers for that. But may grains be gathered thus<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic حبّ “grain”. The commentators are at a loss to connect Hebrew חבית (Arabic خابية) “amphora” in this context.</i>? One does not grow for that. But these<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Bundles of 25 stalks of fenugreek.</i>, one gathers for this, one grows for this.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Profane olives preserved<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Preserved in brine, oil, or vinegar.</i> with heave olives, if they were broken profanes with broken heaves, broken profanes with whole heaves, or preserves in heave fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Water in which heave olives were preserved; cf. Halakhah 7.</i>, are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For non-Cohanim.</i>. But whole profanes<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Whole olives are considered as non-absorbing.</i> with broken heaves are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For non-Cohanim.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jonah said, this implies that broken [olives] absorb, release, and absorb again; whole [olives] absorb, release, but do not absorb again<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">It is axiomatic that anything put in a fluid environment absorbs from the environment. But then the fluid also absorbs what is released from the solid food. Since one cannot say that whole olives do not absorb from their environment (they certainly absorb salt from the surrounding brine), one has to say that they release the taste acquired from heave back into the surrounding fluid and then no longer absorb.</i>. That means, if it is neither improving nor spoiling it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah implies that preserving profane and heave olives of equal quality together makes every thing forbidden to laymen.</i>. The Mishnah is Rebbi Simeon’s, for Rebbi Simeon says if its taste spoils, it is forbidden. But did Rebbi Simeon not say, if its taste spoils, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 8:9" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).8.9">Tosephta 8:9</a>, in slightly different wording. The main source of this paragraph is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Orlah 2:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Orlah.2.4.2-5"><i>Orlah</i> 2:5</a> (fol. 62a); a slightly shortened version is in <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:3 (fol. 44d); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 67b-68a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.67b-68a">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 67b/68a</a>.</i>? Following what Rebbi Simeon says<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 11. One assumes that the cabbage from the unirrigated field is heave. If it is cooked together with the more expensive and better cabbage from an irrigated garden plot, the result is forbidden to laymen even though the admixture of standard cabbage can only reduce the quality of the garden cabbage.</i>, irrigated cabbage preserved with unirrigated cabbage is forbidden because it absorbs.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If an impure fish<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Any aquatic animal without scales and fins. While the non-kosher fish cannot be eaten and cannot be knowingly preserved with kosher fish, fishsauce, whose biblical status is unclear, may be used if the admixture was not intentional and the forbidden amount is minute.</i> was preserved with a pure fish, if in any keg<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In Syriac, גרב is a vessel containing exactly 1 <i>seah</i>.</i> containing two <i>seah</i> the impure fish weighed ten <i>zuz</i> in Judea which are five tetradrachmas<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifra Šemini Parašah</i> 3(9), “Ten <i>zuz</i> in Judea which equal ten tetradrachmas in Galilee.”</i> in Galilee, the sauce is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The contents of the keg are forbidden food. “Sauce” here is not the fluid in which the fish is preserved but the fluid oozing out of the fish.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, a quarter [<i>log</i>] in two <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>seah</i> equals 24 <i>log</i>, this is 1/196 whereas the first Tanna allows only 1/960 of contamination (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.5.3">Note 81</a>).</i>. Rebbi Yose said, one sixteenth of it<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If the forbidden fluid is at least 1/16 of the kosher fish fluid, the mixture is forbidden. In this interpretation, R. Yose does not express a separate opinion on the impure fish itself.<br>According to R. Abraham ben David (whose interpretation is accepted by R. S. Lieberman), R. Yose refers to the <i>garab</i> as “it”. Since the <i>garab</i> contains 9600 <i>zuz</i> (Notes 78–81), 1/16 of it would be 600 <i>zuz</i> and the 10 <i>zuz</i> given in the Mishnah are 1/60 of this quantity. Therefore, R. Yose asserts that in this case also the mixture is permitted if the kosher food is as least 60 times the quantity of the forbidden.</i>.<br>Impure locusts preserved with pure locusts do not forbid the fluid exuding<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This fluid, which can exist only in minute quantities, is disregarded. The list of kosher locusts is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:22" href="/Leviticus.11.22"><i>Lev</i>. 11:22</a>.</i> from the latter. <sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 7" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.7"><i>Idiut</i> 7</a>:2</i> Rebbi Ẓadoq<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">A student of the House of Shammai who survived the fall of Jerusalem and became a follower of the rules of the House of Hillel. When Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai defected to the Romans, one of the favors he asked from Vespasian was medical attention for R. Ẓadoq. The meaning of R. Ẓadoq’s testimony is explained in the last paragraph of the Halakhah.</i> testified that the fluid exuding from impure locusts<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Any locust not on the list of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:22" href="/Leviticus.11.22"><i>Lev</i>. 11:22</a>. The only Jewish group with a continued tradition of identification of the edible locust is Yemenite Jewry. In Yemen, locusts were only eaten roasted.</i> is pure.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi stated from Bar Delaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:2" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.2">Tosephta 9:2</a></i>: “Unsalted pure fish preserved with salted impure fish is forbidden”. But did we not state: “He washes it and it is permitted”<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:2" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.2">Tosephta (9:2</a>), this is stated for the case when the fish are either both unsalted or both salted. In <i>Sifra Šemini Parashah</i> 3(6) it is stated: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:9" href="/Leviticus.11.9"><i>Lev</i>. 11:9</a>) “Those you shall eat,” to include pure fish preserved with impure.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 40a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.40a">Babli (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 40a</a>) reports on a disagreement on whether the fluid oozing out from non-kosher seafood is forbidden. It decides that it is forbidden; a decision accepted by R. Zeïra.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, he who said it is permitted, when he preserved them simultaneously; he who said it is prohibited when he preserved them one after the other. You can understand that this is so, since we have stated “in heave fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Water in which heave olives were preserved; cf. Halakhah 7.</i> it is forbidden;” [is that] not when he preserved one after the other<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This really proves only R. Mana’s second statement.</i>?
“Any keg containing two <i>seah</i>”. How much is in a <i>seah</i>? [24] (14) <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Both mss. have “14”, but this number is not a divisor of 960. R. Solomon ibn Adrat, <i>Responsa</i> vol. 1, No. 281, reads “24”. A <i>seah</i> is 6 <i>qab</i>, each <i>qab</i> is 4 <i>log</i>.</i>. How much is in a <i>log</i>? 2 pounds<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Of the 25 mss. collated in <i>The Mishanh with variant readings</i>(Jerusalem 1975), 11 read <i>zin</i>, 1 <i>ziz</i> (reading also of the Rome ms. in the Halakhah), and 12 <i>zuz</i> in the Mishnah text. Therefore, <i>zin</i> here equals <i>zuz</i> in the Mishnah.</i>. How much is in a pound? 100 <i>zin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>log</i> refers to volume and pound to weight, one has to assume that the volume of 1 <i>log</i> of water weighs 2 Jerusalem pounds. Under the Julian-Claudian dynasty, a denar (<i>zuz</i>) was 1/96 of a Roman 1b. of 12 Troy oz., (about 345 g). It follows from the text that this denar was 1/100 of a smaller pound.</i>. It turns out that each single <i>zin</i> corresponds to one in 960<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since 1 <i>zin =</i> 1/9600 of 2 <i>seah</i>, and 10 <i>zin</i> of impure fish make the 2 <i>seah</i> forbidden, it follows that each single <i>zin</i> of impure fish contaminates 960 times its weight or volume. (Explanation of R. Solomon ibn Adrat.)</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun instructed at Akhbara<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The place of R. Yose bar Abun’s academy. For practical purposes, he replaced 960 by 1000.</i>: one in a thousand.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: A quarter [<i>log</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, רובע means “a quarter <i>qab</i>” which is a dry measure equal to one <i>log</i> in liquid measure. But since the Yerushalmi here follows R. Jehudah in the Mishnah, one has to understand “quarter <i>log</i>”.</i> of fish sauce in two <i>seah</i> of fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Fish sauce is permitted if the proportion of impure fish (in an inadvertent contamination) is less than 1 in 196 (replaced for practical purposes by 1 in 200 by R. Abbahu.)</i>. But the sauce of an impure fish never can save<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Fish sauce is forbidden only as “sweat exuding from the fish” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 99b" href="/Chullin.99b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 99b</a>). It never can contribute to the 196 times the permitted food has to exceed the forbidden.</i>! Rebbi Abbahu said, I computed that close to 200<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">R. S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kifshutah</i> 2, p. 440) accepts an explanation that R. Abbahu determined that the sauce exuded by 10 <i>zin</i> impure fish will be about 1/200 of a <i>garab</i> of fish and sauce. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 99b" href="/Chullin.99b">Babli (<i>Ḥulin</i> 99b</a>), R. Yose bar Ḥanina implies that the anonymous Tanna also accepts R. Jehudah’s opinion about fish sauce and requires 1/960 only for the fish itself. This also is the position of the Yerushalmi which distinguishes between the impure fish, discussed in the preceding paragraph, and fish sauce.</i>. Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Simeon bar Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he appears without a title. He was a student of Rav in Babylonia who immigrated into Galilee.</i>, a quarter [<i>log</i>] of fish sauce in two <i>seah</i> of fish and fish sauce. A case came before Rebbi Joshia and he instructed accordingly<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Practice is decided following R. Jehudah in a lenient way. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 99b" href="/Chullin.99b">Babli (<i>Ḥulin</i> 99b</a>) accepts the ruling of R. Jehudah in the interpretation of R. Abbahu which counts only the amount of sauce.</i>.
We have stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:1" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.1">Tosephta 9:1</a>, in the name of R. Yose ben R. Jehudah.</i>: “When has this been said? If he scoops out piece by piece and puts them before himself, he has to proceed in the way indicated<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">If he scooped out from the barrel to prepare the fish as food and found 10 <i>zin</i> of impure fish, all is forbidden. But if he scooped out and threw the impure away, so that at no time there are 10 <i>zin</i> of impure fish before him, the fish fluid itself is bland and does not forbid anything even if the total sum of impure fish would have been greater than 10 <i>zin</i>. (Explanation of R. S. Lieberman.)</i>. But if he scoops out piece by piece and throws them away, even more than was indicated is permitted.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The same paragraph appears in <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:10 (fol. 42a). In that text, instead of גבונתא one reads זבונתא “merchandise”. Since both texts speak about food that possibly is forbidden, the reading here seems preferable (but גבונתא possibly does not mean “cheese”.) In <i>Avodah Zarah</i>, the paragraph follows a lenient ruling of Rebbi referring to a shipload of wine amphoras. In both cases, it is not clear whether the expression “by some pretext” refers to the previous very lenient statement (and should be translated “as a subterfuge”) or to the following. Therefore, it is left to the reader to decide where or whether to split both paragraphs.</i> By some pretext, cheese was mixed up<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The percentages of forbidden cheese could no longer be ascertained for each individual piece.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked Rebbi Abba bar Zavda. He said to them, the property of the public cannot be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">This argument usually is used for public property, such as public baths dedicated to a pagan deity; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:8:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.8.8"><i>Ševi‘it</i> Chapter 8, Note 132</a>. Here it is extended to mixed cargo on a common carrier. This is a subterfuge.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi said, Rebbi Isaac asked: Is not Caius Lycus written on these documents, and you say, by a pretext<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:2-11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.2-11"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:1</a> (fol. 43b) that a divorce document is valid even if its witnesses sign with Roman (Caius) or Greek (Λύκος “wolf”) names. It is stated there that this refers only to divorce documents executed outside the Land since the Jews of the diaspora have Gentile names and, therefore, there is a presumption that the document was signed by Jews. Since without the document the divorced wife could never remarry, it is the duty of the rabbinic court of her home town to find ways to validate a divorce document for which no replacement could be demanded from overseas. This is a situation in which the rabbis would be forced to find pretexts to declare the document valid. However, the statement is an absolute one, valid also for cases in which there is no urgency.</i>?
“Rebbi Ẓadoq testified that the fluid exuding from impure locusts is pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Locust fluid is not a fluid that can prepare food for impurity; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 141</a>.</i>” In what respect is it pure? It is pure not to prepare. But to make impure<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This means, forbidden as food. (Commentary of R. Abraham ben David to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 7:2" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.7.2"><i>Idiut</i> 7:2</a>.)</i>, the most minute amount makes impure.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All those which are pickled one with the other are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">They do not impart taste.</i> except with bulb plants<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.9.3.1">Chapter 9, Note 89</a>.</i>. Profane bulb plant with heave bulb plant, profane vegetable with heave bulb plant, are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">For laymen.</i>. But profane bulb plant with heave vegetable is permitted.<br>Rebbi Yose says, all that is cooked in water with white beets is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">For laymen if the beets were heave.</i> because it imparts taste. Rebbi Simeon says<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 11. One assumes that the cabbage from the unirrigated field is heave. If it is cooked together with the more expensive and better cabbage from an irrigated garden plot, the result is forbidden to laymen even though the admixture of standard cabbage can only reduce the quality of the garden cabbage.</i>, irrigated cabbage [preserved] with unirrigated cabbage is forbidden because it absorbs. Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of all mss. of the Mishnah. For some reason, the Venice Babli and Mishnah have “R. Jehudah”.</i> says, all those which are cooked together are permitted except with meat<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Any meat cooked with vegetables will improve in taste. Therefore, meat cooked with any heave vegetables will become forbidden for laymen.</i>.<br>Rebbi Yose says, all that is cooked in water with white beets is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">For laymen if the beets were heave.</i> because it imparts taste. Rebbi Simeon says<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 11. One assumes that the cabbage from the unirrigated field is heave. If it is cooked together with the more expensive and better cabbage from an irrigated garden plot, the result is forbidden to laymen even though the admixture of standard cabbage can only reduce the quality of the garden cabbage.</i>, irrigated cabbage [preserved] with unirrigated cabbage is forbidden because it absorbs. Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of all mss. of the Mishnah. For some reason, the Venice Babli and Mishnah have “R. Jehudah”.</i> says, all those which are cooked together are permitted except with meat<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Any meat cooked with vegetables will improve in taste. Therefore, meat cooked with any heave vegetables will become forbidden for laymen.</i>.<br>If an egg was treated with forbidden spices, even its yolk is forbidden because it absorbs. Pickling and cooking fluid of heave is forbidden to laymen.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, one does not speak of pickled [food] but of that cooked in water<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 11 records the dissent of Tannaїm from Mishnah 10, the two Mishnaiot must speak about the same procedures.</i>. Pickled is like being hot<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Joḥanan is reported to hold that being preserved in salt is like being cooked over a flame. There, he explains that being pickled is not like being cooked in water (implicitly stated here) but Samuel holds that pickling and cooking in water are the same as far as dietary laws are concerned.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥanina Tortayya in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: Bulb vegetables with bulb vegetables, a kind with itself<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Heave and profane cooked together.</i>, Rebbi Aqiba permits but the Sages forbid. Rebbi Assi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Sages agree with Rebbi Aqiba that meat with meat is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Joḥanan’s stand that Mishnah 10 also speaks of cooking different foods together in water, he asserts that cooking meat forbidden to laymen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.6.1">Note 102</a>) with other meat does not extend the prohibition.</i>. Rebbi Zeїra said to Rebbi Assi: If that had not been said, we would not know that it is so, but maybe it was said as follows: Rebbi Aqiba agrees with the Sages that meat with meat is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">He denies that R. Joḥanan holds that pickling and cooking have the same rules. Since Mishnah 10 only states “all those which are pickled together”, it follows that the same is not true for “all that are cooked together; in particular, not for meat.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Aqiba agrees with the Sages that meat with meat is forbidden. Rebbi Ḥinena understood it from the next Mishnah: “Rebbi Aqiba says, all those which are cooked together are permitted except with meat.” Therefore, “meat with meat is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:4" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.4">Tosephta 9:4</a>.</i>.” Maybe it was formulated as: The Sages agree with Rebbi Aqiba that meat with meat is forbidden. Rebbi Ḥinena: Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, the Sages agree with Rebbi Aqiba that meat with meat is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Liver is so full of blood that only blood oozes out and nothing is absorbed. But the blood will make anything cooked with it forbidden.</i>.
Rebbi Yose said, we were of the opinion that the unirrigated [cabbage] absorbs<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:4" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.4">Tosephta 9:4</a>.</i>. Since Rav Huna said, take away its growing parts and get moisture; this shows that the irrigated absorbs.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: What is the rule if he cooked it in milk<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The milk is certainly forbidden because it is mixed with blood. But if R. Joḥanan ben Nuri is correct, the liver did not absorb any milk; when taken out of the milk and washed it might be permitted even though the act of cooking itself is forbidden by biblical law. This is one of the impossible questions of R. Jeremiah, cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3:10, Note 156, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 23b" href="/Bava_Batra.23b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 23b</a>. In this case, it proves that the rule of R. Joḥanan ben Nuri leads to an impossible consequence; it cannot possibly be true.</i>? Rebbi Zeїra never in his life ate liver<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was a posthumous child whose mother died in childbirth, he never could see how his mother handled liver. Since he was a Babylonian, he would have seen that in Babylonia liver was broiled to stop it from oozing blood (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 111a" href="/Chullin.111a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 111a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Abba cooked it in water and ate it; some say Rebbi Abba salted and ate it.
“Yolk is forbidden because it absorbs.” Bar Qappara stated: Yolk is forbidden and certainly egg white.
Isaac Rabba<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this is a misattribution since R. Isaac Rabba was a student of Rebbi, cf. Z. Frankel, מבוא הירושלמי, p. 105b–106a. It seems that the tradent is R. Isaac (Napaḥa), of the students of R. Joḥanan, or that the source of the statement was Rebbi, not R. Simeon ben Laqish. The text as it stands is confirmed by the quote of this and the following two paragraphs in <i>Or Zarua‘</i> I #452. The Rome ms. has Jacob Rabba; possibly the source had only י״ר as metathesis fromר״י .<br>An equivalent statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:5" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.5">Tosephta 9:5</a>; that Tosephta clearly was unknown to the compilers of the Yerushalmi.</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: If eggs were cooked in water and a chick was found in one of them, it is [judged] by the taste it gives<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If the taste of the cooked chick is noticeable in the food it was cooked with, all is forbidden since the chick in the egg was not ritually slaughtered. The definition of “giving taste” is discussed in the next paragraph. An equivalent statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:5" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.5">Tosephta 9:5</a>. The Yerushalmi does not consider the possibility of having the food tasted by a Gentile; its definition of “giving taste” is purely numerical and independent of any actual taste. Therefore, the question whether a chick inside an undamaged shell can impart any taste is irrelevant.</i>.
Rebbi Zeїra, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan; Ada bar Gershon<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">He is mentioned only here. In the parallel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 98a" href="/Chullin.98a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 98a</a>, he appears as R. Idi bar Gershom; in Or <i>Zarua‘</i> as R. Ada bar Gershom. [The reading in the printed Babli, R. Idi bar Idi bar Gershom, is not confirmed by any ms.]</i>, Rebbi Birai<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">He is otherwise unknown. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 98a" href="/Chullin.98a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 98a</a>, he appears as R. Biriam.</i>, Rebbi Levi bar Plautus<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of the second generation (רִבִּי לֵוִי בַּר פְּרֹטָא); almost all of his sayings are aggadic and quoted in the old Galilean Midrashim. His father’s name is either <i>Latin Plautus</i> or Greek πρῶτος “first”, used as name of a first-born son. For other examples of names in Greek corresponding to Jewish but not Greek usage, cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Jewish Family Names</i> <i>and Their Origins</i>, Hoboken NJ 1992; <i>Etymologisches Lexikon der jüdischen Familiennamen</i>, München 1996.</i>, an elder<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 98a" href="/Chullin.98a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 98a</a>, “an elder by the name of R. Jacob.”</i> in the name of Rebbi: 61 are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If one part of the total is forbidden and 60 are permitted, for a total of 61, then everything is forbidden. But if more than 60 are permitted, for a total of approximately 62, then the mixture is permitted. This is practice accepted by the Babli.</i>, 62 are permitted. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Our colleague Ada<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">He is the Ada bar Gershon quoted earlier.</i> testified before us: 60<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">This dissenting testimony is the Babylonian version. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 98a" href="/Chullin.98a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 98a</a>. The food is permitted if not more than one part in 60 is forbidden.</i>. He said, if somebody brings before me more than 60, I shall declare it kosher. They said to Simeon bar Abba, you say this, but the others say otherwise! He said to them, I [said] what I heard, they what they heard. Simeon bar Abba said before Rebbi Ḥanina in the name of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: Up to thirty<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In Or <i>Zarua‘</i>: 60. It is impossible to determine the correct version; R. Ḥanina thinks either that 30 is much too small or that 60 is much too large.</i>. He said, an exaggeration. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: A case came before Rabban Gamliel ben Rabbi. He said, my father<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Or Zarua‘</i>:אבא . This reading has been translated.</i> did not agree to 47, I agree to 45. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: A case came before Rebbi; he asked: Are there 50<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">He would permit a mixture of 50 parts permitted and one part forbidden food.</i>?
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Vegetables, shells, and water are counted<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">To establish that there is 45, 47, or 50 times more permitted food than the forbidden chick.</i>. Rebbi Zeїra said, even if the inside is forbidden. Rav Huna said, the shells of the forbidden help the permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Even the shell of the egg in which the chick was found is counted as permitted.</i>. Rebbi Zeїra: That is, if he cooked them in their shells, cooked with cooked. But if he cooked shelled eggs with unshelled in water, or unshelled with shelled, cooked with uncooked<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If uncooked eggs are cooked with shelled, it is clear that the shelled had been cooked first, otherwise the shelled eggs would not preserve their shape. Therefore, the meaning of the sentence is: uncooked unshelled eggs with cooked shelled.</i>, that needs a different measure<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">A minimum of 60 times, not 45, 47 or 50.</i>.
Unimpregnated eggs are permitted: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 21:10" href="/Job.21.10"><i>Job</i> 21:10</a>) “His steer made pregnant, he will not miss impregnating.<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The verse is quoted to show that the verb געל used here does not mean “to loathe” but “to miss impregnation.”</i>” Eggs that started developing<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian spelling, this would be שהרקימו. The paragraph has a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:5" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.5">Tosephta 9:5</a>, but this one sentence is missing there.</i> are forbidden. Twisted ones, he who likes it may eat them<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Eggs in which the yolk does not form a ball and which will not develop even if the hen sits on them a long time. These may be eaten by people who are not repelled by them.</i>. If one found blood in them<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Boiled eggs.</i>, one shaves off the place of the blood.
Rebbi Zeїra went to visit Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">A sick-bed visit.</i>, the son of Rebbi Isaac Aṭoshiyya. He found him sitting. He said, they taught only for yolk, but in the egg white<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The language here is a confluence of two texts. לובן means the white, חלבון the egg white. At the start of the Halakhah, egg white was called “egg water” (at least of uncooked eggs.) לובן חלבון is a double expression; in לובן חלמון the first word should be deleted. In the quote in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Chullin 64b" href="/Tosafot_on_Chullin.64b">Tosaphot (<i>Ḥulin</i> 64b</a>, והוא), לובן is missing altogether but this cannot be used as a witness to the text.</i> it is permitted. He thought that he had heard it from his ancestor. Rebbi Abbahu came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Both egg white and yolk are forbidden. Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul stated: Yolk is forbidden, egg white permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">A similar <i>baraita</i> is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 64b" href="/Chullin.64b">Babli, <i>Ḥulin</i> 64b</a>, in the name of R. Dositheos ben Patricius.</i>. Rebbi Zeїra said, he who says yolk is forbidden if it is found at the male part of the yolk; this applies even to yolk, but only if it is found on its male part since from there the development of the chick starts<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">This text is badly transmitted; in its present form it is self-contradictory. In Tosaphot (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. 135), the text reads: “Rebbi Zeїra said, what was said about yolk being forbidden [with a blood spot], if it is found at the male part of the yolk.” The second part seems to be another version of the same tradition. The “male part” of the yolk is the place where the yolk meets the thicker part of the egg white strung from a vertex to the yolk.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Miqwa’ot</i> 7:2.</i>, we have stated: “The following disable and do not fill<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>miqweh</i>, or ritual bath, must contain at leat 40 <i>seah</i> of natural water, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.8.4">Chapter 4, Note 112</a>. Fluids other than water have no influence on the <i>miqweh</i> but water either drawn in a vessel or used for other purposes in the volume of at least three log makes the <i>miqweh</i> unusable. If there were 40 <i>seah</i> minus 2 <i>log</i> in the <i>miqweh</i>, adding 2 <i>log</i> of water as described in the Mishnah will not produce 40 <i>seah</i> of usable water, it does not “fill”, and the <i>miqweh</i> is still deficient.</i>: Water, whether pure or impure<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">In a vessel.</i>, pickling water, cooking water, afterwine<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Obtained by pouring water over husks of grapes. This is water, not wine.</i> before it became vinegar.” Here<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The only heave from fluids is from wine and olive oil. If the water described in the Mishnah is forbidden for laymen, then it must have the status of solid food.</i>, you treat it as food, there as fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">If the fluids considered food for heave were also food in regard of the laws of the <i>miqweh</i>, they would neither disable nor fill.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, here when it is thick, there when it is thin. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, you may even say in both cases thick or thin. Taste counts for heave<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The taste particles in the fluid make it forbidden for laymen, regardless of its status as food.</i> but not for a <i>miqweh</i>.

Chapter 11



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not put a fig cake or figs<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Of heave. The entire Mishnah deals only with heave.</i> into <i>muries</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Fish sauce, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.16"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 1, Note 156</a>.</i> because he would destroy them<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Fig sap is used as a spice; the figs are then discarded. It is forbidden to use heave for anything but food. Spices which are consumed in their entirety are permitted.</i> but one may add wine to <i>muries</i>. One does not use olive oil as base for perfume<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This is no longer food.</i> but one may use wine as base for ὀινόμελι<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">“Wine-honey”, a mixture of wine, honey, and spices. The Greek has been hebraized, showing the relationship between יין (Arabic וַין “black raisin”) and Ϝοιν-.</i>. One may not cook heave wine because he diminishes it; Rebbi Jehudah permits it because he improves it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not put fig cake or figs into <i>muries</i>”, etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:7" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.7">Tosephta 9:7</a> where, however, the last sentence is completely different.</i>: “One puts fig cake or figs into <i>muries</i> in the way one uses spices on condition he not squeeze them to extract their juice<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A rabbinic prohibition, that one should not treat heave figs as he would profane figs and throw them away after squeezing out the juice.</i>. But spices are permitted, for that is what they are used for. A bundle of spices put into one pot and then transferred to another, if their taste is lost they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">If heave spices are used repeatedly, they reach a stage where they will no longer act as spices and are permitted to lay people.</i>, otherwise forbidden.”
What is the difference between this and dill? Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Uqeẓin</i> 3:4.</i>: “Dill which gave its taste [to a dish] in a pot no longer is under the rules of heave and does not accept the impurity of food.” They gave to you from your own. From biblical law it should not accept the impurity of foodstuffs<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of the Babli, dill was never used for cooking but only as raw addition to a dessert. Solid food used only raw is not subject to impurity. Since the impurity of dill is rabbinical, it cannot be considered for inferences about other substances.</i>; they said that it should accept the impurity of foodstuffs and they said that after it transferred its taste it is disregarded.
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The sentence in the Mishnah which permits heave wine in fish sauce. This and the following paragraphs are also in <i>Avodah Zarah</i>2:6 (fol. 41c).</i> is Rebbi’s, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The last sentence of this <i>baraita</i> is similar to a statement of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:6" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.6">Tosephta 9:6</a>.</i>: “Wine in <i>muries</i>, Rebbi permits but Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, wine is not usually added to <i>muries</i> since it will ruin the taste.</i>. Therefore, if somebody transgressed and put it in, Rebbi forbids it to laymen but Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon permits to laymen.”
Rebbi Mana ben Rebbi Tanḥum asked: Following him who permits to laymen, why is <i>muries</i> of Gentiles forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:5, everybody agrees that Gentile <i>muries</i> is forbidden. R. Meїr forbids usufruct from Gentile <i>muries</i>, the Sages forbid <i>muries</i> only as food.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, it is forbidden as being cooked by Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden in Mishnah <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:9.</i>. Rebbi Yose objected: Did we not state: “From a professional it is permitted, not from a professional it is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 4:13" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).4.13">Tosephta 4:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 34b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.34b">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 34b</a>, state only: “One may buy <i>muries</i> only from a professional.” Rashi explains that this refers to a Gentile professional producer. The Babli specifies that this holds only for good quality <i>muries</i> made with fish fat, which would be spoiled by the addition of wine.</i>” From a professional it is permitted even if it is cooked; similarly, from a non-professional<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile; he might use wine to clarify the <i>muries</i> as explained in the parallel in <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:6. Tosephta (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>.) and Babli (39b) note that at a non-professional Jew’s one may eat <i>muries</i> without asking.</i> it is forbidden even if not cooked. Rebbi Joḥanan bar Maria said, even according to him who says from a professional it is permitted, only if he knows<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He knows for sure that the seller is a professional in the <i>muries</i> trade.</i>. This implies that usufruct of heave is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If the heave wine cannot be tasted, it is permitted to laymen.</i>, usufruct of paganism is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">If libation wine cannot be tasted, or even if it spoils the taste, it still is forbidden to all Jews.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Joḥanan, one of them said because he diminishes its volume, the other says because he reduces the number of drinkers<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph discusses the statement of the Sages who in the Mishnah prohibit cooking heave wine because “it diminishes.” The paragraph was explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.5">Chapter 2, Halakhah 5, Notes 118–119</a>.</i>. We did not know who said what. Since Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Jehudah inverted his reasoning but Rebbi Eleazar said, Rebbi Jehudah did not invert his reasoning, there for the Cohen, here for the owners, it follows that Rebbi Joḥanan said because he reduces the number of drinkers.
Jehudah ben Keni<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This name probably is an error of transmission. The sentence is not in the parallel in <i>Avodah Zarah</i>, nor in Chapter 2.</i>, Rebbi Ammi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Because he reduces the number of drinkers.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Date honey, apple wine, vinegar from winter grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Late grapes that never became sweet enough to be eaten or used to make wine.</i>, and any other fruit juices from heave, Rebbi Eliezer subjects to [repayment of] value and fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If consumed by a non-Cohen in error, cf. Mishnah 6:1.</i>, but Rebbi Joshua frees [from payment]. Rebbi Eliezer declares them impure as drinks<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">“Impure” means (a) potentially impure if exposed to impurity and (b) able to prepare solid food for impurity; “pure” means not subject to impurity; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 141</a>. The seven fluids subject to impurity are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 6:4" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.6.4">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 6:4</a>: Dew, water (including human body fluids), wine, olive oil, blood, milk, bees’ honey. These fluids are called “drinks”, following the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:34" href="/Leviticus.11.34"><i>Lev</i>. 11:34</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joshua said, the Sages did not enumerate seven fluids as one counts raw materials for perfumes<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Which the perfumer chooses according to his own wishes.</i> but they said, seven fluids are impure, all other fluids are pure.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> They want to say that they differ about the fifth but Rebbi Joshua agrees about the principal<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the statement is formulated as tentative, if the farmer uses his own date honey from dates destined for the Cohen, he does not pay at all. But if a third person uses it, he has to pay its value as he would for any robbery (Maimonides and R. Abraham ben David, <i>Terumot</i> 11:2).</i>. They differ after the fact<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">About the amount the transgressing layman has to pay.</i>, but before the fact Rebbi Joshua agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">That date honey made from heave dates is forbidden to laymen.</i>. But we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:8" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.8">Tosephta 9:8</a>; only the statement of R. Eliezer and that of R. Nathan in the next paragraph.</i>: “Rebbi Eliezer subjects date honey to tithes, but Rebbi Joshua frees it.” Here, when they obtained it after it became <i>ṭevel</i>, there before it became <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If the date honey was taken before the dates themselves became subject to heave and tithes as explained in the first chapter of <i>Ma‘serot</i>, R. Joshua holds that date honey itself cannot become subject to heave and tithes. But if the honey exuded from the dates after the dates were given as heave, R. Joshua agrees that it remains heave and is forbidden to laymen. The answer is thoroughly impractical since Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 1:2 states that dates become subject to tithes as soon as they contain moisture; it is difficult to see how honey could exude from dates harvested dry even if they were left to ripen in storage.</i>.
Rebbi Eliezer agrees that if he tithed the dates, their honey is permitted even in Spain<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">If the dates were tithed when dry and the honey was produced later from exported dates, the honey remains permitted. {Since the text is not punctuated, it would be possible to read: If he tithed the dates even in Spain, their honey would be permitted. However, this interpretation is impossible since fruits of the Land cannot be tithed in Spain, not even in Apamea in Syria.}</i>. This implies that he tithes dates including honey proportional to the dates. “Rebbi Eliezer subjects date honey to tithes.” We have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:8" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.8">Tosephta 9:8</a>; only the statement of R. Eliezer and that of R. Nathan in the next paragraph.</i>: “Rebbi Nathan says, not that Rebbi Eliezer subjects it to tithes but Rebbi Eliezer says he should not eat from the tithes until he also tithed the honey.” This implies that he tithes dates including honey proportional<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In volume.</i> to honey and dates.
“Rebbi Eliezer declares it<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:8" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.8">Tosephta (9:8</a>) makes it clear that only date honey is being discussed.</i> impure as drink.” It was stated: “Rebbi Nathan said, Rebbi Eliezer does not declare it impure as drink; what did they disagree about? If some drink<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “Water”.</i> fell into it, then Rebbi Eliezer declares it impure as drink but the Sages say, it follows the majority<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">It is not a “drink” as long as there is more than 50% date honey.</i>.”
In the opinion of Rebbi Nathan, Rebbi Meїr, Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob, and Rebbi Eliezer, all three said the same. Rebbi Meїr, for he says<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this refers to the anonymous Mishnah <i>Miqwa’ot</i> 7:2, presumed to be R. Meїr’s teaching, which states that if a <i>miqweh</i> is short one half <i>seah</i> of the required 40 <i>seah</i> of water (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 4:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.4.8.4">Chapter 4, Note 112</a>, Chapter 19, Note 139), if one half of a <i>seah</i> of fruit juice fell in, the status of the <i>miqweh</i> is not changed. If the character of fruit juice could be disregarded (being less than 1 in 60 of the total amount), then the watered down fruit juice could make up the difference.</i>, “fruit juice never is disregarded.” Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 6:3" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.6.3">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 6:3</a>. Usually, fish sauce is impure since fish, coming out of the water, is always prepared for impurity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 141</a>.) If it is known that the fish sauce was extracted from pure fish by people strictly keeping the laws of purity, then the fish sauce is pure and, since it is not one of the “seven fluids” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.11.2.1">Note 26</a>), cannot become impure. But if any amount of water is added, it is prepared for impurity.</i>: “Pure fish sauce into which fell the smallest amount of water is impure.” And Rebbi Eliezer here.
The reason of Rebbi Eliezer (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:34" href="/Leviticus.11.34"><i>Lev</i>. 11:34</a>): “Any drink.” What is the reason of the rabbis? “That can be drunk.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:34" href="/Leviticus.11.34"><i>Lev</i>. 11:34</a>: “Any food that can be eaten, if water comes upon it it may become impure, and any drink that can be drunk can become impure in any vessel.” For the notion of preparation for impurity based on this and the nearby verses, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.11.2.1">Note 26</a>.<br>The argument ascribed here to the rabbis is difficult to understand and is not found in any of the parallel sources; it is not followed up and is superseded later by the documented opinion of the rabbis. The emphasis is only on the arguments of R. Eliezer, to show that R. Eliezer’s position in the Mishnah is not practice, even if R. Eliezer’s position in the Tosephta would be accepted because he is supported by R. Meїr and R. Eliezer ben Jacob. {Maimonides, <i>Hilkhot Ṭum’at Okhlin</i> 2:14, follows R. Eliezer; the interpretation here follows R. Abraham ben David <i>ad loc</i>.}<br>In <i>Sifra Šemini Parašah</i> 8(1), fruit juices are excluded since the verse speaks only of “water”. It is concluded that only drinks described by a single noun can be meant. Blood is included [<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. 8(4)] since it is compared to water in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 110:7" href="/Psalms.110.7"><i>Ps</i>. 110:7</a>. Fruit juices are excluded since they are called “fruit water”.</i>” Does Rebbi Eliezer hold with Rebbi Ismael, that for a particular statement after a general one, everything is included<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">“A general statement followed by a particular one” is the heading of one of the 13 hermeneutical rules of R. Ismael, listed in the Introduction to <i>Sifra</i>. The detailed description of the rule, that a general statement followed by a particular one applies only to that particular one, is no longer connected with the name of R. Ismael; it represents the opinion of the Sages. For an application, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.1.6"><i>Kilaim</i> Chapter 8, Note 20</a>.<br>It is asserted here and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:2:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.2.2-5"><i>Nazir</i> 6:2</a> that R. Ismael disagrees with the interpretation of the Sages and holds that the particular statement is only an illustration. The concurrence of both mss. here and the one available ms. in <i>Nazir</i> shows that the statement cannot be amended away and that in the Introduction to <i>Sifra</i>, only paragraphs 1 and 2 are attributable to R. Ismael.<br>In our case, “any drink” is taken as a general statement, “that can be drunk” as a particular one.</i>? He assumes more than Rebbi Ismael, in that even if there is a second general statement, all is included<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This is a theoretical statement, not of relevance here, since the particular is not followed by a second general statement. However, it opens a window for a possible study of the development of the hermeneutical rules.</i>. Rebbi Paregoros from Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">He is mentioned only here. Greek παρήγορος “comforting”, a sobriquet for Menaḥem; cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Jewish Family Names and their Origins</i> (Hoboken, NJ, 1992; <i>Etymologisches Lexikon der jüdischen Familiennamen</i>, München 1996), entry Perigord.</i>: Rebbi Eliezer ben Rebbi Ismael said: So did Rebbi Eliezer answer the Sages: Since you interpret (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:34" href="/Leviticus.11.34"><i>Lev</i>. 11:34</a>): “That can be eaten,” to exclude stinking food<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Šemini Pereq</i> 9(1).
